HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Going Bananas

BYLINE: By LESLIE H. GELB

 BODY:
The U.S. of A.: winner of the cold war, model to the world, heir to global leadership and greatness, incipient banana republic. Banana republic?
Banana-republichood is attained when political, business and community leaders do things that are plainly stupid and self-destructive, and when they cannot pull themselves together to fix obvious wrongs and advance the plain national interest. 
 Just read the newspapers in the last few days and contemplate the creeping decrepitude.
Friends and family buried Yoshihiro Hattori on Monday in Japan. He was a 16-year-old exchange student in Baton Rouge, La. Looking for a party, he knocked on the wrong door, was told to "freeze," didn't understand the word, and was shot dead by a homeowner with a .44 magnum pistol.
The Japanese were astonished that such a thing could happen. In their country, private handguns are prohibited. In almost all Western democracies, handguns cannot be purchased without a waiting period, a license and a good reason. Only in America can a tiny fraction of the population (our Frankenstein monster, the National Rifle Association) "convince" political "leaders" that our freedoms depend on easy access to handguns and semiautomatic weapons, and that the 12,000 people killed with handguns in 1990 was "the American way."
Chancellor Helmut Kohl told his fellow Germans this week that he would raise taxes to cut the Government's surging budget deficit. Germans are quite conservative and already more heavily taxed than Americans. But rather than see his newly unified nation go down the economic drain, Mr. Kohl finally stood up, took his political wormwood and asked his countrymen for sacrifice.
The U.S. is heading toward a $5 trillion national debt, and few American politicians dare to suggest paying bills and sacrificing to save the nation. The only politician to have done so and survived is not exactly a politician and has his own private fortune.
That daring fellow is Ross Perot -- whose virtues as an economic truth-teller seem somewhat clouded by his rather erratic behavior. Mr. Perot can be very scary. No one that strange, unpredictable and risky has come near national power in any other industrial democracy since before World War II. As Yogi Berra said when told Dublin had elected its first Jewish Mayor, "Only in America."
Almost only in America could one imagine tolerating a $400 billion annual deficit and a $4 trillion accumulated national debt. Only here, perhaps, can politicians talk about "growing out" of the deficit. If any banana republic tried to sell us that painless baloney, we'd cut off its supply of international loans.
Or take the case of Robert Stempel, whose board of directors just forced him out as boss of General Motors. Over the last two years in its North American operations alone, Mr. Stempel led his company to a $12 billion loss. In the last dozen years, he and his predecessors have presided over the demolition of one of America's greatest corporations.
Almost all the while, the executives of G.M. were awarding themselves fat yearly raises and bonuses -- and laying off workers. Boards of directors in almost any public corporation abroad would have bought gold Toyotas for such executives and sent them off long ago to the year-round golf course.
Or ponder President Bush's charge that Ross Perot is "nutty" for proposing a 50-cent increase in gas taxes. (Bill Clinton also opposes the idea, but secretly likes it, maybe.) Mr. Bush must think it's cosmically nutty, then, for gas taxes to be eight times ours in West Europe and four times ours in Japan.
But higher carbon taxes mean greater energy efficiency (ours is about half theirs), lower growth in air pollution, smaller oil import bills and, above all, greater security. Right now, the United States buys more than half its oil from the Persian Gulf. This means every time some gulf messiah has a yen for Saudi Arabia, we have to go to war. This could happen only in an America fast nurturing the mentality of banana-republichood.
For a decade, Japan and Asia have been trading our pants off. This year, a "Canadian" baseball team bested us for the first time in the World Series. If we don't shape up, in 10 years Mexico will be buying Rockefeller Center.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

LENGTH: 439 words

HEADLINE: Perot Plan Prescribes an Economic Disaster;
Deficit Delusion

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "Perot Medicine for Clinton" (Op-Ed, Oct. 7), Robert Litan is the latest Brookings Institution scholar to recommend some kind of two-part economic plan to jump-start the economy: a temporary stimulus by way of deficit spending on infrastructure and investment tax credits for the first two to three years of a Clinton administration, to be followed by Ross Perot's plan for higher taxes and further budget cuts to eliminate the deficit. 
 Mr. Litan and the other jump-starters do not realize that those who cannot remember the past are condemned to repeat it.
In the Great Depression, some economists urged a similar two-part program that was labeled pump priming, that is, a temporary increase in Government spending financed by deficits to get the economy moving. After the economy improved, traditional budget balancing measures could be reinstated.
Thus, during the early years of Franklin D. Roosevelt's Administration, massive deficit spending on infrastructure (as in the Work Projects Administration) and other actions were undertaken.
By 1936 the economy was well on its way to recovery, while economic pundits were predicting catastrophe unless the deficit was eliminated. Roosevelt, running for re-election, promised to end pump priming and balance the budget. By drastically reducing Government expenditures, Roosevelt submitted a budget for 1937 that cut the deficit almost in half. In the first nine months of 1937 gross national product collapsed to approximately the same level it had fallen to in 1933.
Roosevelt quickly reversed course and throughout the rest of his Administration the Government permanently spent more than the revenues it took in. Prosperity was firmly reinstituted in the United States.
This experience should warn us that today's jump-start approach is a snare and a delusion that turns attention from the long-run and permanent need to develop Government policies necessary for the success of an entrepreneurial economy. A jump-start will merely turn over the motor. Once the ignition is shut off, the motor will die again -- unless the battery has been permanently charged, so that it can carry the load of keeping the economy going -- and in a growing economy, accelerating.
As long as our economy has idle workers who want to work and contribute to the growth of America, it is the Government's responsibility to assure them meaningful productive jobs. The best defense against welfare dependency is a full employment economy.
 
PAUL DAVIDSON
Knoxville, Tenn., Oct. 7, 1992
 
The writer holds a University of Tennessee chair in political economy.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

LENGTH: 192 words

HEADLINE: Perot Plan Prescribes an Economic Disaster;
Roads to the Future

 BODY:
To the Editor:
It is a grave mistake to view Federal spending on public works as merely a means of generating temporary work in times of economic distress. Rebuilding the nation's infrastructure should not simply be motivated by "activist risk-taking," as one economist characterizes it in "Old Idea Gains New Respect: Spending Way Out of Slump" (front page, Oct. 8). 
 American economic supremacy in the post-World War II period was directly linked to the superiority of our transportation and communication systems, and the decline of that infrastructure presaged the decline of our economic position in the world. The Federal Government should pursue programs that will restore our infrastructure while generating productive and rewarding work. The objective is not, however, simply to increase public works employment. It is to rebuild the foundation on which our future economic security rests.
In the long run, policies that rebuild our airports, highways and waterways will enable us to be a more productive country -- and that is surely the best way to reduce the deficit.
 
ALBERT R. GAMPER JR.
Livingston, N.J., Oct. 13, 1992

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
McDonnell To Contract Work Out

BYLINE: By ANDREA ADELSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Oct. 28

 BODY:
As part of a reorganization announced in August, the McDonnell Douglas Corporation may start trimming costs by contracting out some work that has been performed in house, company spokesmen said today, confirming a published report.
The cost-cutting strategy, which can save millions of dollars but costs workers their jobs, is just taking hold among aerospace companies, which are having to become more competitive as orders for airliners, fighter planes and weapon systems have declined. 
 Among the first companies to take this step was the General Dynamics Corporation, which last year sold its data processing unit for $200 million to the Computer Sciences Corporation. The business is worth $3 billion over 10 years to Computer Sciences and is that company's first important commercial contract, said Larry DeJarnett, a vice president with A. T. Kearney, a management consulting firm.
 
Trend in Aerospace Industry
 "It's a growing trend in the aerospace business," Mr. DeJarnett said, but one heretofore shunned by the industry, primarily because of worries about secrecy.
"Economic pressure to survive finally forces you to do it," he said.
The move to "outsourcing," in industry argot, by McDonnell Douglas could cost 2,500 to 5,500 jobs and could begin after Nov. 6, The St. Louis Post-Dispatch reported today. The paper said McDonnell was reviewing the possibility of contracting out research and testing, data processing and mail services. While McDonnell would not verify any of the numbers specified in the St. Louis article, it acknowledged that the idea was being considered.
As part of its previously announced restructuring plan, the nation's largest military contractor put its data processing group, which employs 1,400 people, up for sale.
A team of nine executives has just begun looking at whether laboratory jobs can also be hired out, said another company spokesman. The company is the Pentagon's No. 2 research contractor, having received $300 million in contracts this year, and it employs 2,000 lab workers in various sites. "We're taking a look," a spokesman said. "No decisions have been made."
In previous downturns, research and engineering staffs at aerospace concerns had some measure of job protection in working on the next generation of weapon systems, said Paul Nesbit, an analyst at Prudential Securities. These staffs are being let go today, he said. "You don't invest in the future if there isn't a future."
Martin J. Bollinger, a vice president in the aerospace group of the management consultant Booz, Allen & Hamilton, said: "Until two years ago, this industry had no incentive to cut costs. Now if they don't, they'll lose market share, because their competitors will."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Republicans -- Candidate Aim at Crucial States, and Each Other;
Bush, Buoyed by Polls, Scrambles to Rebuild Winning Coalition

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Oct. 29

 BODY:
With some new polls providing encouragement, President Bush and his strategists have begun scrambling, hoping in the five days left them to rebuild the shattered bloc of Republicans and disaffected Democrats that won the White House in 1988. That is why they came to Michigan today.
Mr. Bush returned to an old wellspring of Democratic support, suburban Macomb County, to sound alarms about high taxes and low character that he hopes will drive voters away from Bill Clinton and into his camp. 
 Hoarse and shouting over the crowd of 10,000 people, Mr. Bush stripped his message to its most pointed and strident essence.
 
'Crazy, Way Out, Far Out'
 He accused Mr. Clinton of having "given up on reducing the deficit." Arms flailing, he denounced Mr. Clinton's running mate, Senator Al Gore, as an environmental extremist who is "crazy, way out, far out, man."
He underscored his "big difference" with people who would protest the Vietnam war overseas, as Mr. Clinton did, "when your brother's in prison in Hanoi or your kids are being drafted out of a ghetto somewhere."
Twice, he dismissed his two rivals with contemptuous remarks, declaring that "my dog Millie knows more about foreign affairs than these two bozos."
And he turned one of Mr. Clinton's favorite anti-Bush buzzwords against him, calling a band of sign-waving Clinton supporters at one Bush rally "a little pathetic."
"They feel it slipping away from them," he exulted. "They know we're going to win the election." The crowd at a cavernous junior-college fieldhouse roared its approval.
Later, at an outdoor rally in Grand Rapids dominated by a huge, pink, elephant-shaped balloon, Mr. Bush punched away at Mr. Clinton's credibility again, saying, "What's catching up with him now -- what's catching up with him just as we go down to the wire -- is this pattern of deception."
Mr. Bush's aides asserted today that their attacks had shifted the focus of the political debate to the issue of character, where the White House wants it, and to the best way to revive the economy, where they feel they are on at least equal footing.
In fact, Mr. Bush has begun to leaven his assaults on Mr. Clinton with large doses of can-do optimism about the future, something glaringly absent until this month. He has seized on Tuesday's report of a healthy rise in economic output as evidence of a looming American revival, even if voters have yet to notice it.
"There was very good news for this country two days ago, and they hate it," he told 9,800 supporters in Grand Rapids today. "The only way they can win is to convince America that we're in a recession. We had growth of 2.7 percent. We have grown for six quarters. And now let's keep it going."
Later, he spoke of "a worldwide recovery," led by the United States, that will "bring jobs to more Americans."
Mr. Bush's aides assert that the combination of slashing attacks and hopeful messages is raising Mr. Bush's popularity and lowering Mr. Clinton's.
The President's campaign chairman, Robert M. Teeter, said today that he believed Mr. Clinton's lead was shriveling in Michigan. In large part, he said, that is because Mr. Bush had shone a spotlight on Mr. Clinton's pledge to raise taxes by $150 billion.
Mr. Clinton says that new burden will fall mostly on the well-off and on foreign companies. Bush strategists have responded by blanketing rallies with cardboard devices, akin to simple slide rules, that supposedly enable voters to determine how much Mr. Clinton's package of tax proposals "could" cost people in their income brackets. Invariably, the totals are in the thousands of dollars.
Mr. Clinton's economic proposals, Mr. Teeter said, "are the antithesis of what they want to hear."
How much ground Mr. Bush has gained in recent polls depends on which of the wildly varying surveys one reads.
The "tracking poll" conducted for CNN and USA Today showed Mr. Clinton preferred over President Bush by three percentage points today, 41 percent to 38 percent, with 16 percent favoring Ross Perot and 5 percent undecided or supporting another candidate. The margin of sampling error was three percentage points. In a tracking poll, new samples are interviewed each day and reports are issued on the combined results for two or more days at a time.
When the findings of the most recent major national polls are averaged, Mr. Clinton leads Mr. Bush by 43 to 36 percent, with 16 percent for Mr. Perot.
 
Poll in Michigan
 But the President was cheered this morning by a local television station survey that showed him in a statistical tie with Mr. Clinton in Michigan.
Macomb County's blue-collar conservatives are the key to Mr. Bush's failure or success here, and an indication of his national standing as well. They are the archetype of the financially struggling, anti-tax Democrats who deserted Jimmy Carter for Ronald Reagan in 1980, and stayed to vote for Mr. Bush in 1988.
With the economy lagging most of this year, conventional wisdom this year has held that these Reagan Democrats were rejecting Mr. Bush and swinging back to their party.
But Mr. Teeter, the Bush campaign chairman, said in a telephone interview today that it was not happening. He said Mr. Bush had pulled even in Macomb County, which he said "was strongly in favor of Bill Clinton" only two weeks ago.
Whether such trends are sufficiently strong or rapid to make a difference in Tuesday's election remains unclear. But Mr. Teeter, who has every incentive to appear optimistic, argued that the race was now close enough for Mr. Bush to begin consolidating his hold on the normally Republican South and West. Those are regions that Mr. Clinton has strongly contested all summer and fall.
"If we can get that base back of 190 to 210 electoral votes in the West and South," he said, "then you can go fight him for those 60 or 70 other votes you need to win an election."
Mr. Bush plans to pursue those votes next in Tennessee and Missouri, where he campaigns on Friday, and on Saturday by train in Wisconsin.
In addition to a faster pace on the campaign trail, Mr. Bush has been jamming as many television interviews as possible into his last week before the election, appearing on all the network breakfast programs. Tonight he appeared on the ABC News program "Prime Time Live" and he even consented to an interview on Saturday with a reporter for MTV, which he had been avoiding for months. The interview is to be broadcast on Sunday.
Tonight Mr. Bush seemed bent on driving home his argument that national news organization were unfair to him, saying he had been "hammered, hammered, hammered" on Iraq and other issues.
The ABC interview with Sam Donaldson grew testy at several points and Mr. Bush may have been trying to use the combative reporter as a foil for his feud with the press, much as he did with Dan Rather of CBS News in the 1988 New Hampshire primary campaign.
When Mr. Donaldson suggested that the Administration knew that Iraq's President, Saddam Hussein, was diverting agricultural loans to weapons programs, Mr. Bush snapped: "Wrong, wrong and wrong. Wrong, wrong and wrong."

LENGTH: 1286 words

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Democrats -- Candidates Aim at Crucial States, and Each Other;
Clinton Is Abandoning His Emphasis on Issues For the Attack Strategy of an Underdog

BYLINE: By GWEN IFILL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SECAUCUS, N.J. Oct. 29

 BODY:
Responding to a tightened Presidential race, Gov. Bill Clinton today essentially scrapped plans to spend the final days of his campaign concentrating on substantive issues and instead hop-scotched to three states directing his firepower at President Bush.
Even as he sought to strike a sober tone for speeches on the economy and AIDS research, Mr. Clinton cast his policy discussion in terms of what Mr. Bush lacked. And although he still led in most polls, Mr. Clinton said for the first time in weeks that he considered himself an "underdog" in the race for President. 
 "When Mr. Bush tells us we have to rejoice at our economic plight, I think we have to tell him it's time to move beyond the passivity and complacency of his Presidency and of this campaign," he said during a speech at the University of Toledo.
 
Crisscrossing Campaign Paths
 Mr. Bush and Mr. Clinton have been all but colliding with each other as they jet from one must-win state to another. Today, Mr. Clinton followed Mr. Bush by a day in Toledo, then campaigned at the same time as the President in a different part of Michigan. He spent the afternoon and evening in New Jersey.
"The American people are going to have to think a long time before they want four more years of this," Mr. Clinton said of the Bush Administration. "It will be a miserable experience for them, and I don't think they're going to go through with it."
When a supporter wished Mr. Clinton good luck in Ohio and Michigan as he boarded a plane for Newark, he responded: "If I win both of them, this election's over."
Mr. Clinton's campaign day reflected the strategic tug-of-war within the Democratic campaign. Plans called for him to stick to a loftier message about the economy and jobs, and to deliver a long-awaited speech on AIDS in Jersey City.
In that speech, in the rotunda of the Brennan Courthouse this evening, Mr. Clinton linked the need for Federal action on AIDS to steps he has advocated on other health-care issues, including breast and ovarian cancer. But he made a special plea for new attention to AIDS and urged an end to prejudice directed at those who carry the virus.
Mr. Clinton promised AIDS advocates months ago that he would speak before the campaign ended about his plans for attacking the disease, and today was the fulfillment of that vow. New Jersey is a political plum that he would certainly have visited anyway, but he said he chose to speak on AIDS here because the state leads the nation in the incidents of AIDS cases among women and ranks third in the number of children infected with the disease.
"We must all remember that for all its terror and far reach, it is still a disease," Mr. Clinton said. "It is not vengeance or punishment or just desserts. It is an illness. We have fought illnesses before, and we must fight this one now."
 
Plans for War on AIDS
 Mr. Clinton said he would increase financing for AIDS research, enforce anti-discrimination laws, appoint a Federal official to oversee the Government's response and mount a "loud, clear and consistent war on AIDS."
He also said children should be taught about how the AIDS virus is contracted "at the earliest practical time."
"I do believe the instruction of our children should be based on values that we all share," he said. "I believe children should be discouraged from having premature sexual conduct and from ever having any conduct with needles carrying drugs. But they must be told that this killer can kill them."
Mr. Clinton criticized the Justice Department's support for allowing private employers to cut off insurance coverage for employees who are infected with the virus that causes AIDS.
Campaign aides had planned for Mr. Clinton to stick to such loftier tones throughout the day, but after he left the courthouse, he returned to the attack, launching another verbal assault on Mr. Bush outside.
Apparently stung by the President's energetic recent attacks, Mr. Clinton counterpunched for the second straight day, vowing, "We're going to slam him home in the next five days."
At a college, a downtown convention center and a small-town courthouse, the Governor's remarks, both on and off the stage, reflected new turmoil in a campaign that had been sailing well ahead of Mr. Bush in public opinion polls for weeks.
Michigan, where Mr. Clinton appeared today, is a classic example of the state of flux that has marked the recent days. Democratic officials there and in Ohio said Mr. Clinton's support had dropped significantly after Mr. Perot re-entered the race.
"It's all about trust," Donald Sweitzer, a national Democratic official who is following the Michigan campaign said. "On issues, it's all moving the other way. Bush is wrong on every issue with these voters."
The Clinton campaign today began broadcasting two new 30-second commercials. One uses blurry television images of President Bush making promises four years ago, like telling people they will be "better off" under his Presidency. The ad closes with a sober announcer saying, "Well, it's four years later. How're you doing?"
Another ad, which Clinton aides said they completed at 4 A.M. today, is a direct response to a Bush commercial that was broadcast beginning Wednesday, attacking the Governor's record in Arkansas. The ad uses third-party sources to accuse the Bush commercial of being dishonest.
"Harry Truman said it, and we're going to live by it," said Paul Begala, a Clinton strategist and speechwriter. "If he's going to tell lies about us, we're going to tell the truth about him."
Mr. Begala tried to draw a contrast between Mr. Clinton's tactics and the President's. "George Bush is out there bordering on the hysterical," Mr. Begala said.
Mr. Clinton has seemed more unsettled in recent days as well. After he first dismissed the tightening race as a natural phenomenon that had been expected, his remarks today were more aggressive, reminiscent of his style when he was struggling in the bleakest days of his primary campaign in New Hampshire.
"I wish it was Tuesday," said one campaign aide. "In 1988 with Dukakis, we used to say every day at this time that we wished we had two more weeks. Now I wish this had ended two weeks ago."
Throwing his entire arsenal of negative oratory at Mr. Bush today, Mr. Clinton accused the President of "fearmongering" and "mudslinging."
"He has made up charges about my record," he said in Toledo. "The man has no core of conviction. This is not Harry Truman. Harry Truman was never called, never called, dishonest by the American press corps. He fought for average people."
"There is no standard of truth in the Bush campaign," Mr. Clinton said. "He will say anything."
Although his attacks on Ross Perot have been much more muted, Mr. Clinton has also questioned the independent's candidacy, charging that Mr. Perot has been "spending anything to get elected."
"When Mr. Bush looks at an anxious and frightened America and tells us this is as good as it gets, that says something worse about his Presidency than anything I could ever say," Mr. Clinton said.
Mr. Clinton has returned to the talk show cricuit for the closing days of the campaign. Today he taped appearances on both "Prime Time Live" on ABC and participated in a "town meeting" telecast on WWOR in New Jersey. He is also scheduled to appear on ABC'S "Good Morning America" on Friday and tape the PBS show "Talking with David Frost."
Campaign officials also said they planned to undertake a final marathon of campaigning on Monday, embarking on a 24-hour campaign day that would take Mr. Clinton through at least eight states from Pennsylvania to New Mexico before he returned to Arkansas to vote on Tuesday.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Bristol-Myers Squibb to Cut 4% of Its Global Workforce

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM

 BODY:
Acknowledging difficult times in its core drug and infant-formula businesses, the Bristol-Myers Squibb Company announced yesterday that it would eliminate some 2,200 jobs in the next five months.
The big New York-based health-care company said it would cut the jobs in its worldwide pharmaceutical and nutritional groups. Jerry Parrott, a company spokesman, said the reductions would hit "all levels, departments and operations" of the drug and nutrition units around the world in roughly the same proportions. 
 About 330 of the cuts will be in central New Jersey at the company's units in Lawrenceville, Princeton, Plainsboro and New Brunswick. But none would apply to the headquarters staff in New York.
Bristol-Myers Squibb shares rose 25 cents to $67.25 on the New York Stock Exchange.
 
Workforce to Shrink 7%
 Combined with the announced sale this week of its Drackett household products unit to S. C. Johnson & Son Inc., Bristol-Myers Squibb is shedding a total of 3,800 of the 53,500 employees on its payroll at the beginning of this year, or 7 percent. The company said the cuts would be partly absorbed by vacant positions.
The moves followed the appointment of Charles A. Heimbold Jr. as president two weeks ago, as Bristol-Myers Squibb struggled with disappointing single-digit earnings growth.
Wayne A. Davidson, president of the pharmaceutical and nutritional group, said, "1992 has been a difficult year." He said that the growth of health-care cost-containment programs are "increasingly affecting the business worldwide."
Analysts had mixed reactions. Richard R. Vietor at Merrill Lynch said the cutbacks were "very surprising -- you don't think of Bristol as a layoff type of company."
Barbara Ryan at Alex. Brown & Sons, noted that American patent protection on Capoten, a heart drug with annual sales of about $1.6 billion, would expire in 1995. Capoten generated about 26 percent of Bristol-Myers Squibb's prescription drug sales last year.
But David Lippman at Dean Witter Reynolds said Capoten sales growth was still strong overseas.

HEADLINE: 2 in Race Disagree On Defense And Trade

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY

 BODY:
With a troublesome economy and social issues as focal points of their rancorous race for the United States Senate, Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato and Attorney General Robert Abrams have virtually ignored any discussion of foreign policy issues.
Even on those rare occasions a foreign policy issue has been raised, like military budgets and the future of Russia, their answers have been framed in domestic concerns, like the impact on jobs and trade possibilities. 
 But a broader examination of their positions on world affairs reveals a striking difference between them despite their relative agreement on narrow issues like favoring improved relations with Israel after the periods of strain through the last four years.
In essence, Mr. D'Amato, a Republican seeking his third Senate term, and Mr. Abrams, a Democrat in his first bid for national office, embrace fundamentally different visions of the future, in that Mr. Abrams sees a world less threatening to the United States than Mr. D'Amato does.
 
Quick-Strike Force
 The disparity emerges through the role they see the United States playing in the near future and the degree to which they would reduce the current national defense budget of $290 billion over the next five years.
While both envision the kind of mobile, quick-strike military force used in the Persian Gulf war in 1991, with a strong presence in Europe and other strategic places, Mr. D'Amato's force would be larger. He favors reductions of $85 billion in the defense budget over the next five years, and a decrease of 400,000 troops, to 1.5 million. Mr. Abrams would cut spending by more than three times as much, $280 billion over the same period, and require only 1.1 million troops.
Both see the need for reducing the 300,000 troops stationed in Europe -- Mr. Abrams to 75,000 to 100,000; Mr. D'Amato, to about 100,000.
In a recent interview, Mr. D'Amato assailed the continuing presence of Saddam Hussein in Iraq and the ethnic wars in Yugoslavia, citing them as reasons for slower defense reductions. He said the demise of the Soviet Union and Communist governments in Eastern Europe was no guarantee military threats had evaporated. He called North Korea "still a military threat" and said he worries about countries like Iran and China buying weapons on the open market.
 
Similar Concerns
 Mr. Abrams cited similar concerns in a separate interview but not to the degree expressed by Mr. D'Amato, who he said "hasn't yet realized the cold war is over." Mr. Abrams argued that the cuts and troop levels he advocates, drawn from a recent study by the Brookings Institution, would be sufficient to safeguard the interests of the United States.
While he would keep the United States military advantage over other countries, he played down the need for the United States to act as "the world's policeman," saying he preferred what he described as the "collective action" of the gulf war.
In an election year in which voters are far more focused on domestic concerns, Mr. D'Amato's approach of seeking smaller cuts in military spending is tied to jobs. He says slashing the military further would not only undermine national security but also devastate New York's economy by eliminating hundreds of thousands of jobs from defense contractors like Grumman on Long Island and General Electric in Schenectady.
But Mr. Abrams argued that the longer-term economic stability of New York requires conversion of military production to consumer businesses and reallocating dollars saved from military spending into programs to rebuild the nation's infrastructure. Mr. D'Amato favors the same kind of programs but a more gradual conversion rate.
 
'Responsible Reductions'
 "With the end of the cold war, we cannot bury our head in the sand and miss this opportunity," Mr. Abrams said. "We can remain the strongest military power in the world, but at the same time get the benefit of the end of the cold war with responsible reductions in the military budget."
On trade issues, Mr. D'Amato favors approval of the North American Free Trade Agreement with Canada and Mexico, which means he supports the measure as written.
Like Gov. Bill Clinton in the Presidential race, Mr. Abrams supports the agreement in principle but would prefer amendments or parallel legislation to require Mexico to enforce its environmental standards and insure worker safety and the United States to provide retraining programs for Americans losing their jobs when their employers move to Mexico.
Both candidates contend that trade relations with China, specifically most-favored-nation status, should be tied to improved human-rights policies in China, especially in the wake of the Chinese Government's crackdown on pro-democracy demonstrators in Tiananmen Square in 1989.
But Mr. Abrams takes a harder line against the Chinese Government than Mr. D'Amato, saying "the furthering of democratic principles and human rights should be at the cornerstone of foreign policy" with China.
The Senator called China a "very, very difficult situation." In July 1991, he voted against granting most-favored-nation status to China because of Tiananmen Square. Less than two years later, when the issue came before Congress a second time, he voted with the minority to grant most-favored trade status as a way to encourage the Chinese leadership to adopt more democratic principles.
But a month later, after the President vetoed Congressional rejection of favored-nation trade status, the Senator joined 59 other senators in an override vote that fell 7 votes short.
Mr. Abrams contended that Mr. D'Amato's change in position represented either an inconsistency in position or political opportunism, in that it never appeared that the Senate would gather enough votes to deny China the preferred status.
The Senator disagreed, saying his initial support in 1991 was based on the President's satisfying concerns of Congress through an executive order that, among other things, protected Chinese students living in the United States by not forcing them to leave. His override vote, he said, was cast in the spirit of supporting human rights.
Both candidates chided the Bush Administration for reacting too slowly to help Russia toward democracy and a market economy. Both would encourage investment to further open Russia to American products.

HEADLINE: G.M. Had Big Deficit In Quarter

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Oct. 29

 BODY:
The General Motors Corporation, laboring with excess car-manufacturing capacity and unable to trim costs fast enough, posted a third-quarter loss of $752.9 million, reflecting lower car and truck production in the United States and strikes by the United Automobile Workers union at G.M. plants at Lordstown, Ohio, and Lansing, Mich.
G.M.'s poor financial results come as the board of the No. 1 auto maker has ousted its chairman and chief executive, Robert C. Stempel, contending that he did not move quickly enough to turn the company around. Still, the financial results represented an improvement over a $1.1 billion loss in the third quarter of last year. 
 Revenue in the latest quarter was $29.44 billion, a 1.7 percent increase from $28.94 billion a year earlier. A spokesman said the strikes cost the auto maker about 50,000 vehicles' worth of lost production.
G.M. was the last of the Big Three to report financial results for the quarter. The Ford Motor Company said Wednesday that it had a loss of $158.9 million, compared with a loss of $574.4 million a year earlier. The Chrysler Corporation previously posted a profit of $202 million, in contrast to a net loss of $82 million a year earlier.
 
Similar to Estimates
 G.M.'s loss was in line with analysts' estimates and a company disclosure in a Securities and Exchange Commission filing last month. G.M.'s common shares were unchanged today on the New York Stock Exchange at $31.375.
John Casesa, an automotive industry analyst for Wertheim Schroder & Company in New York, estimated that G.M.'s worldwide automotive operations lost about $1.3 billion, offset by about $600 million in profit from its three nonautomotive subsidiaries, which reported earnings yesterday. G.M. said its overseas automotive operations remained profitable.
Mr. Casesa rates at 50 percent the chances of a reduction or elimination of the dividend on G.M. common stock when the board meets Monday in New York. The auto maker, which last cut its dividend in 1991, currently pays 40 cents a quarter, or $1.60 a year; the dividend payments amount to about $1 billion annually.
"If G.M. is going to clean house, cutting the dividend would be consistent with a fresh start," he said. "Many institutional holders I speak to not only expect it, but think it's the right thing to do."
G.M. has been trying to cut costs so it can make money on lower production volume. But because the cost-cutting has not occurred fast enough to satisfy the directors, the G.M. board will be meeting to select a replacement for Robert C. Stempel, who resigned as chairman this week. It appears likely that John G. Smale, a G.M. director, will become chairman, while John F. Smith Jr., G.M.'s president, will be given the additional role of chief executive.
For the moment, however, Mr. Stempel said in a statement accompanying the financial results: "Aggressive cost-cutting activities are being intensified, and plans to streamline G.M. into a lean, flexible and highly competitive organization are being implemented at an accelerated rate." Mr. Stempel agreed to remain on the job until the board replaced him.
For the first nine months of the year, G.M. lost $970.7 million on revenue of $96.67 billion, compared with a loss of $1.99 billion a year earlier, on revenue of $89.45 billion.
 
Larger Tax Credit
 G.M.'s said it benefited from a $629.8 million income-tax credit in the quarter, compared with a year-earlier credit of $327.3 million. G.M.'s nonautomotive pretax income rose to $536.5 million, from $259.1 million a year earlier, partly because of a gain from the sale of its interest in a robotics joint venture.
Worldwide factory shipments of G.M. cars and trucks to dealers reached 1.5 million in the third quarter, down 5.2 percent from the quarter in 1991. G.M. records revenue and profit when a vehicle is shipped from the factory.
G.M. said its volume reflected an emphasis on retail sales to individuals and less stress on fleet sales, which are not as profitable as sales to individuals.
G.M. dealers in the United States sold 1.1 million vehicles in the quarter, down 3.2 percent from a year earlier. The auto maker's penetration of the American new-vehicle market dropped to 33.3 percent, compared with 34.5 percent in the 1991 quarter.
According to Mr. Casesa's calculations, G.M.'s gross profit margin dropped slightly from a year earlier, because of three factors: lower production volume, strengthening of the German mark and more money set aside to pay warranty claims.
Acknowledging that the rest of the year could be difficult for G.M. if the economy does not show more signs of strength, Mr. Stempel said, "G.M. is even more determined to continue reducing structural costs, improving efficiency and quality, and strengthening its product line."

HEADLINE: Bitterness for One Family and Relief for the Other

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL

 BODY:
They had lived in limbo for more than a year, two families whose lives had been changed by the racial violence that ripped through Crown Heights in August 1991.
Norman Rosenbaum, an Australian tax lawyer, flew back and forth from Melbourne, urging the authorities to arrest and convict the black youths who set upon his younger brother Yankel, fatally beating and stabbing him. The attack was apparently in retaliation after a car driven by a Hasidic Jew killed a black child. 
 Lemrick Nelson Sr. and his former wife, Valerie Evans, waited to see whether their 17-year-old son, held in Rikers Island for a year, would be freed or convicted of the murder of the Hasidic scholar from Australia.
A Brooklyn courtroom held the answer. And for a moment this week as the jury deliberated, the bereaved brother and the worried father crossed paths in a hallway of the courthouse. The two shook hands, both now at the mercy of the jury.
But yesterday, as Lemrick Nelson Jr. was acquitted, one man's joy became another's searing pain.
 
A Cry of Pain
 Mr. Rosenbaum, who had sat so quietly in the courtroom, hoarsely and angrily vented his rage at the verdict in his heavy Australian accent. The sea of words could not stop.
"The ladies and gentlemen who were chosen to uphold the responsibility of justice have disregarded the blatant and obvious evidence that was placed before them," he cried out in pain, in the twilight outside State Supreme Court. "I believe that this jury -- in the same way that Lemrick Nelson has the blood of Yankel Rosenbaum on his hands and will have it until his dying day -- now the jury has the blood of Yankel Rosenbaum on their hands."
He alluded to the police testimony that had not swayed the jury -- testimony that 29-year-old Yankel, who was lying in a pool of blood awaiting an ambulance, stared at Mr. Nelson and cried: "The red shirt. That's the one. Why did you stab me?"
"My brother looked him in the eye and he identified not the shirt," Mr. Rosenbaum said to the throng of reporters. "He identified the person, the murderer, the person who killed him, and by God, if Mayor Dinkins hasn't heard me before, he better hear now: Jewish blood is not cheap."
As he spoke, Lemrick Nelson Sr., an unemployed baker, and Mrs. Evans were reunited with their son. Each day of the trial they had shared a few moments of whispered conversation with the young man before he was returned to jail.
 
A Good Turkey Dinner
 When the verdict was announced, the elder Mr. Nelson crossed himself. Lemrick Jr. hugged his mother, saying only, "Mommy, Mommy."
In the office of his defense lawyer, Arthur Lewis Jr., as Lemrick Jr. sat free and eating a hamburger, Mrs. Evans said, "It feels good that God freed him." She said she would cook her son a good turkey dinner.
"He's going to need some good soul food," the young man's father added. "As a first thing, he's going to need a good human bath."
Mr. Nelson, an immigrant from Trinidad, said his son would not remain long in New York. He said he would send him to stay with the youth's half-sister in Atlanta, then to Trinidad to finish his schooling, and would join him there soon.
Mr. Nelson had a message for Norman Rosenbaum: "Two days ago I shook his hand and wished him the best of luck. It could have been my brother it happened to. We have to work out a system to have harmony between blacks and Jews in Crown Heights. There's been tension for the past 12 years there."
He added: "I have no hatred against him. I feel bad about his brother."
But for Norman Rosenbaum, the ordeal was far from over. For one year he had crisscrossed continents, returning to New York again and again to demand justice. He spoke at rallies, denouncing the city's "pathetic leadership." He called for a Federal investigation.
Yesterday, dressed in the traditional dark suit and hat of the Hasidic Jew, he was left calling for justice once more. "You had a pogrom on your hands," he said. "My brother died. You could have died."
Behind him a crowd of supporters chanted, "Jewish blood is not cheap!"

HEADLINE: Nations Helping Ex-Soviet Lands Switch Systems

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Oct. 29

 BODY:
The nations providing emergency aid to the former Soviet republics have agreed at a conference here to dismantle the year-old system for funneling billions of dollars to those newly independent countries and replace it with a process that would still offer large amounts of aid but would apply more pressure on the countries to open and reform their stagnating economies.
While the decision is intended to transform what had been an ad hoc process into a more permanent and efficient one, it also sends a strong message to political leaders in Moscow and the other capitals that the assistance they receive from the West could be jeopardized if they do not move faster to build market economies, officials here said. 
 The purpose of this conference was organizational rather than to provide a forum to pledge new funds for the former Soviet states. But the United States and Japan did announce new programs. The Japanese said they would provide an additional $100 million in food and other humanitarian assistance and the United States announced that it would provide $100 million in corn. Both countries said their aim was to insure that the people of the new states were adquately fed through the coming winter.
Delegates to the conference were united in calling this the last meeting under the old aid-coordination system, which was begun in January in Washington, and the first in a new regime. It will have two immediate effects.
 
Bleakness Gives Impetus
 Instead of being organized by sectors, like humanitarian assistance, technical assistance and energy aid, the new process will be broken down by country. And that means that Russia and the other new states will be treated much like third-world nations are treated by the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund.
The Russians had been resisting that approach until now. But what seems to have given impetus to the new phase is the bleakness of the economic situation in Russia and the other states.
In a report to the conference, which includes the United States, the European Community, Japan and more than 50 other countries here to observe the proceedings, the I.M.F., World Bank and other organizations offered a sobering analysis of the problems in Russia and the 14 other former Soviet republics.
Oil production has declined 30 percent over the last four years, they said. Grain production, while up this year from 1991, is still below average levels in the preceding five years. Inflation is rising at more than 20 percent a month.
The I.M.F. said today that it estimates Russia will need $22 billion of external loans and aid to pay its debts and meet its other financial needs next year. That calculation rests on the assumption that Russia meets its targets for reining in its budget deficit and slows the growth of its money supply, two things it has failed to do so far.
That means the number could be far larger. But another aim of the conference is to push ahead with programs that will make the newly independent states more self-sufficient. Most delegates said that was the heart of what is called technical assistance.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Independent;
Campaign Stalled, Perot Team Tries to Fix It, Fast

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DALLAS, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Acknowledging that the Ross Perot's rise in the polls has stalled, his aides tried today to defuse two issues that they fear have slowed his momentum: the charge that he is trying to buy the election, and his unsubstantiated assertions that the Bush campaign planned to disrupt his daughter's wedding and to wiretap his business telephones.
The Perot campaign has developed different strategies for dealing with the two issues. Aides are confronting the first with claims that President Bush and Gov. Bill Clinton have actually outspent Mr. Perot. They are confronting the second with silence. 
 "I think the realization was made yesterday or the day before that the attention on the nonissues, the character assassination, was really playing into the hands of the other two candidates," Clayton Mulford, the campaign's general counsel, said today. "We need to get back to our message."
 
'The Recession is Here'
 Mr. Perot hit hard on that message during an appearance on "Larry King Live" tonight, asserting that, contrary to Mr. Bush's claims, the recession has not ended.
"Oh, give me a break, Larry," Mr. Perot said when asked by his host if the recession was over. "The recession's not over. The recession is here. The recession is going to stay here because of deficit spending and competition for money."
Mr. Perot went on to say that "everybody out there except the White House knows the recession is here. And if you lived in that insulated bubble they've created for the President, you wouldn't know it either."
According to the classic definition of a recession as two straight quarters of no economic growth, the recession ended last year. The nation has had six straight quarters of economic growth, but because the recovery has been so sluggish, many people feel the nation is still mired in a recession.
In an interview taped with David Frost on Wednesday that is scheduled to air Friday night, Mr. Perot asserted that he would "hate being a Cabinet member," refused to rule out a 1996 Presidential race, and seconded the assertion of his running mate, Adm. James B. Stockdale, that anti-war protests had prolonged the Vietnam War.
Mr. Perot also repeated his charges that he has been redefined by political opponents. "I'm not paranoid," he said, rejecting the word used by Marlin Fitzwater, the White House spokesman. "You will never find the word 'paranoid' used once in all of the thousands, hundreds of thousands of words written about me before this started."
John Florescu, the executive producer of the Frost show, said today that Mr. Perot had become "testy" when Mr. Frost had asked him about his July withdrawal from the race and about his allegations of Republican dirty tricks. At one point, Mr. Florescu said, Mr. Perot asked that the cameras be turned off, and they were. "I think he just wanted to collect his thoughts," Mr. Florescu said. "He was annoyed."
 
Sliding in the Polls
 At a press briefing Mr. Mulford agreed with a reporter's suggestion that public opinion polls suggest that the air seems to be leaking out of Mr. Perot's campaign, and he attributed that development to critical stories about the independent that were "planted" by his opponents. Mr. Mulford produced no evidence to support that assertion.
A tracking poll conducted by Gallup for USA Today and CNN that will be released Friday indicates that 16 percent of those surveyed backed Mr. Perot. Last week a similar poll gave Mr. Perot the support of 20 percent of those surveyed. Similarly, a tracking poll by ABC News today showed Mr. Perot at 16 percent, a five-point drop from a similar poll released on Monday that had the Texan at 21 percent.
A tracking poll is a survey in which fresh samples of voters are interviewed daily and the combined results of two or three days of interviews are released.
Public opinion surveys are not the only indicators that the Perot boomlet may have peaked. Figures compiled by Nielsen Media Research News indicate that the number of people tuning into Mr. Perot's 30-minute and 60-minute "infomercial" ads has dropped sharply. About 16.5 million viewers watched his first one on Oct. 6. Since then none of the other half-hour programs have come close to attracting that many viewers. On Monday, 12 million viewers -- a respectable showing, but a 25 percent decrease -- watched at least part of his half-hour ad.
Obviously concerned about their candidate's stall, Mr. Mulford and Sharon Holman, the campaign's press secretary, told reporters today that they would no longer answer questions about Mr. Perot's assertions that Republican operatives had planned to conduct a smear campaign against his daughter or about his involvement in getting the F.B.I. to conduct an undercover sting operation on the chairman of the Bush campaign in Texas.
Meanwhile, seven former Perot volunteers held a news conference in Washington today to accuse Mr. Perot of ordering illegal credit checks on them and of forcing them out of the campaign. They also told stories of high-handed tactics like being pressured to sign "loyalty oaths."
Mr. Perot has admitted investigating some of his volunteers because he suspected wrongdoing. The participants provided documents showing they had been investigated but had no proof that Mr. Perot was directly involved.
 
All or Nothing
 Mr. Perot also continued his running feud with the news media today. The Texan was scheduled to be interviewed today on "Prime Time Live" and on Friday on "20/20". Since both programs are broadcast on ABC, the network's executives decided they might be giving Mr. Perot too much exposure. When they asked which program he would prefer to appear on, Mr. Perot told them that if he could not appear on both, he would appear on neither.
During his interview with Mr. King, Mr. Perot said ABC had appeared to be pushing him to appear on only "Prime Time Live," where host Sam Donaldson would interview him during one of the show's segments.
"I really object to being jerked around because I am neither a Democrat nor a Republican," Mr. Perot told Mr. King. "I'm just a cur dog put on the ballot by the people. I feel that if you invite somebody to do something, you should honor your commitment or at least give them a voice."

HEADLINE: Shirley Journal;
Where Bush's Remarks Hit Too Close to Home

BYLINE: By RONALD SMOTHERS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HIRLEY, Ark., Oct. 29

 BODY:
Linda Burgess at the Hair Fashion Beauty Shop said she didn't "give a rip because it's just politics." Watson I. Smith, a 79-year-old retired teacher, said it struck him as a "tad too mean-spirited."
And Everett Henderson, a farmer who warmed himself with his back to the pot-bellied stove in Privitt's Hardware store, said it reminded him of the "hillbilly put-downs" he and other Arkansans used to hear in the Army. 
 A little more than a week after President Bush spotlighted Arkansas's shortcomings in a Presidential debate and referred to the state as the "lowest of the low," feelings still run strong among many in this town of 354 in the foothills of the Ozark Mountains, where homes seem to hang from the sides of the roads.
Situated 75 miles northwest of Little Rock, Shirley is a place where the appellation "hillbilly" has ceased to be a fighting word and become more of a marketing tool, as in Bea Macke's "hillbilly pizza" at B & G's Bakery. ("It's a deep-dish affair with Parmesan, mozzarella and provolone cheeses," she said.) But many heard in the President's words an echo of the old negative stereotypes.
"He didn't use the word 'hillbilly,' but that's what a lot of us thought about when he said that," said Mayor John Clark Jr., a strong supporter of Gov. Bill Clinton, as he stood in the parking lot of Jones Grocery Store, where farmers, retirees and others often gather for morning coffee. "It's like he thinks we all left fresh, barefoot footprints out in the parking lot."
The President's reference to the state as "the lowest of low" as he recounted its meager standing in areas like children's health, teachers' pay, education spending, working conditions and income, reverberated throughout Arkansas, making many people feel as if they had been cast adrift from the rest of the nation. It sent Republican candidates scrambling either to distance themselves from the comments or to try to clarify them as aimed more at policies than people.
But more than anything else, it struck at what Art English, a political science professor at the University of Arkansas, called the war between pride and a pervasive "inferiority complex" in the state. Arkansans are aware that they rank low in a number of categories, he and others noted, but also have a sense that they have done much with little and have come a long way in a very short time.
Betsey Wright, the research director of the Clinton campaign whose office prepared detailed responses challenging some of the President's comments, called them more of the effort to "divide us by race, age, gender and income" rather than bring people together.
"Bush doesn't know anything about rural people," said Mr. Smith. "He may be a smart man, but he's a dumb politician for attacking the state and the character of the people here."
The schools in Shirley sit atop a hill that looks down on the valley where the middle fork of the Little Red River flows. The river marked the path of the old Missouri and North Arkansas Rail Road that ran between Joplin and West Helena on the Arkansas side of the Mississippi Delta. Shirley was founded in 1907 as a station along that line carrying timber between the two cities. It is named for the first stationmaster's daughter.
The 46-year-old Mr. Clark remembers the old clapboard schoolhouse that was too cold in winter and sweltering in spring. The building, due to be renovated, sits in stark relief to the sprawling one-story brick buildings of the high school and elementary school. All that came in the last 10 years, he said, when Governor Clinton and his wife, Hillary, "literally forced the state education system to help itself and become better."
Not everyone in town was offended by the President's remarks. Mrs. Macke, the co-owner of B & G's Bakery, said that it was all hot air and that she still intended to vote for Mr. Bush. Jo Reid, Ailene Smith and Alice Wiley, customers at the bakery from nearby Fairfield Bay, a retirement community of 2,500, said that among their neighbors Republicanism runs deeper than Arkansas chauvinism.
Jades Goldman, a native of Shirley, pulled his pick-up truck in front of Privitts today on what was the last day of muzzle-loader deer season, got out and showed off the two-point, white-tailed deer he had shot with his single-shot weapon. The short season is one of the state's refinements for thousands of enthusiasts who like the gradations of skill and purity offered by special times for long-rifle and multi-shot hunting, the muzzle-loader season and the bow-and-arrow hunting season. But Mr. Goldman conceded that he enjoyed deer hunting so much that he would be out there if they had a rock-throwing season as well.
"I'm a George Bush fan and not a Clinton fan," said the 33-year-old. "After all, we are kind of low on the totem pole, and I don't think he meant anything bad or derogatory."
But others like Tom Kimmons, who runs a local development company, said President Bush's comments betrayed a certain condescension to rural people. "He made a fundamental error," said Mr. Kimmons. "He separated and excluded us and others like us. But we're Americans too."

HEADLINE: Yeltsin Suspends Baltic Troop Pullout

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Oct. 29

 BODY:
President Boris N. Yeltsin issued a decree today suspending the withdrawal of Russian troops from the Baltic states, asserting a "profound concern" over the treatment of Russians there.
The decree was certain to provoke a storm in Estonian, Latvia and Lithuania, whose governments view the continuing presence of Russian troops on their territories as the greatest single threat to their security and independence. 
 The decree came nine days after the Russian Defense Ministry announced that it was suspending the withdrawal because there was nowhere for the troops to go. The announcement said that 24,000 more troops had been scheduled to leave the region by the end of the year, but that thousands of officers were already homeless in Russia and that forces were still returning from Eastern Europe.
Various numbers have been given for the size of the force in the Baltics. About 130,000 Soviet troops were stationed in the Baltic lands before they gained independence, and the United States State Department has said about 40 percent have been pulled out.
 
Rights Abuses Charged
 In the announcement of Mr. Yeltsin's decree, however, no mention was made of the housing problem. It said rather that troop withdrawal would be resumed after Russia signed agreements with the three Baltic states that included protection for the servicemen and met Mr. Yeltsin's "profound concern over numerous infringements of rights of the Russian-speaking population in the Baltic countries."
The announcement said Mr. Yeltsin also linked this issue to "the implementation of economic agreements," suggesting that he might consider withholding fuel and other materials from the Baltic states, which are struggling with dire economic problems.
It was not clear what immediately prompted Mr. Yeltsin's decree, or why he included all three Baltic states. While Moscow has locked horns with Latvia and Estonia, both of which have large Russian minorities, it has agreed to pull all troops out of Lithuania by September 1993.
At home, the President is locked in a bitter power struggle with nationalist and Communist forces, and one possibility was that he wanted to deny them one inflammatory issue while combating them on others.
 
Voting Rights Limited
 Russians have seethed over laws passed in Latvia and Estonia limiting voting rights to residents who lived there before 1940 and their descendants. That in effect excluded many of the resident Russians, especially those in the military or military industries. An exception is made for Russians who are literate in the local language.
The Russian Parliament has passed stern resolutions threatening sanctions against the two Baltic states and has harangued the Government for purportedly doing nothing for the expatriate Russians.
At the same time, the absence of housing for returning officers has become a serious problem, one Mr. Yeltsin obviously did not want to worsen at a time of political instability.
There was the possibility that Mr. Yeltsin was reaching for a moral justification for a decision that had already been made by the military, or to which there was no alternative.
In August, Russia proposed agreements to the Baltic states offering to withdraw troops by 1994 on the condition that the three republics guarantee the rights of resident Russians and soldiers and abandon territorial claims on Russia. The agreements have been rejected by Latvia and Estonia, which argue that Russia is raising the human-rights issues only to sustain its hegemony over the region and to delay withdrawing its forces.
 
Hoping to Postpone Congress
 On the domestic front, Mr. Yeltsin said in a published interview that he still hoped to postpone the convening of a full Congress of Peoples' Deputies, the highest legislative body in Russia, which is now scheduled for Dec. 1.
"The struggle to postpone the Congress is not over yet," he said in an interview with the weekly Argumenty i Fakty.
Parliament's refusal to delay the session has fueled one of the most severe confrontations yet between Mr. Yeltsin and an increasingly aggressive bloc of hard-line deputies hostile to his Government and his reforms.
Mr. Yeltsin also reaffirmed his determination to keep Yegor T. Gaidar, the acting Prime Minister and chief architect of reforms, in office. Forcing him to step down, he said, "would be tantamount to derailing the reform."
In a war of nerves over the last week, the leadership of Parliament ordered guards from its 5,000-man security force to take up posts outside Izvestia, the independent newspaper that Ruslan Khasbulatov, the ambitious Speaker, has claimed for Parliament.
 
Force Said to Ignore Order
 Mr. Yeltsin countered by declaring the security force illegal. But at last report commanders of the force were ignoring his order.
The President also issued a decree banning the newly proclaimed National Salvation Front, a coalition of Communist and nationalist hard-liners that had called for his resignation.
But leaders of the front called a news conference today saying the ban was unconstitutional and accusing Mr. Yeltsin of "an open war against the people."
Adding to the air of intrigue was the fact that Mr. Khasbulatov spent the week in a hospital, reportedly suffering from hypertension. He was released today.
Mr. Khasbulatov was hospitalized Oct. 21 after being helped out of the Parliament chamber. Some deputies accused him of being drunk.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Digital to Close Two Plants

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: SPRINGFIELD, Mass., Oct. 29

 BODY:
The Digital Equipment Corporation announced today that it was closing its manufacturing plants here and in Burlington, Vt., putting about 670 employees out of work.
The Springfield plant employs about 320 people and the Vermont operation about 350. Officials of the struggling computer maker said they expected the work force to be phased out and the plants shut by April. 
 "The movement of computing power to semiconductors; the shift in demand to high-volume, low-margin commodity products, and the decreased demand caused by a soft worldwide economy has left Digital with excess worldwide manufacturing capacity," said Edward B. McDonough, vice president for manufacturing and logistics.

HEADLINE: Jobless Claims Up Slightly

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
The number of Americans seeking unemployment benefits rose slightly in mid-October after falling for three straight weeks, the Government said today.
The Labor Department said 375,000 workers lined up at state unemployment offices to file claims for jobless benefits during the week ended Oct. 17. That compared with 367,000 applications filed the week before. 
 The four-week moving average for claims -- viewed as a more accurate indicator of longer-term labor conditions -- fell for the third straight week, to its lowest level in two years.
The Administration was quick to draw encouragement from the longer-term trend, as President Bush strives to overtake Gov. Bill Clinton with five days left until the election.
"This indicates a stronger labor market and is good news for America's working men and women," Labor Secretary Lynn Martin said.
 
Economists Restrained
Economists were more restrained, although they agreed that the worst might be over for the job market. "It's not death and destruction any more," said Brian Jones, an economist for Salomon Brothers in New York.
While layoffs may be moderating, the economy is growing so slowly that it is not creating many new jobs, said Robert Falconer, senior vice president at Aubrey G. Lanston & Company, a New York brokerage firm.
In a separate report today, the Conference Board, a New York-based business research organization, said that help-wanted advertisements in 51 newspapers throughout the country fell in September from August to a level below that of a year earlier.
Still, analysts said they expected the job market to improve slowly in 1993 as the economy gathers steam.
"I suspect we will see some gradual movement there as we go into 1993, but it is not going to be dramatic," John LaWare, a Federal Reserve governor, said.
The Labor Department said 15 states reported that initial jobless claims fell by more than 1,000 in the week ended Oct. 17, led by California, Pennsylvania and New York. Only Delaware saw jobless claims rise by more than 1,000, a result of layoffs in the automobile industry.
Economists said the number of workers seeking jobless benefits may have been understated because of the Federal emergency unemployment compensation program. The Labor Department said 21,418 workers filed for Federal benefits in the latest week, compared with 23,340 the week before.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Media;
For the Most Negative Ads, Turn On the Nearest Radio

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT

 BODY:
It's the Great Invisible Attack campaign.
Heading into the final days of the Presidential race, the Bush campaign is running a series of highly critical, carefully aimed radio advertisements aimed at undermining Gov. Bill Clinton's support in crucial states. Over the weekend, when it will be almost impossible for the Clinton campaign to respond, the Bush campaign is planning to put more attack advertisements on the radio. 
 Far cheaper than television, radio advertising is also widely regarded as less effective. But radio, which receives far less press scrutiny than television, is often the medium that candidates use to deliver their most scathing -- and sometimes misleading -- critiques.
As the campaign moves toward the close, all three leading candidates -- Mr. Clinton, President Bush and Ross Perot -- are planning heavy advertising blitzes over the weekend on both radio and television.
On television, both the Clinton and the Bush campaigns are expected to continue broadcasting a mixture of positive and negative messages. But on radio the message is likely to be unremittingly downbeat.
 
'Hard-Hitting Attacks'
 "Radio is a great medium for delivering hard-hitting attacks," said Darrell West, a professor of political science at Brown University who studies campaign advertising. "It's a risk-free type of advertising. You can be incredibly obnoxious without being blamed for it."
Many of the television advertisements that have been broadcast during the Presidential campaign have been based on information that could, at best, be called questionable. But it is on the radio that the truth has been treated the most casually.
Several of the Bush campaign's radio commercials are variations on a single theme -- that Mr. Clinton will impose a tax on energy, a "carbon tax," that would drive up utility bills and cost jobs.
"He's said he's for change, but look at the change he's really talking about," says an advertisement running in states like Maine, Michigan, Maryland and Illinois. "An independent analysis says that Clinton-Gore radical environmental taxes will drive up our annual utility bills over $350."
 
Great Leap in Logic
 Mr. Clinton has not proposed a carbon tax, and aides to the Governor call the advertisements grossly misleading. But the Bush campaign says the commercials are justified because Mr. Clinton's running mate, Senator Al Gore, advocates such a tax in his best-selling book, "Earth in the Balance." Also, they note, the Governor himself has said that the nation's carbon dioxide emissions should be reduced, something, Bush aides insist in a leap of logic, that can be achieved only with a carbon tax.
"Governor Clinton has very specifically said he'll entertain the idea," said Bill Lacy, a senior political adviser to the Bush campaign.
Acting on the belief that any attack that goes unanswered is taken by voters to be true, the Clinton campaign has tried to reply to each of the Bush campaign's radio commercials within 24 hours. In their responses, the Clinton campaign has tried to inoculate the Governor against future attacks as well by planting doubts about the President's credibility.
"It's the same old story: George Bush will say anything to get elected," says a Clinton radio commercial that, with minor adjustments, is running in several states. "He said so himself."
The commercial ends, "So the next time you hear a George Bush ad, ask yourself, isn't it time for a President who will fight for Americans' jobs?'
The most recent Bush radio advertisement, which is running in New Jersey as well as in several other states, purports to "examine the facts" about Governor Clinton's record on child welfare and on crime.
"If he can't care for children and keep the public safety in his own state," the commercial asks, "how can he expect a promotion?"
The Clinton campaign has responded with an advertisement that says: "George Bush is at it again. Because he can't run on his own record, Bush is resorting to negative attacks against Bill Clinton."
Mr. Perot's radio advertisements do not mention either Mr. Bush or Mr. Clinton by name. Instead they offer the message of a pox on both your houses.
"Now here's an extreme case that just shows you how goofy things are in Washington," Mr. Perot says in one of his advertisements. "In order to find a Christmas tree, the White House decided it was necessary to send two people all the way to Oregon to look at the tree at taxpayers' expense. They are in the piggy bank that you and I created with our taxpayer money. We need to get 'em out."
 
New Bush Ads Planned
 Yesterday, aides to the President would not say what new radio advertisements were planned for the weekend. But when a reporter asked whether such commercials could be expected, Sig Rogich, who is coordinating the President's media campaign, said simply, "Yup." And because it is so difficult to respond to new advertisements over a weekend, when station offices are closed, the Clinton campaign is especially concerned about any last-minute attacks.
"Local radio is where the vilification and fear-mongering takes place," one adviser to Mr. Clinton said. The Clinton campaign has prepared several new radio advertisements, which aides to the Governor say they will broadcast, depending on what the Bush campaign has on radio.
To close its advertising campaign, the Clinton team is planning to air at least two new television commercials before Election Day. One is expected to continue the campaign's attacks on the President's economic record, while the other is supposed to be more positive, featuring either scenes from Clinton campaign bus trips or Mr. Clinton making a direct appeal to voters.
Yesterday the Clinton campaign began broadcasting a new television advertisement in Michigan and Ohio that describes how Japanese mini-vans that were labeled cars avoided import duties.
For its part, the Bush campaign has begun broadcasting two new television commercials, one that features spooky black and white landscapes and attacks Mr. Clinton's record in Arkansas and another that touts Mr. Bush's qualifications for the nation's highest office.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Campaign Almanac;
Countdown to Election Day

 BODY:
GEORGE BUSH
Buoyed by several new polls, President Bush made a foray into an old source of Democratic support, suburban Macomb County in Michigan, to sound the alarms about high taxes and low character that he hopes will drive voters away from Gov. Bill Clinton. Stripping his message to its essence, Mr. Bush told a crowd of 10,000 people that Mr. Clinton had "given up on reducing the deficit." Arms flailing, he denounced Mr. Clinton's running mate, Senator Al Gore, as an environmental extremist who is "crazy, way out, far out, man." He said of the Democrats, "They feel it slipping away from them."
 
BILL CLINTON
Governor Clinton dropped his plans to stick to a lofty message about the issues and leveled harsh words at President Bush in three toss-up states, Ohio, Michigan and New Jersey. While he sought to strike a sober tone in speeches on the economy and on AIDS research, Mr. Clinton cast his policy discussion in terms of what Mr. Bush lacks. And although he still leads in most polls, Mr. Clinton said for the first time in weeks that he considered himself an "underdog" in the race. "The American people are going to have to think a long time before they want four more years of this," Mr. Clinton said of the Bush Administration.
 
ROSS PEROT
Mr. Perot's aides tried to defuse two issues that they fear have slowed his momentum: the accusation that he is trying to buy the election and his unsubstantiated assertions that the Bush campaign planned to disrupt his daughter's wedding. Aides are confronting the first issue by saying that his two rivals have actually outspent him. They are confronting the second issue with silence. In an interview with David Frost, Mr. Perot testily rejected criticism leveled by a Bush spokesman. "I'm not paranoid," Mr. Perot said. He also said he would hate being a Cabinet member, a possibility held out by the Clinton campaign.

HEADLINE: Cuomo Pulls for Jobs Bond Act as Regan Pushes Objections

BYLINE: By SARAH LYALL

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has been traveling feverishly around New York State for the last few days, urging people to vote for his proposed $800 million jobs bond act. And practically wherever he goes, there also goes State Comptroller Edward V. Regan, nipping at his heels.
Mr. Regan, a Republican, is working just as hard to defeat the bond act as Mr. Cuomo, a Democrat, is to get it passed, saying that he would support it only if the Legislature passed measures giving voters more authority over borrowing, as well. "It is unaffordable in the current environment in which we finance ourselves," he said in Manhattan yesterday. 
 Under the bond act, Proposal 1 on the ballot in next week's election, the state would borrow $800 million to invest in a slew of infrastructure projects, like roads and sewers, with the goal of putting people to work and attracting businesses to the state. Governor Cuomo estimates that it would create 35,000 immediate jobs and eventually lead to 122,000 permanent ones.
Introduced by the Governor in his State of the State Message last January and finally passed after a bitter fight in the Legislature over the summer, the bond act is one of the Governor's major legislative achievements this year, and it would be a big blow to him if it were defeated. His previous bond proposal, a $1.975 billion environmental bond act, was turned down by the voters in 1990.
But convincing the voters of the necessity of borrowing money at a time when they are especially leery of excessive spending is no easy feat. The Governor's problem has been compounded by the opposition of Mr. Regan and of conservative groups like Change-NY and by a prohibition against using state money to promote the bond act.
Even so, the Cuomo administration has spent more than $150,000 on materials on the bond act, saying they are educational, not promotional. It has printed almost four million informational brochures and palm cards, which it is doling out at Gov. Thomas E. Dewey Thruway toll plazas, government buildings and the like.
And various agencies, including the Department of Environmental Conservation, the Office of Hispanic Affairs and the Office for the Aging, have sent out letters that describe the bond act but stop short of asking for votes.
The Governor, who has made a number of trips on behalf of the bond act, said he is doing the best he can within the confines of the law.
"We're living within the letter of the law, even if the law seems irrationally inappropriate," he said at a rally with unemployed construction workers in Nassau County on Wednesday. "My counsel's office instructs me that I'm free to advocate with all the vigor I possess."
For his part, Mr. Regan has made a number of appearances and plans another swing through upstate New York, urging the public all the while to reject the proposal.
