HEADLINE: On My Mind;
It's the Economy, Stupid

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL

 BODY:
Now that the campaign is almost over, the country owes the candidates gratitude for what Americans want so much -- a vacation from the realities of national life.
That might seem a crabby thought -- after all those months when the candidates kept lecturing the country about the economy. 
 "The economy, stupid," reads the sign in the office of Bill Clinton's campaign strategist. President Bush is boiling down the whole election to two words: trust and taxes. Ross Perot managed to do it in one -- deficit.
The attitude surveys convinced candidates that all that Americans cared about was the economy and how it translated into jobs. Ignore that and lose.
The surveys were probably right. So given the choice of lead, follow or get out of the way, the candidates trotted right behind the polls.
But something strange happened -- all the virtuous talk and position papers on the economy had a smothering, flattening-out effect on American reality and the American memory. It homogenized the very problems that a sturdier economy was supposed to cure. That allowed them to be comfortably diminished in our mind, priorities and conscience.
In April, when Los Angeles was burning, the country, in shock, literally prayed that this time our "leaders" would pay attention and do something, do something.
But look what happened, lamebrain: by summer Los Angeles was getting footnote political attention, barely.
Los Angeles was the result of economic and racial rage. Nonsense -- Los Angeles was the work of lawless hooligans looking for an excuse to riot. No -- Los Angeles was the result of both.
Take your pick. But one thing is sure -- everything that made for the Los Angeles riot still exists in cities around the country.
The mesmerized focus on broad-issue economics blunted the needle of national awareness that emergency social and law-enforcement action was imperative. That is dangerous.
Incidentally, whatever happened to the matter of racial tensions? Democrats are pushing race relations into the background because they do not want Jesse Jackson in the foreground. Republicans don't see political capital in talking about it at all. Mr. Perot is still recovering from the discovery that "you people" is not the way African-Americans prefer to be addressed.
Drugs, crime, a paralyzed justice system: candidates do put out some position papers. They don't get much attention, because they do not convey the horror.
Did anything from the candidates show they understood what "urban affairs" now means? What it is like to live in a neighborhood controlled by drug mobs and know they kill at whim or at random? Or where parents take their kids out of school to try to keep them from being shot down in the streets outside?
And which candidate or which position paper dealt with men and women with broken minds living, wandering and defecating in the streets?
The political professionals pound it into our heads that the rest of the world matters zero in this campaign -- no clout in surveys at all. Still, it is hard to believe that Americans really don't care whether the next Presidency would try to prevent Bosnians from being murdered or Somali children from walking about with swollen bellies, just because Bosnians and Somalis don't create jobs, except for foreign grave diggers.
Unpleasantly specific truths are not ignored -- just smoothed down, made less scary, by over-generalized economy talk. We are told repeatedly that unemployment is about 7.5 percent -- but rarely that it is at least triple that for teen-agers in ghettos.
The candidates explain that strengthening the whole economy will help us find money to deal with drugs, crime, violence, maybe even a few Bosnians and Somalis.
That's nice. But the candidates should explain something else. How do other democracies manage to have cleaner streets, safer neighborhoods and safer banks, less drugs, no guns, better medical insurance, fewer wreckages of tenements and people, without waiting until the complete grand, overall, very macro, big-picture economic problem is settled, by some unspecified superstrategy?
Maybe Presidential candidates will find out. Maybe they will think city, town and neighborhood economics, as well as national.
When? In 1996, stupid.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING -- ADDENDA;
Omnicom Reports 35% Gain in Net

BYLINE: By Eben Shapiro

 BODY:
Omnicom Group Inc. reported yesterday that third-quarter net income climbed 35 percent as its foreign business improved and its domestic revenues finally fared better.
Net income increased to $10.4 million, or 37 cents a share, from $7.7 million, or 28 cents a share, in the year-earlier period. Revenue grew 19 percent, to $327.8 million from $275.4 million. 
 Despite the sluggish American economy, domestic revenue rose 6 percent, to $165.4 million from $159.5 million. International revenue jumped 40 percent, to $162.4 million from $115.9 million.
The New York company, with agencies including BBDO Worldwide and DDB Needham Worldwide, is the second large agency company this week to report solid earnings gains. The Interpublic Group of Companies reported a 26 percent increase in profits earlier this week.

HEADLINE: On Stage, and Off

BYLINE: By Bruce Weber

 BODY:

Sex Battle, No Codes
Landing in the wake of the Clarence Thomas-Anita Hill hearings and in the midst of the Barbara Wood-Randy Daniels tumult, "Oleanna," David Mamet's stringent battle of the sexes at the Orpheum, has an obviously polarizing potential. During one performance, when the college professor (William H. Macy) who is accused of harassment finally lashes back at his accuser (Rebecca Pidgeon), there was scattered applause in the audience, leading one woman, when the lights came up, to mutter angrily: "Let's find those guys who clapped."
In an interview this week, Mr. Mamet, who has, fairly or not, been accused in the past of misogyny, would not be drawn into a discussion of his intentions. "The play is not a code for some deeper feelings of mine," he said. "It's not an elaborate attempt to say anything other than what it says." 
 The first draft, he said, was written some eight months before the Hill-Thomas hearings, but it was the hearings that led him to pull it out of a drawer and work on it again.
Mr. Mamet acknowledged that a longtime friend of his, a college teacher whom he would not name, had been the target of a sexual harassment charge, and that the friend had written an article relating to it, which he has submitted to The New Yorker magazine. But Mr. Mamet, who had read the article, said that the play was written before he learned of his friend's story.
"He sent me the piece," Mr. Mamet said. "It's about his coming to see the show, and he describes himself as realizing that it isn't about him." (An editor at The New Yorker acknowledged the manuscript's existence but would not comment further, saying no decision had been made on whether to publish it.)
Asked, then, what drew him to the subject in the first place, Mr. Mamet, who has taught at Yale, the University of Chicago, New York University and other places, said: "That's like asking Capa why he took pictures of war."
Mr. Mamet also spoke briefly about the evolution of the end of the play. In an earlier version, the play ends, with several pages of dialogue in which the student tries to get her teacher to admit he is a harasser and that he has failed in his responsibilities.
The final lines were these:
CAROL: What do you see? What will you do differently?
JOHN: I...I... (PAUSE)
CAROL: It's difficult. Isn't it? (PAUSE) I know.
But as it is being performed now, the end is more oblique, more of a standoff. Both characters are exhausted, their weapons spent. The silence chills.
Here's Mr. Mamet's explanation for the change: "Stanislavski said the last 90 seconds are the most important part of the play. And Aristotle said that what one wants in a tragedy is for the hero to undergo the recognition and reversal of the situation in the same moment. That's the climax of the play. Put those two ideas together, and what you end up with, as Mel Blanc informed us, is: 'Th-th-th-that's all, folks!' "
 
'Tommy' to Be Staged
 Though it seems a natural mix at a time when producers are desperate to attract new audiences, rock-and-roll -- real rock-and-roll -- and musical theater have never been easygoing partners. On the one hand, the throbbing, high-volume rock sound is exactly what traditional musical audiences are seeking to escape, and on the other, the musical is part of a tradition that rock-and-roll has long been in rebellion against. Nonetheless, times change and extremes slouch toward the middle, and it was announced this week by Dodger Productions and the Pace Theatrical Group that "Tommy," an adaptation of the 1969 rock opera by the Who, would be presented at the St. James Theater beginning in April.
For those too old to care or too young to remember, "Tommy" was one of a number of song-cycle albums produced by the most ambitious bands of the time, among them the Beatles' "Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band" and "Arthur" by the Kinks. "Tommy" tells the story of a young boy, just after World War II, who is traumatized by witnessing his mother's infidelity and becomes psychosomatically deaf, mute and blind. Tormented by demonic relatives, he seeks refuge in his uncanny ability to play pinball. Eventually cured, he becomes, during the early 1960's, a kind of pseudo-guru, leading true believers to salvation.
Over the years, "Tommy" has been dramatized in various ways, including a wacky 1975 movie directed by Ken Russell, but it has never received a full-scale musical theater production.
The show's developmental run at the La Jolla Playhouse in California, with a cast of 22, closed on Oct. 4. It had an eight-piece rock band, including three synthesizers, a French horn (John Entwistle of the Who played the horn on the original recording), two guitarists, bass and drums. The director, Des McAnuff, will also direct in New York.
He said this week that he and Pete Townshend, the composer and a founding member of the Who, had just begun adjusting the show for Broadway. The budget has not yet been determined, but Mr. McAnuff said it was likely the show would be recast and the size of the company would increase. He also said it was possible that new music by Mr. Townshend would be added.
"I'd like to think that this is the ultimate family show," said Michael David, one of the Dodger producers, who is 50 years old with two grown children. "Unlike most shows, you can take your kids and they'll think you're cooler when it's over."
 
'Falsettos' in the Black
 The economic news from Broadway is generally so dismal that a small-scale success is welcome. "Falsettos," the William Finn and James Lapine musical at the Golden, has earned back its investment after just 28 weeks, the producer Barry Weissler said this week. Capitalized at $1 million, and with a small cast and minimal set, it is, of course, a relatively low-budget show for Broadway, particularly for a musical. But it is rare nonetheless that a show recoups so quickly, more so since the house is small (805 seats) and attendance has dropped to about three-quarters capacity for the last several weeks.
The "Falsettos" star, Michael Rupert, will be vacationing from Nov. 5 through Nov. 21. His central role as Marvin will be taken by Adrian Zmed, who will subsequently play Marvin in the national tour, which begins in Fort Lauderdale on Dec. 1.
 
A 'Guy' Takes a Vacation
 Speaking of replacements, from Nov. 9 through Dec. 22, Adam Arkin will fill in for Nathan Lane/ Detroit in "Guys and Dolls," while Mr. Lane films "Life With Mikey," with Michael J. Fox, for Touchstone Pictures.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 3, 1992, Tuesday

HEADLINE: Politics of the Trade Pact: A Lawmaker Who Is Torn

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BALTIMORE, Oct. 26

 BODY:
In the neighborhoods of Maryland's Third Congressional District, where Barry Levinson's movies and Anne Tyler's novels are set, people talk about lacrosse games and crab cakes and crime and health care and, especially this week, whether Baltimore is getting a raw deal from the National Football League.
But in the Washington office of Representative Benjamin L. Cardin, the Congressman from the Third District, the talk is of little else but the North American Free Trade Agreement. Labor leaders business executives, professional lobbyists, Cabinet members and even other members of Congress, all wanting to make the case for or against the trade measure, line up daily in his waiting room like ants at a sugar bowl. 
 "This is a very active issue in terms of lobbying, more active than any other issue since I've been in Congress," said Mr. Cardin, a Democrat in his fourth term.
It makes him very uncomfortable, he says. If he votes for the agreement, he offends the labor movement, which for years has been a foundation of his political strength. If he votes against it, he will be bucking President Clinton, to whom he is devoted.
He thinks both sides have vastly exaggerated the potential benefits and drawbacks of expanded trade with Mexico. So his decision on how to vote will ultimately be a political one.
Not quirky enough to be a character in a Tyler novel and too polished for a Levinson movie, Mr. Cardin is nonetheless a Baltimorean through and through. Born and reared in a neighborhood northwest of downtown, he was first elected to the Maryland legislature when he was 23 years old and still in law school. Now 50, he has been in politics and represented many of the same constituents ever since.
He is an unusually serious politician, a member of the Ways and Means Committee who works to master complicated legislation and who would much rather be devoting his time to health care, which is what he says his constituents truly care about.
 
Stuck With Trade
 But that will be impossible, the Congressman says, until after mid-November, when Congress votes on the trade accord, which would gradually lift trade barriers between the United States, Mexico and Canada. Politically, it is the most important vote facing him, Congress and President Clinton the rest of this year. The outcome will probably hinge on the votes of a couple of dozen Democrats like him who have not made up their minds on the issue.
And while most of his constituents do not know Nafta from a cheese spread, the ones who come to his office to lobby him view the agreement as a matter of life and death.
"This isn't a moral issue for me an issue of conscience," Mr. Cardin said. "I'm going to vote for it or against it depending on what I decide it means for the jobs in my district."
His district is in many respects a microcosm of the country as far as international trade is concerned. Extending from Baltimore's newly developed Inner Harbor, the district takes in two wide strips of the city and some of the northwest and southwest suburbs.
It has large Bethlehem Steel, General Motors and Westinghouse factories and a Domino Sugar refinery. It has the nonunion plants of national consumer-products companies like Procter & Gamble and McCormick, the spice company, that are eager to sell their wares in Mexico. The district also has strong unions, which fear losing jobs to Mexico. It has the vestiges of a once flowering textile industry. And it has one of the largest ports on the Atlantic Seaboard, which stands to benefit from imports and exports alike.
To help him decide how to vote, Mr. Cardin's staff is trying to evaluate how the various industries in the region might fare under the trade agreement.
 
'Faces of the Employed'
 A preliminary analysis shows that jobs would clearly be lost in the garment industry. Financial services companies like USF&G, the insurance conglomerate whose headquarters are in the Third District, would probably not be affected one way or another. Procter & Gamble, which makes Dawn, Ivory and Joy detergents in Baltimore, does not export from this plant to Mexico, so there should be no effect on jobs.
But the staff found that the steel products made in Baltimore would probably find a market in Mexico and that General Motors ought to be able to sell in Mexico many more of the commercial vans it builds at its plant on Broening Highway.
For a politician like Mr. Cardin, this kind of cold analysis may be helpful but it is generally not decisive. Even if he decides that more new jobs would be created than lost in his district as a result of the trade agreement, he says, he still may vote against it.
"I see the faces of the employed people who are vulnerable," he said. "I don't see the faces of those who will gain jobs. They don't even know who they are. Gaining jobs is just much more uncertain."
For anyone following Mr. Cardin around this week and watching the lobbying, one point is clear: If his decision boils down to who has presented the best case, the unions will win hands down.
Take, for example, Carmen Papale, the Baltimore regional director of the Amalgamated Clothing and Textile Workers. In a session in Mr. Cardin's office this week, the first sentence out of Mr. Papale's mouth was, "All these years, you've been great with us."
Then he said: "This is a very serious issue. This is such a critical issue. This Nafta is so important to us."
And then he mentioned an unsubstantiated rumor that if the trade agreement is approved, London Fog might move 500 jobs and its Maryland plants to Mexico.
Mr. Cardin's next meeting was with Rodney Trump, president of the Baltimore local of the United Automobile Workers. It is much more likely, Mr. Trump said, that G.M. will close its plant in Baltimore, where 3,500 people work, and move to Mexico than it is that Mexicans will buy the commercial vans assembled in Baltimore. "Mexicans," Mr. Trump said, "don't make enough money to buy the headlight of my Chevy Cavalier."
Mr. Cardin was blunt. He told the union officials that two factors weighed against them.
First, he said: "I want to support the President. I worked hard for him, and he's the person I want in there."
Second, he said, even without the trade pact, companies like London Fog and General Motors might move.
But afterward, Mr. Cardin said he had been very impressed. "They told me directly what they thought it would mean for Baltimore, for our jobs," he said. "We haven't been able to pin the companies down on that."
Indeed, General Motors and Bethlehem Steel, for example, sent Washington lobbyists instead of executives from their Baltimore plants to see Mr. Cardin, and the pitch they made was about how good the agreement would be for their companies nationwide, not what it would do for Baltimore.
The exception to this rule about ineffective corporate lobbying has been Bailey A. Thomas, the chairman of McCormick.
The company, which has 2,000 employees in the Baltimore area, is selling $150 million worth of spices and seasonings in Mexico this year, and the market is growing by as much as 20 percent a year. Much of what the company sells in Mexico is produced there.
 
Administration Unpersuasive
 But if Mexican tariffs are cut, Mr. Thomas told the Congressman, a significant portion would probably be produced in the United States, some of it in Baltimore, and shipped to Mexico.
Over time, Mr. Thomas said, manufacturing jobs at the Baltimore plants, which pay $9 to $12 an hour, could be expected to increase by 10 percent because of additional sales to Mexico.
That, Mr. Cardin said, is the kind of information he is looking for.
Strangely, the Clinton Administration has also been generally unpersuasive, Mr. Cardin said. He recalled, for instance, that when Laura D'Andrea Tyson, head of the Council of Economic Advisers, came by, he asked her for a historical example of a free-trade agreement between countries with economies as disparate as those of the United States and Mexico. (Mexico's economy is one-twentieth the size of this country's -- about the size of the economy of Southern California.)
According to Mr. Cardin, Ms. Tyson replied that no such case existed but that she saw an analogy in the way the North and South in the United States both prospered after Northern industry moved South beginning in the 1920's.
Mr. Cardin said after the meeting that Ms. Tyson seemed to be making her opponents' point. While, say, Massachusetts as a whole surely prospered, some towns in the state never recovered after their mills closed.
That is his dilemma, the Congressman said. Because he really wants to support Bill Clinton.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Representative Benjamin L. Cardin, left, discussing the free trade agreement at a meeting on Wednesday with Carmen Papale, center, and Bruce Dunton of the Amalgamated Clothing and Textile Workers Union. (Mike Geissinger for The New York Times) (pg. 7)

HEADLINE: Books of The Times;
Selling What America Should Keep

BYLINE: By FRANK GIBNEY; Frank Gibney, the president of the Pacific Basin Institute and vice chairman of Encyclopaedia Britannica's Board of Editors, has written widely on Japan and on Asian economies. His most recent book is "The Pacific Century."

 BODY:

Selling Our Security
The Erosion of America's Assets
By Martin and Susan J. Tolchin
427 pages. Alfred A. Knopf. $25.
 This is an important and an intensely disturbing book. The authors are Martin Tolchin, a longtime Washington correspondent for The New York Times, and Susan J. Tolchin, a public administration professor at George Washington University. The Tolchins have set out to show how the United States, in the name of global free enterprise, neo-classical laissez-faire economics and a mythical level playing field, has bartered its economic future for the temporary lure of foreign investments and the illusion that strategic industries or investment in technology don't really matter, as long as you can buy what you need somewhere in the world, at the moment. 
 "None of America's major trading partners," they write, "subscribe to the U.S. vision of free trade. None regard technology with a cavalier notion that ownership doesn't matter -- unless they are the owners. Instead, each nurtures technologies it deems vital to its economic competitiveness in the 21st century."
With this as their starting point, the Tolchins go on to show first how American policy makers' trust in a global economy -- still piously believed to be based on the laws of comparative advantage -- has sacrificed national security considerations to the point where much of our leading technology in the Persian Gulf war, for example, was dependent on foreign suppliers. But the problem transcends mere defense concerns. In the post-cold war world, it is almost impossible to disentangle a nation's security from a strong and technology-oriented economy, whether or not the connection with actual defense needs is a direct one.
Despite the constant talk about trade friction, the United States remains the one country that as a matter of policy separates trade and investments; thus foreign investment -- as distinct from trade -- is not scrutinized for any negative effects it may have. On the contrary, during the Reagan and Bush Administrations, with the horrendous deficit piling up, the country became increasingly dependent on foreign investment. Foreign buyers were given a constant walk-in tour of what the Tolchins call America's "casino economy."
By contrast, American corporations and big-time investors were notably laggard in putting money in good, innovative technology companies, which require what the Tolchins call "patient capital." (That is to say, you don't see a return on your money until a long way down the road.) Nor did the Government assist them. This contrasts with the behavior of nations like Japan -- and for that matter, the countries of the European Economic Community -- whose Governments have clear ideas of where their national security lies and how broad that definition is today.
Actually from the 1950's and 60's on, Americans thought of defense in very broad terms. Building roads, improving education and developing technology were all part of building up the national defense posture. But with the 80's, the authors point out, the definition of what was actually security shrank to a narrow, strictly military perimeter. Even in military applications, the idea of keeping a certain amount of technological productivity in this country was often sacrificed to a totally uncritical definition of global interdependence.
The ideas in this book are by no means new. The argument over whether this country should have an industrial policy -- "the notorious I-phrase" that became anathema to the Bush and Reagan White House -- has been going on for a long time. Many people have been sounding the alarm bell about the erosion of American technological advantage in industry after industry. The drift intensified through the 80's and into the 90's, as both Presidents Reagan and Bush persistently refused to avail themselves of Congressional safeguards adopted to try to keep at least part of American technology at home and intact. All this is part of the national debate.
The particular value of this book, however, lies in the abundance of fact and detail with which the authors support their thesis. Starting with the almost successful attempt of a British corporate raider to hijack the Goodyear Company, and the sale to a German group of the last American silicon wafer maker in the commercial market, they have compiled a case history of technologies and companies -- robotics, digital imaging, supercomputers and complex machine tools, among them -- where American advantage and security have been lost to what they call the "passive globalism" of the last two Administrations.
They summarize: "The ease of acquiring assets in a nation that refuses to differentiate between potato chips and computer chips virtually invites the rest of the world to snap up its choice industries."
The loss of irreplaceable assets is facilitated not only by the hordes of foreign-employed lobbyists in Washington -- many of them only recently resigned from the United States Government -- but also by the international irrelevance of so many United States laws, antitrust legislation in particular, which were created originally to regulate a game with only American players. Another contributing factor is a tax code that favors spenders, rather than savers. Worst of all is a business philosophy that seems to hold that government must always be the enemy.
The authors' suggestions for reform range far and wide. They include a kind of Export-Import Bank for technology to provide a reservoir of patient capital and an Endangered Technologies Act. Why not, they argue, provide critical technology the same protection we give the spotted owl and the snail darter?
They urge a greater pooling of resources in research consortiums; Japan, they note, has 75 of them going. Above all, they warn us that cherished ideals of American pluralism and a reliance on Adam Smith's marketplace no longer work in a constantly changing global climate.
"Participation in the global economy," they conclude, "shouldn't mean wholesale capitulation on issues that will determine the nation's national security and economic health. If a nation can't collect its own data, protect its national security and promote its economic interests without foreign intervention, it begins to look more and more like the original colonial government its forebears fought so hard to overturn."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Democrats -- Clinton and Bush Compete to Be Champion of Change;
Democrat Fights Perceptions of Bush Gain

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SPRINGFIELD, Ohio, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Battling the impression that President Bush is closing on him, Gov. Bill Clinton returned today to the basics of his strategy, playing to the interests of the vast middle class of Americans who decide every Presidential race.
Mr. Clinton and his strategists said Mr. Bush had moved closer, as they have predicted he would. But Mr. Clinton's poll taker and several other aides said today that their own surveys showed Mr. Clinton's slippage had stopped, and that he still retained a seven-point lead in their own surveys. 
 Arguing this and working to dispel the perception that Mr. Bush is gaining on Mr. Clinton, the Democratic campaign tried to renew its focus on the message that got it to this point.
 
'The Economy, Stupid'
 Mr. Clinton and his aides have said from the very beginning that those interests can be summed up in three phrases, which are on a sign in the campaign's headquarters: "Change vs. more of the same. The economy, stupid. Don't forget health care."
Campaigning in the state of almost perpetual motion that characterizes the Democratic candidate's final campaign days, Mr. Clinton made a mantra of those points today, in speeches streamlined to include only one other critical element: Don't believe anything George Bush says.
In selling that latter point, Mr. Clinton received a gift today with the disclosure of a memorandum written in 1986 by the Secretary of Defense at the time, Caspar W. Weinberger, in which Mr. Weinberger wrote that Mr. Bush, then the Vice President, had, in an Administration meeting, supported selling arms to Iran in exchange for the freeing of Americans held hostage by Iranian-backed terrorists in Lebanon.
"Today's disclosure that President Bush knew and approved of the arms for hostages deal with Iran not only directly contradicts the President's claims, it diminishes the credibility of the Presidency," Mr. Clinton said in a hastily called news conference this afternoon at a hotel in Pittsburgh.
Not surprisingly, Mr. Clinton used the disclosure to try to turn against Mr. Bush the questions of honesty and character the President has so assiduously raised about his adversary.
"President Bush says this election is about trust and character and judgment," Mr. Clinton said. "He has seriously called into question those issues and now has to answer your questions on all."
While Mr. Clinton took care to avoid seeming pleased about the disclosure of the Weinberger memo, contained in an indictment by a Federal grand jury under the direction of the Iran-contra special prosecutor, his aides greeted the news with open excitement.
The news came on a day that began with Mr. Clinton in a notably upbeat mood. Chatting with staff members and reporters aboard his jetliner this morning, the candidate was in such an apparently good mood that he even joined in joking about his weight.
Mr. Clinton's mood seemed to reflect a sense within the campaign that his slippage in popular support had stopped, at least for now, with the Democrat still well ahead of Mr. Bush.
Stan Greenberg, Mr. Clinton's poll taker, said today that the campaign's national and state-by-state surveys of 1,200 and 10,000 people respectively both showed the race between Mr. Clinton and Mr. Bush had stabilized on Wednesday and Thursday, with Mr. Clinton retaining a lead of seven points among respondents saying there was more than a 50 percent chance they would vote.
 
Last-Minute, Nonstop Tour
 While such a claim serves the campaign's interest in stemming a perception that Mr. Clinton was slipping and Mr. Bush gaining in popular support, the figure cited by the Clinton campaign does jibe with many of the independent polls published in the last two days.
With Mr. Clinton's lead seemingly intact, but -- in a year marked by extreme volatility among voters -- still fragile, the campaign has decided to try the safe strategy of focusing attention on the theme of middle-class oriented economic change that has brought the Democrats this far, while still keeping a heavy fire on Mr. Bush.
Clinton campaign strategists said this lean mixture would be the essential message Mr. Clinton would carry in his last campaign tour, a nearly nonstop swing that will last through the Election Day morning, taking the candidate in repeated visits to some of the most important contested states: Pennsylvania, Ohio, Michigan, Georgia, Missouri, Wisconsin, Kentucky Iowa, Illinois and New Mexico.
Mr. Clinton delivered that message to thousands of people gathered under a light rain in a downtown square in Pittsburgh, and an evening address in this farming city.
Calling himself the "one person in this race who really comes from the great American middle class and understands the American dream," Mr. Clinton opened with a promise to provide "basic health care to all Americans," moved swiftly on to the allure of "change" and then delved into his many and varied promises of a better, richer life for the middle class.
Woven all through, like barbed wire sewn into a tapestry, were deftly cutting observations, each glinting shard designed to draw just a little blood from Mr. Clinton's adversaries.
Fighting against slippage in his lead over Mr. Bush, Mr. Clinton has in recent days demonstrated a mastery of this sort of folksy thrust, in the manner of Will Rogers with a stiletto. He used it in Pittsburgh to effect a contrast with the edgy tone of Mr. Bush's most recent attacks on him, while still sticking to the all-important economic theme.
Mr. Clinton recalled, in lightly chuckling tones meant to represent amused disbelief, Mr. Bush's remark on Thursday that Mr. Clinton and his running mate, Senator Al Gore, were crazy.
"Now, let me tell you something," Mr. Clinton told the crowd in Pittsburgh. "I'll tell you what I think is crazy. I think crazy is unemployment going up and income going down. It's 100,000 Americans a month losing their health insurance."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Republicans -- Clinton and Bush Compete to Be Champion of Change;
President Paints Rival Leading U.S. Astray

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Having battered Bill Clinton with assaults on his platform and character, President Bush tried today to challenge Mr. Clinton's claim to be the candidate of change.
Speaking in Nashville early today, the President argued that the nation would change no matter who leads it. "The real question," he said, "is not who is for change, but whose changes will make your life better and your world safer." 
 On that score, Mr. Bush argued, Mr. Clinton seeks to take the nation back to a philosophy of bigger and more powerful government that American voters, and much of the developed world, have rejected in the last dozen years.
 
An 'Active Conservative'
 By contrast, the President cast himself as an "activist conservative" who believes that individuals, not government, are the best source of economic growth and innovation.
The argument is the core of Mr. Bush's plea for a second term in office, and it played well with his audience, a convention of Kentucky Fried Chicken store owners who are by nature small business owners and risk-takers.
"It doesn't make sense that restaurant owners will somehow get richer by giving more of their money to the I.R.S.," Mr. Bush said to a burst of applause. "And at a time when every other institution is decentralizing power, why turn back to a central bureaucracy in Washington?"
The speech amounted to a fresh wrapping for the message the White House has pounded relentlessly all week: that Mr. Clinton is a danger to the nation's economy and its character.
 
Trying to Change Minds
 Mr. Bush was also seeking to provide to voters what his campaign has so far lacked: affirmative reasons for wavering voters to switch from Mr. Clinton to the President. Mr. Bush's strategists are running positive commercials about the President's second-term agenda in the campaign's final days, dovetailing with negative advertising attacking Mr. Clinton.
But for the message to succeed, voters will need to put aside their feelings about the economic record of the President's first term, something many of them seem unwilling to do so far, and by midday Mr. Bush was back on the attack circuit at a sparsely attended rally in suburban St. Louis.
Bush aides continued to say today that their candidate was closing on Mr. Clinton, a view underscored by the stop in Tennessee, the home state of Mr. Clinton's running mate, Senator Al Gore. As Mr. Bush arrived here, a new Mason Dixon Poll concluded that the two men were in a statistical tie here: Mr. Clinton had a five-percentage-point lead, but that was within the poll's margin of sampling error.
The aides disclosed more of a campaign schedule that will take Mr. Bush in the last four days to Midwestern and Eastern states, where he trails Mr. Clinton but where Bush strategists say the President is gaining ground.
After a daylong train trip through Wisconsin on Saturday, the President will fly to at least three battleground states on Sunday: Michigan, New Jersey and Connecticut.
Mr. Bush's shifting plans call for him to barnstorm on election eve through 14 airport rallies at undisclosed sites. The campaign is scheduled to end in Houston on Monday night.
The schedule "is not something you normally do in the dying days of a campaign," Mr. Bush's campaign chairman, Robert M. Teeter, said in an interview on Thursday. "But when you're behind and you need 270 electoral votes, you go where you think you can get the votes."
Vice President Dan Quayle touched down today in four Western and central states. In Denver, he pressed the assault on Mr. Clinton's character by recalling Democratic opposition to Senator John Tower's confirmation as Secretary of Defense in 1989.
"Sam Nunn," Mr. Quayle said, "who led the opposition to Senator Tower, said that in three cases, including 'behavior toward women, Senator Tower falls short of the standards which must be demanded of those who would bear the awesome responsibilities of this position.' " Mr. Quayle added: "Does Bill Clinton pass the Tower test?"
 Mr. Bush's gains in recent days seemed to generate a peculiar new version of the political expectations game, in which candidates try to shape expectations of victory in ways that will best motivate voters and campaign workers.
 
A Touch of Realism
 Even as they professed confidence in a last-minute surge of support, Bush aides sought to portray a sober realism about their chances, arguing that come-from-behind victories are needed in many large states.
Campaigning in recent days, Mr. Bush has used the word "change" almost as a mocking refrain against his rival's calls for higher taxes and greater Federal spending.
"Change -- Governor Clinton and Ozone, they just keep talking about change, change, change," Mr. Bush said on Thursday in Detroit, using his pet name for Mr. Gore. "That's all you will have left in your pocket if Governor Clinton becomes President."
In Nashville today, Mr. Bush treated the topic more seriously, seeking to seize the issue for himself.
"Over the past four years, we have seen change of almost Biblical proportion," he said. "Always, the shadow of the cold war lingered right outside our window. You talk change -- well, all that has changed." Since Mr. Bush took office, the Soviet Union collapsed and the East moved toward democracy and a free-market.
"But here's the irony: at the very moment when the rest of the world is moving my way, my opponent, Governor Clinton, wants us to move the old way," he said.
 
Assails Clinton AIDS Plan
 As one example, he took aim at Mr. Clinton's call on Thursday for more spending and a new Federal official to oversee the battle against AIDS. Noting that the Government already spends more on AIDS than on any other ailment, Mr. Bush suggested that Mr. Clinton favors a reflexive answer to a problem with no easy solution.
"AIDS is a national tragedy, but we don't need a bureaucratic czar in our nation's capital," he said. "A President has to set priorities, because it's your money that we're talking about."
The President has come under sharp attack from AIDS activists and experts for what they say is inattention to the epidemic and a refusal to risk political problems by trying policies like sex education.
Later, in St. Louis, Mr. Bush opened a rally by accepting the endorsement of Maryland's Democratic Governor, William Donald Schaffer. Mr. Schaffer said his decision had been difficult, but that he "had to consider what is truly best for my country."
Besides, the ebullient Governor whooped at one point: "He's an honest guy! He's my friend! He cares about people!"

HEADLINE: Delay by Yeltsin In Baltic Pullout Irritates the U.S.

BYLINE: By CELESTINE BOHLEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Oct. 30

 BODY:
A day after President Boris N. Yeltsin suspended a pullout of Russian troops, the independent Baltic states reacted with anger and dismay today and new questions were raised in the West regarding aid to Moscow and Russia's long-term intentions in the Baltics.
A spokesman from NATO headquarters in Brussels urged Russia not to delay the pullout, despite the logistical difficulties of housing returning troops. 
 "This is a matter of concern," the spokesman said of the decree, issued on Thursday, which said withdrawals would resume only after Latvia, Estonia and Lithuania signed agreements providing "social guarantees" for Russian troops on their territory.
 
The Russian View
 At a news conference today in Moscow, Deputy Foreign Minister Vitaly I. Churkin denied "linkage" between the pullout and the protection of the human rights of Russians in the Baltics. But he said that Russia's view is: "the better the human rights situation, the easier it will be for us to conclude agreements."
In Washington, the State Department called the decree "disappointing" and said the delay could jeopardize American aid to Moscow.
The bill makes all aid except food and medical relief contingent on the President's being able to report to Congress that the pullout is progressing.
In the Baltics, the decree was widely seen as an attempt to appease both Russia's unhappy officer corps and a growing nationalist opposition that has made an issue of the treatment of ethnic Russians in the Baltic states, particularly Latvia and Estonia.
With a Congress of People's Deputies, Russia's highest legislative body, scheduled to convene in December, Mr. Yeltsin has been marshaling his forces, striking out against nationalists with one hand, and now, commandeering one of their pet issues with the other.
Moscow's tough new stance, which effectively suspends a recent agreement with Lithuania, is likely to raise suspicions in the West that Russia has retained some Soviet habits, including a peremptory negotiating style and a high priority for military interests.
The Russian Deputy Defense Minister, Boris Gromov, just back from a tour of installations in the Baltics, told a news conference today that the pullout could take from "seven to eight years," because of a lack of fresh recruits to help officers dismantle the Russian bases and equipment. Mr. Yeltsin had previously set a target of 1994.
The Latvian Deputy President, Andrejs Krastins, said in Riga today that the decree was a symptom of Moscow's uncertain political climate.
"It reflects the instability of power in Russia," he said. "Russia's position changes every day with every twist and turn of its own political situation."
The Estonian President, Lennart Meri, told the Interfax news agency that the question should be put before the Conference on Security and Cooperation in Europe.
 
A Diplomatic Turn
 A week ago, the Russian Defense Minister, Pavel Grachev, said the pullout of troops, now said by Russian officials to number 40,000 in the Baltics, not including family members, would be suspended because of a shortage of housing in Russia for returning officers.
Lieutenant General Gromov said 67 percent of personnel and 73 percent of equipment had already been brought back from Germany, and that the withdrawal from Mongolia had been completed. Pullouts from Georgia, Moldova and Tajikistan are being planned, he said.
But Mr. Yelstin's decree cited "profound concern over numerous infringements of rights of the Russian-speaking populations in the Baltic countries." The Russian President set a three-day deadline for the Baltic states to agree to provide "social guarantees" for Russian troops and said economic agreements with Russia would be contingent on their fulfillment.
This kind of tough language suggested a new turn for Russian diplomacy, as Moscow seeks to defend its own strategic interests against the claims of other former Soviet republics.
 
The Lithuanian Exception
 The Russian Foreign Ministry has recently stepped up its protests about the treatment of ethnic Russians living in Estonia and Latvia, where they make up more than 40 percent of the population. In both countries, families of people who moved there after the Soviet takeover of 1940 must pass special language and residency requirements before being granted citizenship and the right to vote. This has proven particularly difficult for Russians, many of whom have lived in the Baltic states for years, because during the Soviet times, Russian was a kind of lingua franca, and learning Estonian or Latvian would be a formidable task.
The decree came as a particular blow to Lithuania where Russians, who make up less than 9 percent of the population, were granted automatic citizenship rights after independence last year. The decree did not differentiate among the Baltic countries.
Yet on Sept. 8, partly as result of its more liberal policies, Lithuania signed an agreement with Russia that set an Aug. 31, 1993, deadline for the departure of the last Russian soldier from Lithuanian territory.
"The treaty became valid as soon as it was signed, and the Russians started to fulfill it immediately," said Audyus Azhubanis, press secretary to the Lithuanian President, Vytautus Landsbergis. "We cannot understand why they announced they will stop this process."
 
The Signature Question
 In Moscow today, Mr. Churkin noted that an inter-state agreement had not yet been signed by Mr. Yeltsin and Mr. Landsbergis -- even though both were present at the Kremlin signing ceremonies on Sept. 8. At the time, Mr. Yeltsin's press secretary said an inter-state agreement would be signed by within a month.
Furthermore, Mr. Azhubanis said that in preparation for the Sept. 8 ceremony, both sides had agreed on a document providing "social guarantees" for the departing Russian troops. But, he said, at the last minute, the Russian side had withheld its signature.
"Now it looks as though Russia created this misunderstanding, because they do not want to withdraw their troops," Mr. Azhubanis said. "They want to exploit the issue of social guarantees."
But he said the victory of former Communists in the first round of Lithuania's parliamentary elections on Sunday may have also played a role. The Democratic Labor Party, headed by Lithuania's former party leader, Algirdas Brazauskas, has said it wants to improve relations with Moscow, and some analysts say Russia may have decided that it can win more time for the pullout if Mr. Brazauskas replaces Mr. Landsbergis as head of the Parliament after run-off elections on Nov. 8.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
U.S. Aid Imperiled
 WASHINGTON, Oct. 30 (Special to The New York Times) -- Russia's indefinite postponement of the withdrawal of troops from the Baltic states has placed United States economic assistance to Moscow in peril, a State Department spokesman said today.
The spokesman, Joseph Snyder, pointed out that a foreign aid bill signed into law by President Bush on Oct. 6 stipulates that before Russia may receive its portion of $417 million approved for the 15 republics of the former Soviet Union, it must make "substantial progress" toward completing a timetable for a Baltic pullout.
Mr. Snyder said the Administration had "indicated our concern" to Russia. "We are disappointed by this announcement, but hope the problems that have arisen can be resolved expeditiously and by mutual agreement," he said
He said: "We have welcomed earlier statements that Russian troops will be withdrawn from the Baltics and expect that the Government of Russia will fulfill its political commitment to do so. We want to see agreements on troop withdrawals completed as soon as possible."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: News Analysis;
A Battle of the Negatives

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
As the 1992 campaign enters its final weekend, President Bush has grasped for the offensive with an attack on Gov. Bill Clinton's credibility that clearly surprised the Democrats with its ferocity and that shows no sign of abating.
Mr. Clinton, who had been calling the shots for months, suddenly found himself in a position no front-runner wants -- slightly offstride and struggling to turn the debate back to issues more favorable to him. 
 And although this tortuous race remains unsettled, this much seems likely about the final going: Rather than ending on a high note, more nastiness is in store.
Mr. Bush's behavior on the stump in recent days was vivid evidence of what Republican strategists were saying in private: The Bush camp decided that the only thing that worked was to get negative and, for the most part, they planned to stay negative despite the obvious risks of this strategy.
New polls suggest some narrowing of the race, but they also show that Mr. Bush's own unfavorable ratings have changed little, despite the boost he got this week from new signs of economic growth that the President hailed at every campaign stop.
Mr. Bush is accusing Mr. Clinton of flip-flopping, not having any principles and not helping his constituents, but the President is in political trouble because many voters have reached those same conclusions about him.
 
Questions and Risks
 The President is also hammering Mr. Clinton on what he calls a "pattern of deception" about his draft record and other issues. But the new questions raised today about Mr. Bush's involvement in the Iran-contra affair only underscored the risk that many voters will decide that he has shown the same pattern -- raising taxes after pledging not to do so and following conciliatory policies toward Iraq before the Persian Gulf war.
This battle of the negatives comes in a year in which voters got fed up with gutter politics and were examining policy papers and getting unfiltered television views of the candidates. But the dynamics of the Presidential campaign and dozens of Congressional races across the country have made it clear that attack politics still works.
"Everyone wants to say that stuff doesn't work, but the fact is that everywhere in the country I've gone on the attack, I've made progress," said Eddie Mahe, who handles Congressional campaigns for Republican candidates.
In the Presidential race Mr. Bush's campaign oratory and paid advertising have virtually abandoned any positive message about his own ideas. By this morning, after Mr. Bush called Mr. Clinton and his running mate, Senator Al Gore, "bozos," even Barbara Bush seemed a bit taken aback by the tone of the attack.
 
'In a Heated Moment'
 "That wasn't so nice," she said in a CBS News interview. "I'm surprised George said that. But maybe that was in a heated moment."
But Mrs. Bush made it clear she was not going to ask her husband to stop the offensive. "No," she said. "Are you kidding? I certainly am not."
The Clinton team moved today to brush back Mr. Bush and turn the debate away from Mr. Clinton's record and his character and back to Mr. Bush's record.
But Mr. Clinton's advisers acknowledge that the Bush attack has been damaging. The Democratic nominee, who has long battled the image of an accommodating politician and has, indeed, offered conflicting stories about his past, will also have to stay on the attack or risk having Mr. Bush's charges stick.
"We've always said you hit back and then move on with a counterpunch," said George Stephanopoulos, Mr. Clinton's communications director. "That's what we're doing. All of our media that Bush is going to face this weekend is on the economy and his record."
 
Failed to React Quickly
 This is clearly not what the Democrats planned as recently as a week ago, when Mr. Clinton was concentrating on looking like a winner.
The Democrats distinguished themselves by their ability to forecast an opponent's moves and then react quickly. But they seem to have failed to see the attack on waffling and trust coming, or at least underestimated its power and waited too long to respond.
At the same time, Mr. Clinton seems to be having difficulty finding ways to sharpen a political message that once seemed fresh but is now familiar to many voters.
"He has to phrase the choice for the voter again and go back to the message that worked, which was an economic-based message and the performance of the President," said Brian Lunde, a Democratic political consultant.
What makes the Clinton team's performance look sluggish in the past few days is that it was no secret that Mr. Bush was going to attack in the final going. Not only had he himself said that publicly and often, but the attack actually had been going on for some time. It only escalated and seemed to take hold in recent days, helped along by news coverage of Mr. Bush's claim that the race is turning in his favor.
The campaign against Mr. Clinton's record in Arkansas should have been the least surprising of all to the Democrats. The attempt to paint Arkansas as a low-wage, high-crime wasteland replays the 1988 campaign against Gov. Michael S. Dukakis's "Massachusetts miracle," and Mr. Clinton's aides consider themselves the nation's leading experts on not repeating the mistakes of the 1988 campaign.
"Clinton's people should perhaps have dealt with those problems more forcefully early in the campaign," Mr. Lunde said. "But what could he do? Did he want to raise the subject of taxes and trust? Those were the two weak links in the chain."
Mr. Bush's advisers believe the President has no choice but to keep up a relentless attack, but some Republicans questioned whether the tone was too strident and the attack too diffuse.
"It's the old spaghetti theory -- take everything you have and throw it at Clinton, and whatever sticks to the wall is good," said James Innocenzi, a Republican advertising consultant. "It's got Bush to this point, but I don't know for sure that it's going to take Bush much farther."
Mr. Mahe said the President was showing signs of his habit of becoming over-excited in his campaign oratory.
"I was feeling pretty good up until I heard about yesterday's performance," Mr. Mahe said. "He's starting to be goofy again. It's too much. Hopefully, Jim Baker or Barbara took him to the back of the plane, got a hairbrush, patted him on the tailbone and told him to settle down a bit."

HEADLINE: Religious Right Intensifies Campaign for Bush

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ATLANTA, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Little noticed since the Republican Convention in Houston, the religious right is mounting a sophisticated, high-intensity battle on behalf of President Bush that it claims can provide a margin of victory if the race continues to tighten.
Pat Robertson's Christian Coalition, by far the most powerful force, says it is planning to send out 40 million voter guides, which compare the three Presidential candidates' positions on issues like abortion, prayer in school, and homosexual rights. The group plans to distribute them in 246,000 churches by Election Day. On Sunday alone, 30 million voter guides will be put in churches ranging from evangelical to mainline Protestant to Catholic. 
 Mr. Robertson calls the distribution "the most comprehensive effort in the history of the country." He says well-organized evangelicals can make the difference in the Presidential race.
"The entire race will come down to who turns their people out to the polls," said Ralph Reed, executive director of 350,000-member Christian Coalition, founded in 1989 from the remnants of Mr. Robertson's Presidential campaign. "It will be the union boiler rooms and the black churches versus the evangelicals, and I think we've got a very good chance of getting our people out. I think there's going to be a lot of surprised faces on election night."
Mr. Reed said the group was working on state and local races as well as the Presidential race. But at the Presidential level the issue is not just the election, but the role that conservatives and the religious right will play in the party afterward.
The tone of the activities varies widely.
A leaflet put out by another coalition of Christian conservatives, including Operation Rescue's leader, Randall Terry, begins: "Christians beware, to vote for Bill Clinton is to sin against God." A full-page newspaper advertisement by the same group in today's USA Today raises issues of abortion, homosexual rights and condom distribution in schools and concludes: "The Bible warns us not to follow another man in his sin nor help him promote sin -- lest God chasten us. How then can we vote for Bill Clinton?" The advertisement is to run in 157 more papers this weekend.
A group of Protestant, Jewish and Catholic religious leaders denounced the ad this week, terming it "a piece of spiritual arrogance and political extremism that has no place in our public discourse."
Mr. Robertson said language referring to a voting choice as sinful was "totally inappropriate." He said a vote for Mr. Clinton would be "stupid" rather than sinful. "Billy Sunday said God can forgive sin, but stupid is forever," he said referring to a famous evangelist.
 
Morals Are Questioned
 But while citing economic issues as a reason to vote for Mr. Bush, he also questioned Mr. Clinton's morality.
"This man is supposed to be the role model for America," he said. "I just don't want somebody like that in office. George Bush is an outstanding church man, father, grandfather."
The religious right is hardly alone in mobilizing for the election. And some political experts voice skepticism about how effective it is likely to be.
"They can help in the South, where the President is most competitive already," said Kevin Phillips, a Republican political analyst. "But usually in the northern states they provoke more in a counter-reaction than they give you in a direct benefit. I think they've passed their peak."
But some experts say that despite the relative lack of attention the religious right has received since the Republican convention, the movement is mounting a sophisticated, mainstream effort that far surpasses what it did in 1988.
Lyman Kellstedt, a professor of political science at Wheaton College in Illinois, who studies the political impact of evangelicals, said the religious right's activity in the Presidential race is conventional grass-roots organizing, using churches as bases. He said white evangelicals in denominations including Baptists, Pentacostals, Assemblies of God and others, make up 23 percent of the adult population.
 
'Playing the Game'
 "Political parties don't play the role they used to play, so who's going to play this role in the future?" he said. "Well, here's a player, and they're playing the game the way it ought to be played.
"If you had to choose between the National Organization for Women and the Christian Coalition, just in terms of wanting to win, you'd choose the Christian Coalition."
It is clear that many conservatives, upset with the President's record and particularly his backtracking on taxes, remain uninspired about the Bush campaign. Some prominent conservatives gathered Friday in Washington in a session that was envisioned as a way to plot a future for conservatives after an expected Presidential defeat.
But religious and anti-abortion groups groups were working hard for the President long before his recent turnaround in the polls.
"George Bush defunded Planned Parenthood," said Mr. Reed of the Christian Coalition. "He robbed Planned Parenthood of anywhere between $130 to $150 million through the Title 10, the so-called gag rule veto. That was a very courageous veto."
Along with the Christian Coalition, conservative groups like the National Right-to-Life Committee, the Family Research Council, the American Freedom Coalition and others have been mobilizing members and highlighting the conservative issues in the race.
Most are prohibited by tax-exempt status from endorsing candidates. Typically, their involvement includes phone banks and literature to mobilize their constituencies. Especially popular this year are nominally non-partisan voter score cards with selected economic and social issues -- taxes, abortion, homosexual rights, condom distribution in schools, prayer in school -- intended to give a clear message about which candidate is favored.
For the religious right, a comeback by Mr. Bush would redeem not just his campaign but their role in it. Many conservatives like Pat Buchanan, who has campaigned for the President and Republican candidates for Congress, have argued that the news media distorted the impact of the conservative tilt to the Republican convention.
"The media have an interest in marginalizing us and in portraying us as an albatross around the President's neck," Mr. Reed said.
 
Role in the Future
 At stake beyond the election is how big a role conservatives and the religious right will play in the party's future. Paul Weyrich, a conservative organizer who has little enthusiasm for the Bush campaign, said he was not at all convinced conservatives would be in control of the party whether or not Mr. Bush won.
But many experts say that particularly if Mr. Bush loses, conservatives could fill a power vacuum in the party.
"Conservatives have an important influence now, and I think it will continue to grow," Mr. Robertson said. "The key is building a coalition just as it was built under Ronald Reagan."
Mr. Buchanan added: "Conservatism is at the core of the Republican Party just like liberalism is the great motivating force in the Democratic Party. To me, a liberal Republican is a contradiction in terms."
Asked if that applied to moderate Republicans, he said: "A moderate Republican is a liberal who doesn't like to call himself a liberal."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Independent;
Perot Aides Count on a 'Silent Majority'

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DALLAS, Oct. 30

 BODY:
As Ross Perot prepared to join his rivals for a final weekend of cross-country campaigning, his aides placed their faith today in what they maintain is a "silent majority" of newly registered voters whose views have not been reflected in public opinion polls.
Those polls continued to show Mr. Perot's support on the decline in the wake of a week of turmoil that began when he accused Republicans of dirty tricks. 
 Mr. Perot departs Saturday morning on a two-day campaign swing that includes rallies in Tampa, Fla.; Kansas City, Mo., and Long Beach and Santa Clara, Calif. He will wrap up his campaign with a rally in Reunion Arena in Dallas on Monday.
Even as Mr. Perot continued to assert not only that he could win, but also that he could win in a landslide, his campaign officials began speaking wistfully today about the legacy of the Perot movement and of the prospects for keeping it alive after Nov. 3. Their comments stood in stark contrast to those of President Bush and Gov. Bill Clinton and their strategists, who would never publicly acknowledge any consideration of defeat.
 
Looking Beyond Election
 "As long as this man has a breath to breathe he will be concerned about the welfare of this country and its people," said Orson G. Swindle 3d, the director of Mr. Perot's political organization, United We Stand, America. "It is beyond my imagination that after Nov. 3 he will have less enthusiasm for this cause. I would like to see him try it again if he doesn't succeed this time."
Mr. Swindle said he hoped to maintain United We Stand as an educational and advocacy group after the election, financed perhaps by membership dues instead of by Mr. Perot.
For Mr. Perot, far more than his rivals, the sliding poll numbers could have a devastating effect on voter psychology. In a political Catch-22, many undecided voters may not support him unless the polls indicate he has a chance of winning. But the polls cannot give him that chance unless voters are persuaded to take a risk on his long-shot candidacy.
"My family and friends would all vote for Perot if they thought he could win," said Thomas Walsh, a Woodbridge, N.J., man who showed up at a Perot rally last Sunday. "I had an attorney friend tell me when Perot hits 25 percent in the polls, he'll vote for Perot."
That seemed possible a week ago, when Mr. Perot was riding a wave of momentum generated by his performances in the Presidential debates and a series of groundbreaking paid television programs.
But a CBS News poll taken Tuesday through Thursday showed that only 15 percent of those questioned either supported or leaned toward Mr. Perot. It had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 3 percentage points. Other polls published in the last two days have yielded similar results, with all showing Mr. Perot dropping from last week's peak of about 20 percentage points.
Mr. Perot's aides tried to make the case at a morning news briefing that the polls were not registering his support among new voters.
"We feel that these newly registered voters are out there and are for the most part going to be voting for Ross Perot," said Sharon Holman, Mr. Perot's press secretary. "This is the silent majority that has never been tapped before."
But a survey released this week by the Committee for the Study of the American Electorate, a nonpartisan research group in Washington, warns against "premature euphoria" over reports about increased voter registration. Using partial data, it estimates that voter registration will be only 1 percentage point higher than in 1988.
Following the lead of her candidate, who has shown a new willingness to attack his opponents in recent days, Ms. Holman ridiculed Mr. Bush and Mr. Clinton for their recent round of name-calling. She said their behavior illustrated the causes of government gridlock.
"We've got 'liars' who can't work with 'bozos' and then we've got, what, the 'ozone' that's above it all," she said, using her fingers to place quotation marks around the candidates' favorite slurs against each other.
 
More Aggressive Tack
 There were indications that Mr. Perot would be more aggressive in taking on Mr. Clinton and Mr. Bush in his final round of television presentations on Sunday and Monday nights.
His Sunday program, which will be broadcast simultaneously on both ABC and NBC, is titled "Chicken Feathers, Voodoo Economics and the American Dream." Aides would not describe the program. But in recent days Mr. Perot has taken to describing Mr. Clinton as the Governor of a small state whose main industry is raising chickens while mocking Mr. Bush's adherence to the "voodoo" of trickle-down economics. He likes to offer himself as evidence of the "American Dream."
Mr. Perot is also scheduled to be interviewed on MTV, the cable music-video channel, on Sunday night.
Mr. Perot has bought a total of three and a half hours of television time on the networks on Sunday and Monday nights, including an hour on NBC on Monday that was sold for a stunning $1.8 million. He has spent a total of $24.1 million on advertising on the three networks and on Ted Turner's four cable networks -- CNN, TBS, TNT and Headline News. His 30-minute and 60-minute shows accounted for $8 million of that total.
By contrast, Mr. Bush spent $18.6 million and Mr. Clinton spent $9.9 million, according to network figures.

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

 NEW YORK CITY
 
 POLICE CHIEF ADDRESSES LOST TRUST
Faced with what he considers to be an alarming loss of credibility for the New York City Police Department, Commissioner Raymond W. Kelly lashed out at a general climate of "police bashing" in the city and at public officials "whose knee-jerk response is to criticize the police at every turn." In an effort to deal with problem of lost trust, the city agreed to Mr. Mr. Kelly's request to postpone the next police entry examination by six months to give recruiters the time to lure more minority applicants to the force. Page 1.
 
 OLD FOES JOIN TO FIGHT FOR CHANGE
Mayor Dinkins has found a surprising ally in his drive to remake the Board of Education. Robert F. Wagner Jr., a former president of the Board of Education, is putting together a panel of prominent New Yorkers to press for the abolition of the current board. Page 25.
 
 REGION
 
 BUDGET CHANGES
FOR SUFFOLK?
If county legislators have their way, Suffolk homeowners will get a 2.3 percent cut in their property taxes next year, instead of the 20 percent increase proposed by County Executive Robert J. Gaffney. But Mr. Gaffney, after reviewing a package of amendments that the legislators say will balance the 1993 budget, said that "it was not real" and that more than half the proposed changes were illegal. Page 26.
 
 A RETARDED WOMAN'S REPUTATION
The defense in the Glen Ridge sex-assault trial began cross-examination of a state psychologist, trying to reinforce its portrayal of a mildly retarded woman as an aggressor who craved sex. In the psychologist's view, the woman was compliant, easily victimized and mentally incapable of refusing. Page 26.
 
 FIGURING UP WHAT MATTERS
It meant something to be in a support group at last, talking openly with others who understand what it means to have a passion for polygonal curves, to be intimate with inverse functions. But ultimately the 4,000 people at a convention in Hartford seemed to return to one theme: that they, the math teachers of America, are perceived as misfits in a world that just does not know how to have fun with numbers. Page 26.
 
 A REPRIEVE FOR AMY FISHER
Two days after she was released from a psychiatric ward, Amy Fisher appeared in court and received good news: She will remain free on bail until her sentencing on Dec. 1 for the shooting of Mary Jo Buttafuoco. Page 26.
 
 LAST LAP IN CONNECTICUT SENATE RACE
In the final weekend of campaigning before the election, Senator Christopher J. Dodd offered handshakes and small talk through small towns today, while his opponent, Brook Johnson, met business leaders with an urgent message about turning the state's economy around. Page 28.
 
 TRENDS COLLIDE IN DISTRICT REMATCH
For the second time in two years, voters along Connecticut's middle coastline face the same choice for Congress: Rosa DeLauro, a Democrat who scraped her way to a 7,000-vote victory in 1990, or Tom Scott, a Republican and a former State Senator best known as the archfoe of the state income tax. HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
U.S. Closes First City Bancorp

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Federal banking authorities announced tonight that they had closed the First City Bancorporation of Houston, one of the largest banks in Texas, after finding that its two largest subsidiaries were insolvent.
Officials of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation said the Government's bank insurance fund would put up about $500 million to protect depositors in First City and its 20 bank subsidiaries, which had total assets of $8.8 billion, and prepare the bank for sale to private interests. 
 Regulators said the $500 million bank bailout would be the 10th-largest ever. The Government says the bailout will be financed by the bank insurance fund, which is financed by premiums paid by banks. But many experts expect that taxpayers will eventually be called on to contribute to future bailouts.
This is the second time in four years that the Government was forced to spend heavily to deal with First City's troubles. In 1988, the F.D.I.C. contributed an emergency infusion of $900 million toward a bailout plan in which a new owner took control of the bank.
 
104th Closing This Year
 The closing of First City highlights the continued problems of many banks even as the industry as a whole is enjoying record profits because of low interest rates. Tonight's move by the F.D.I.C. brought the number of banks closed so far this year to 104, with combined assets of $36.6 billion.
Federal regulators said all offices of the 20 banking subsidiaries of First City would be open on Monday, but as "bridge banks" under F.D.I.C. control. Federal regulators said they hoped to sell the subsidiaries to private owners within three months.
Analysts said that the Chemical Banking Corporation, which already owns the Houston-based Texas Commerce Bank, had expressed interest in acquiring all or part of First City.
Andrew C. Hove Jr., acting chairman of the F.D.I.C., said depositors in the 16 First City subsidiaries that had adequate capital would be fully protected, even the 5,700 accounts in excess of the $100,000 insurance limits.
But it will be a different story for depositors in First City's four subsidiaries in Houston, Dallas, Austin and San Antonio that did not have sufficient capital. While deposits of less than $100,000 will be fully protected, depositors with accounts in excess of $100,000 will receive just 80 percent of their uninsured amount. Those 5,000 accounts with a combined $260 million might receive more than that if sales of the bank's assets exceed expectations.
Stephen R. Steinbrink, the acting Comptroller of the Currency, said his office moved to close First City after an examination of the First City Bank of Houston, the holding company's largest subsidiary, found that a management-led plan to revive the bank was inadequate.
His office found that First City of Houston, with $2.6 billion in assets, needed to make additional provisions for its reserve to cover potential loan losses. But because the bank had just $15 million in capital, Federal regulators deemed the bank insolvent.
To explain why the F.D.I.C. took this delicate move now, just four days before Election Day and in President Bush's home state, Mr. Steinbrink said, "Once we clearly identify that a bank is insolvent, we must act."
He said that First City had submitted a plan to raise more capital by selling 13 subsidiaries, but added that regulators found the plan would not do enough to shore up its capital.
Also today, the Texas Banking Commissioner, Catherine A. Ghiglieri, closed First City's subsidiary in Dallas after finding it insolvent.
First City has been an object of concern among regulators for years. Four years ago, the bank received the infusion of more than $900 million from the F.D.I.C. as part of a bailout plan that made A. Robert Abboud, the former chairman of the First Chicago Corporation, First City's chairman with a mandate to turn the bank's fortunes around. But the real estate slump and an aggressive foray into foreign lending continued to generate losses, and prompted criticism of the chairman's free-wheeling style.
In August, First City gave up on its search for a merger partner and announced a plan to raise new capital to meet regulatory standards without Federal intervention.
At a news conference today, Mr. Steinbrink said: "The reason it didn't work the first time was because the plan was not sufficient to restore it to clear health. The bank that was left behind continued to suffer losses."
Federal regulators said they had ousted First City's top two executives and had appointed Edward G. Harshfield, a longtime banker, to head the bank. Mr. Harshfield, a former senior officer at Citicorp, heads the executive committee of the Federal Capital Bank in Washington and is chairman of EH Thrift Management in Chicago.
