HEADLINE: Japan Says Bad Bank Loans Soared by 50% in Six Months

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Oct. 30

 BODY:
The Government reported figures today showing that Japan's financial crisis is growing more severe, despite its efforts to rescue the ailing banking sector.
The Finance Ministry reported that bad loans at major banks had soared more than 50 percent in the last six months. It also said that the toll of failed loans, mostly to the real estate sector, would continue to rise in the near future. 
 In another important announcement, the banks presented the outline of a long-awaited plan for writing off tens of billions of dollars of failed real estate loans. The plan calls for them to sell off their bad loans to a newly formed corporation, to be financed with money from the banks themselves. The Government insisted that not a yen of taxpayer money would be used to help the banks, but analysts said that billions of dollars in tax breaks were a keystone of the rescue package.
But financial experts dismissed the plan as inadequate, saying it was too modest given the dimensions of the bad loan problem and that it relied on financial sleight of hand rather than a clear formula for lifting the banks' burden of bad loans and getting them fresh capital.
"This is too preliminary to call a plan," said Akio Mikuni, president of Mikuni & Company, an independent credit rating agency. "It's just the start of the process of creating a plan. They're starting small and will enlarge the proposal as they need to."
There was widespread agreement among banking experts that today's report showed that the Finance Ministry was still underestimating the depth of Japan's banking problems. The banks have been battered by reckless lending practices and a severe drop in property and stock prices over the last three years.
Today's announcements also suggested that Japanese taxpayers would ultimately have to bear far more of the cost of rescuing the financial industry than the Government has admitted, these analysts said.
The problems could affect other countries as well. Japanese banks had been big lenders in overseas markets, from London to Los Angeles to New York. The Government here is already worried that the failure of the banks to increase their lending in Japan is worsening a sharp economic slowdown here. By cutting back further overseas, as many analysts expect, they could also reduce the flow of loans to businesses in the United States and elsewhere, although that would also make the Japanese banks less competitive with foreign banks in the overseas markets.
Although they were said to have understated the amount of bad loans, the figures released today by the Finance Ministry still showed a sharp deterioration in banks' loan portfolios.
As of Sept. 30, the Finance Ministry reported, loans on which no interest payments had been made for six months or more came to 12.3 trillion yen, or $100 billion at current exchange rates. While that represents a 54 percent increase from $65 billion at the end of March, most analysts said they thought that if loans being supported artificially were added, the figure would probably be closer to $244 billion. Of that amount, 4 trillion yen, or about $32.5 billion, is expected to be a total loss, a 56 percent increase from 2.56 trillion yen on March 31. These figures applied to the 21 largest commercial banks, long-term banks and trust banks.
Analysts said they doubted those numbers for several reasons. One is that if a borrower makes just one payment in six months, the loan is no longer considered nonperforming. Thus some loans might be classified as current even if the borrower is not making regular interest payments.
In addition, many borrowers are paying rates that are well below market levels, since banks have been forced to make significant concessions to keep borrowers from collapse.
 
 
Interest Rate Cut
 David Snoddy, the banking analyst here for Jardine Fleming Securities, pointed out that the number of loans in which banks had cut the interest rate to these low levels had been soaring. The official figures, he said, show that the amount of loans for which the interest rate is less than 3.25 percent -- which is roughly half the market rate -- jumped to 1.989 trillion yen, or $16.2 billion, in July, from 972 billion yen in March.
"The corpse is just being kept warmed over in these cases," he said.
Mr. Noguchi said he thought that of the $244 billion in loans he estimates to be nonperforming, 10 trillion yen worth are complete losses.
The Finance Ministry first announced its intention to rescue its financial system in August, after the stock market had lost more than $3 trillion in value and the economy had slid into a severe slowdown. For two years, ministry officials denied that any sort of bailout for banks was necessary. But they finally acted when the stock market plummeted to a six-year low and signs began to appear that the failure of the ailing banks to make new loans was stifling the already weak Japanese economy.
The package announced by Finance Minister Tsutomu Hata on Aug. 18 called for the bank bailout plan announced today and some accounting rule changes that permitted banks to withhold from the public certain losses they were suffering.
 
 
Plan on Real Estate
 The plan provisions for helping banks write off bad real estate loans were complex but straightforward in principle. They were prepared by the Mitsubishi Bank, whose chairman is the head of the bankers association here.
Banks will assemble their nonperforming real estate loans and sell them to the newly formed corporation. This corporation will have capital of about $50 million, which will come from the member banks.
The purchase price of these loans will be lent to the corporation by the bank selling them. If the price of the land being held as collateral for the loan rises later, the original bank will receive any increase in the value of the loan or its underlying real estate. So the bank selling the loan will effectively still retain a financial interest in it. The "sale" will merely shuffle the loan off the banks' books.
The banks will sell the loans at less than face value. The difference between the face value and the sale level will be deducted from banks' profits before taxes. That will save the banks billions of dollars in taxes. Corporate taxes in Japan run about 50 percent of earnings.
 
Market Is Paralyzed
 Normally, when banks want to write off a loan they must do so after taxes. That means the new plan, by permitting banks to sell their loans to this new company, will save them large sums.
According to the plan, the corporation will stop buying these loans after about 5 years, and will try to sell off the land holdings over about 10 years.
That still leaves the problem of determining at what price land will be bought or sold. As Government officials generally agree, the problem today is not just that property prices have fallen, but that the market is paralyzed. There are no transactions to use a comparison to determine appropriate price levels. This means that when the banks and the new corporation buy or sell property or loans backed by property they will essentially be able to set prices where they like.
"The problem is not that prices would go up or down," one senior Finance Ministry official said in a briefing for reporters. "The problem is that people don't know the price."
But perhaps the biggest problem, Professor Noguchi said, is that the plan would probably benefit only banks that are better off. It requires the banks to have large amounts of capital with which to finance the write-offs, but that is something the medium-sized and smaller banks lack, he said.
"The system as a whole can cope," he said. "The problem is the marginal banks."
Some experts said that even though the Government had previously pledged not to use taxpayer funds for the bailout, the plan outlined today would give the banks billions of dollars in tax breaks to help them absorb the costs.
"The key to the whole scheme is the tax advantages," Professor Noguchi said. "The banks have obtained a tax benefit, a huge benefit, and that is what they are relying on."
Added J. Brian Waterhouse, the banking analyst here for James Capel Pacific Ltd., "This is not going to solve the real estate problem, and it's not going to solve the bank problems."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Al Gore;
Gore and Clinton Share Language of Their Own

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MANCHESTER, N.H., Oct. 30

 BODY:
Looking a bit stiff and sleepy, Senator Al Gore sat on a stage listening to his running mate speaking from halfway across the country over a television hookup that had linked them both to dozens of union halls. Then, something made him toss his head and chuckle.
Gov. Bill Clinton was stealing his lines. 
 Mr. Gore has concluded hundreds of speeches by saying: "They've run out of time. They've run out of excuses. They've run out of ideas, and on Nov. 3, with your help we're going to run them out of office." The fact that Mr. Clinton now sometimes does the same is indicative of the unusual role Mr. Gore is playing on the Democratic ticket.
 
Clinton's Political Twin
 When the Democratic Presidential nominee picked another young, moderate Southerner to run with him in July, traditional notions of ticket balancing went out the window. In three and a half months of campaigning since then, Mr. Gore has become Mr. Clinton's political twin, intellectual sidekick and traveling buddy. Along with their wives they have turned eight bus trips into smiling double dates that projected the ticket's defining image.
At the end of the last bus trip Monday, after nearly 15 hours of joint campaigning across North Carolina, the two candidates were still locked in conversation in the back of their bus. Midnight had passed, yet for more than hour they kept their entourages waiting on the chilly tarmac of an isolated airport while they talked.
"These guys spend so much time together that they really have their own deal worked out, and everyone, even the staff, is looking at it from the outside," said Robert Squier, a political consultant for the Democrats. "They are constantly trading off ideas so that it is not only one message but it has gotten to be one language."
But, there is only one Presidency. Mr. Gore was ambitious enough to seek it in 1988, before he was 40 years old. Sometimes he still seems to be adjusting to his supporting role in this campaign. But in a recent interview Mr. Gore insisted it has not been that difficult. "Bill and I share so many basic ideas and dreams for what the country ought to do," he said, "that it is an opportunity to focus on those broader goals."
 
Fascinated by Complex Issues
 Both men are steeped in the precepts of the Democratic Leadership Council, an organization of office holders, most of them Southern, seeking an alternative to the liberalism that has dominated the party since the 1960's.
When Mr. Gore speaks of his running mate, he emphasizes their philosophical affinity and shared fascination with complex policy issues. Their relationship, he said, has developed through long conversations on topics like the need to devise an American version of the collaboration among Japanese universities, businesses and government agencies. Mr. Gore has fully embraced not only Mr. Clinton's message of change, but also his specific proposals.
Indeed, for the good of the overall campaign, Mr. Gore has tempered his vigorous advocacy of environmental causes, delaying a major policy address on the topics until today and delivering it here in New Hampshire, far from any of the big industrial states where it might cost votes and where the election may be decided.
The speech itself is a paradigm of moderation, for a man who trumpets imminent global dangers in his best-selling book, "Earth in the Balance." Mr. Gore dwelled on assertions that the Bush Administration has undermined environmental standards but never said how strictly a Clinton administration would enforce the standards.
While avoiding any mention of politically delicate issues like automobile fuel-efficiency standards or endangered species protection, Mr. Gore backed away from Government intervention on his major proposal to encourage the growth of environmentally sound industries. "We will use market forces wherever possible to accomplish our goals," he said.
Like most Vice-Presidential candidates, Mr. Gore has attacked the ticket's opponents, mostly through dozens of small events designed to produce local television coverage. His tone is mocking and derisive. Several times a day he draws laughs, saying, "If George Bush went to Hollywood and made a movie, they'd have to call it 'Honey, I Shrunk the Economy,' and if he made a sequel they'd have to call it, 'Honey, I Blew Up the Deficit.' "
Mr. Gore resists any suggestion that he has been relegated to the delivery of red meat lines. "I haven't done that, in the traditional way," he said.
"I would like to think that I've done it in a very substantive and thorough way that is well documented," he said, noting his heavily footnoted speeches accusing Mr. Bush of coddling President Saddam Hussein of Iraq before the Persian Gulf war.
Although he has improved the wooden delivery that dogged his 1988 candidacy, Mr. Gore remains enigmatically inconsistent on the campaign trail. On a given day he can be rousing and witty at one event, dull at another even as he delivers the same speech. While Mr. Clinton has a polished sense of cadence and uses personal reminiscences to connect with audiences, Mr. Gore can seem mechanical and distant.
His performance improves noticeably in question-and-answer sessions with ordinary voters, perhaps as a result of his having conducted some 3,000 town meetings with Tennessee voters during his 16 years as a Representative and Senator. On these occasions he explains complex issues like welfare reform by carefully defining terms and alternatives with the patience of a high school teacher.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Like most Vice-Presidential candidates, Senator Al Gore has attacked the ticket's opponents, mostly through dozens of small events designed to produce local television coverage. He spoke with audience members during a break in the taping of a town meeting last week for MTV in New York. (Jim Estrin/The New York Times)

LENGTH: 1455 words

HEADLINE: Trouble, but No Time Bomb, at Banks

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
The savings and loan mess was swept under the rug during the 1988 Presidential campaign only to explode immediately afterward. Whether the nation's commercial banks are a similar time bomb has emerged as an issue in this year's campaign.
Ross Perot says both Republicans and Democrats are trying to hide a crisis in which commercial banks may require a huge taxpayer bailout. President Bush and Gov. Bill Clinton say the system is fundamentally sound, despite some problem banks. 
 As politicians wrestle over how large the problem is and who is to blame, Federal banking regulators insist they have nothing to hide. They like to recall that months ago they stated their intention to step up the pace of closing weak banks on Dec. 19, when bank regulations, required by Congress last year, take effect.
 Indeed, tonight, the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation called a news conference to announce that had closed the troubled First City Bancorporation of Houston. The move came after months of speculation that the bank would fail in its efforts to strengthen its capital on its own.
The closings will certainly make the situation appear more dire. But more than a dozen banking experts in Government, industry and academia said in interviews that they did not see a crisis akin to the savings and loan bailout, which will cost taxpayers more than $200 billion.
Yes, the commercial banking industry is fragile. But it is far stronger than the hemorrhaging savings and loan industry was four years ago.
 
'Misleading' Assumption
 "To assume the commercial banking industry is the savings and loan industry in sheep's clothing is terribly misleading," said James J. McDermott Jr., a banking analyst with Keefe, Bruyette & Woods in New York.
And L. William Seidman, the former chairman of the F.D.I.C., said: "The disaster scenarios we're seeing are based on a total collapse in real estate markets in the future. The real estate markets are terrible and will stay terrible, but I don't think they will get a lot worse."
Like the savings institutions, the ailing banks are usually plagued by problem loans for commercial real estate, including office buildings or shopping malls. Unlike most savings institutions, some banks are also saddled with soured loans for leveraged buyouts, oil and gas exploration and third world nations.
Still, Federal regulators insist that the worst of the commercial bank crisis is over. They project that they will shut 100 to 125 failing banks, with combined assets of $76 billion, in 1993. That compares with an average of 175 failures annually during the last four years. But the amount of assets of failed banks, at $76 billion, would be a record amount for one year.
As for the cost, the Government says it will put up about $13 billion to protect depositors in those failed banks. Regulators say that these costs will be covered by the deposit insurance premiums collected from the banking industry. These premiums go into a bank insurance fund that is already short of money.
Banking experts are leery of the Government's estimates. Regulators systematically underestimated the costs of the savings and loan bailout. Some experts say they are certain that taxpayers will be called on to make up the difference between what the bank insurance fund takes in from premiums paid by banks, and what is needed to finance the bailout of commercial bank depositors.
The F.D.I.C.'s latest estimate of the overall costs of bank closings through 1996 -- $45 billion -- could be underestimated by tens of billions of dollars, these experts say, especially if the nation falls back into recession or if real estate values continue to move downward.
"They're giving a very rosy projection, no matter how you cut it," said Edward J. Kane, a professor of finance at Boston College. He predicts that the Government will need $69.7 billion to protect depositors at failing commercial banks in the next few years.
 
 
If a Giant Stumbles
 Analysts also say that all projections are out the window if one or two banking giants fail.
And that is a crucial difference between the bank situation and the savings and loan crisis. If a single huge bank fails, the Government might have to come up with $10 billion to protect depositors.
Indeed, with Citicorp, the nation's largest banking company, having difficulties, some pessimistic analysts said its collapse alone could overwhelm the system. But Government officials and most analysts say Citicorp is slowly but surely on the mend, as are the few other shaky institutions among the top 100.
Despite all such assurances, suspicion lingers that there may be a December surprise.
A big reason is the gap between the number of banks that regulators said a year ago that they would have to close in 1992, and the number they have actually closed so far this year. Projected for closing were 200 institutions with combined assets of more than $80 billion; in fact, 85 banks with $29 billion in assets had been closed through Oct. 16.
Here is how Andrew C. Hove Jr., the acting chairman of the F.D.I.C., answers skeptics who say that the Government is holding back on a huge wave of closings until after Election Day: In Congressional testimony this week, he said the main reason for the lower-than-expected number of closings was not an orchestrated slowdown, but the fact that "lower interest rates have kept many doomed institutions afloat in the short run, and low rates have even saved some institutions."
Banks often pay 3 percent for deposits and lend at 7 percent or more -- considerably more on credit cards -- giving them huge profit margins that helped to beef up their capital. The more capital a bank has, the better it can withstand loan losses.
 
 
Faster Closings
 One slowdown was orchestrated, however. The Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation Improvement Act of 1991 was intended to compel regulators to close troubled institutions faster, hoping this would prevent bank losses and Government bailouts from mushrooming.
Some lawmakers wanted the tough new rules to take effect in December, when the law was enacted. But other lawmakers -- and some Administration officials as well -- wanted enforcement delayed for a year. They prevailed. On Dec. 19, regulators will be required, with few exceptions, to shut down within 90 days banks that are critically undercapitalized -- that is, banks with capital of less than 2 percent of assets. Those who argued for delay feared angry executives and depositors -- and voter backlash -- if banks were closed before they were at death's door.
The nation's 12,000 federally insured banks are often divided into two groups: the 10,500 healthy ones and the 1,500 weak ones. Of the weak ones, the F.D.I.C. has identified 1,044 problem banks, with $568 billion in assets, as requiring close scrutiny.
The troubled banks are large and small, and are located disproportionately in New England as well as in New York, New Jersey, Colorado, Texas and California.
The F.D.I.C. has identified 46 banks with assets of $19 billion that will now be classified as critically undercapitalized. Veribanc Inc., a Massachusetts-based bank rating agency, says this list includes First City, the Bank of San Francisco and the Burritt Interfinancial Bancorporation of New Britain, Conn.
The Bush Administration attributes the banking industry's problems to the Democrats in Congress. They failed to pass the President's bank proposals, which he said would strengthen banks by removing barriers to their expansion across state lines and into new services, including insurance and securities.
Governor Clinton and the Democrats trace the commercial banks' woes to the go-go 1980's and the deregulation of the Reagan-Bush years.
 
Fear of Rate Increases
 Low interest rates have resulted in record profits for the banking industry this year, but this rebound does not mean all is well. "There is a concern that these operating profits obscure the fact that there are parts of the banking system that remain very weak," said Robert R. Glauber, a Treasury official in the Reagan and Bush Administrations who is a professor at the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard.
Some lawmakers are urging far-reaching changes in the industry. Representative Charles E. Schumer, Democrat of Brooklyn, has proposed setting up core banks that could use Government-insured deposits only for safe, traditional loans to small businesses and consumers.
"The basic problem is you have an industry that was structured for a regulated time, but now exists in a deregulated world," Mr. Schumer said. "Until that changes, the banking industry is going to be in a fundamental state of disequilibrium."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Florida;
Clinton Sees Opportunity to Break G.O.P. Grip on Cuban-Americans

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MIAMI, Oct. 30

 BODY:
In 1988, more than 85 percent of Cuban-American voters in Florida cast their ballots for George Bush. But sensing that Mr. Bush is vulnerable this year, the Clinton campaign has stepped up its drive to win the support of the ethnic group both sides regard as a key to the state's 25 electoral votes.
With the latest polls showing the Presidential race a dead heat here, in the nation's fourth-most-populous state, even the slightest shift among Cubans, who vote Republican more consistently than any ethnic group in the nation, could be decisive. With such stakes, Gov. Bill Clinton has made two forays into the Republican stronghold of Little Havana and has used Spanish-language radio commercials and numerous appearances by surrogates to offer his anti-Castro credentials. 
 Mr. Clinton got a big lift on Tuesday when Jorge Mas Canosa, chairman of the Cuban American National Foundation, the most powerful Cuban-exile lobbying group, flew to Tampa to meet with him. Though he has formally endorsed Mr. Bush, Mr. Mas infuriated Republican leaders by then issuing a statement saying any fears that the Cuban-American community may have had about a Clinton Administration and its attitude toward Fidel Castro "have dissipated today."
 
Heavy Turnout Expected
 Statewide, Cuban-Americans account for fewer than 10 percent of the registered voters. But because they go to the polls at a much higher rate than the population as a whole, over 90 percent in some precincts, and have tended to vote as a block, they have often provided the margin of victory for Republican candidates in recent years.
"In a close general election in which Cuban-Americans turn out more heavily than anyone else, they can give a Republican candidate a net gain of up to six percentage points," said Robert Joffee, director of the Mason-Dixon Florida Poll, an independent opinion-research group here.
No one in the Democratic Party here expects the majority of Cuban-Americans to vote for the Democratic ticket. But party leaders and poll takers said Mr. Clinton had a chance to win between a quarter and a third of the Cuban vote. "And in a close race in Florida, that could be enough to tip the balance," said Simon Ferro, the state party chairman.
Mr. Clinton's backers say the Democratic nominee is attracting Cuban voters because of his early support for the Cuban Democracy Act, which tightens the American economic embargo of Cuba. Mr. Clinton endorsed the measure on April 23 during a speech in Little Havana, in which he criticized Mr. Bush's Cuban policy as another example of the President's failure to be tough with dictators.
 
Cheering for Clinton
 "I think this Administration has missed a big opportunity to put the hammer down on Fidel Castro and Cuba," Mr. Clinton told hundreds of cheering Cuban-Americans at a dinner that raised $125,000 for his campaign.
Days after Mr. Clinton's speech, Mr. Bush reluctantly endorsed the bill despite reservations about provisions affecting United States allies and American companies whose foreign subsidiaries do business with Cuba, and signed it last week. But Mr. Clinton's quick, enthusiastic endorsement, his supporters say, gave him instant credibility among Cuban-American voters and neutralized a traditional Republican electoral advantage.
"He can't be tagged as an agent of Communism or a liberal who is going to cozy up to Fidel, as other nominees were," said Mr. Ferro, who is Cuban-American. "Once you strip them of that weapon," he said, referring to the Republicans' image as the only anti-Castro political force, "you can deal with them on other issues, like the economy."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Taxes;
Clinton Promises to Protect Middle Class on Taxes

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Faced with attacks from President Bush and skepticism from outside experts about his spending and tax proposals, Gov. Bill Clinton has begun to emphasize that he would cut back spending for new programs rather than raise the taxes of middle-class Americans.
"If the tax proposals I have made do not fund the spending levels I have proposed," Mr. Clinton said Thursday night at a town meeting in Secaucus, N.J., "then I will scale back the spending, cut other Government spending or both. I will not lower the income levels to raise taxes for the programs I have proposed." 
 Mr. Clinton has proposed raising the top tax bracket to 36 percent from 31 percent on family incomes over $200,000 a year and single people's incomes over $150,000. He would give a small tax reduction to families he describes as middle income: those with incomes below $80,000. Families with incomes between $80,000 and $200,000 would not have their taxes changed, according to the Clinton staff.
In an interview today on the ABC program "Good Morning America," Mr. Clinton said he believed his tax estimates were "good and sound." But he said that if he was wrong and tax revenues proved insufficient, "We might have to phase in the health care program over a couple of more years; we might have to slow down the education programs."
 
Independent Experts' Views
 The new emphasis Mr. Clinton has been placing on his tax policies seemed to be a response to statements by President Bush's staff that the Clinton spending proposals could require higher taxes on Americans with incomes as low as $20,000.
Independent budget experts who have analyzed the Clinton figures find no merit in the Bush Administration's calculations, but many do question whether some of Mr. Clinton's tax measures could raise as much money as he intends and whether some of the spending cuts he has offered are practical.
Mr. Clinton refuses to make the kind of absolute pledge against raising taxes on the middle class that Mr. Bush made during the 1988 campaign.
"If the money doesn't come in, then we ought to find some other part of the budget to cut or we ought to phase in the programs over a longer period of time," he said Thursday on the NBC News program "Today."
"Now, that's not the same thing as saying 'read my lips,' in the event of an economic emergency," he continued. "What would happen if the deficit went up by $100 billion more than anybody thought? What would happen if interest rates went haywire? I mean, I can't predict everything that could happen in a very tough global economy."
 
Change in Figures
 Mr. Clinton's statements on who would be entitled to the middle-class tax cut have changed somewhat recently, but the difference seems to be more apparent than real.
On Oct. 11, in his first debate with Mr. Bush and Ross Perot, Mr. Clinton said the tax relief would go to "middle class people with families with incomes of under $60,000." Now, he is saying the figure is $80,000.
Gene Sperling, economic policy director of the Clinton campaign, said in an interview today that in the debate, Mr. Clinton was thinking in terms of taxable income, or income after deductions and exemptions are taken. Afterward, Mr. Sperling said, the staff advised Mr. Clinton to begin talking about adjusted gross income, or total income, since that is the way voters generally think of their own income.

HEADLINE: Trends Collide in 3d District Rematch

BYLINE: By CONSTANCE L. HAYS

 BODY:
For the second time in two years, voters along Connecticut's middle coastline face the same choice for Congress: Rosa DeLauro, a Democrat who scraped her way to a 7,000-vote victory in 1990, or Tom Scott, a Republican and a former State Senator best known as the archfoe of the state income tax.
In this season of widespread voter discontent, Ms. DeLauro could find her incumbency a handicap, and Mr. Scott is quick to characterize her as a Washington insider because of her work with Emily's List, a political-action committee that supports women running for office, and the seven years she spent on the staff of Senator Christopher J. Dodd. 
 But it is also expected to be a good year for women, and the rage over the state income tax, enacted in 1991 after bitter debate, seems to have diminished. Those factors could undermine Mr. Scott's bid to become the Congressman from the state's Third District, which zigs north from Stratford to Wallingford, encompassing New Haven and returning to the coastline at a town called Clinton.
A poll released by Quinnipiac College found Ms. DeLauro firmly in front, with 56.8 percent of 456 voters queried between Oct. 23 and 28 saying they would support her, compared with 24.6 percent supporting Mr. Scott. However, 18.6 percent were undecided. The poll had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 6 percent, according to the college.
The district, like much of the rest of Connecticut, is reeling under the impact of the recession. The economy is a major issue for voters here, and both Mr. Scott and Ms. DeLauro have addressed it, in different ways.
 
Different Styles
 Ms. DeLauro points to her record in Congress, listing votes for a highway jobs bill and a proposed bill that would make job creation a requirement of the Clean Water Act. Mr. Scott emphasizes reducing the Federal deficit as the first step toward reviving the economy. He also said he would try to get a seat on the House Armed Services Committee, "to fight for defense jobs in Connecticut."
On the stump, Mr. Scott has a button-down sartorial style, his hair close clipped and his delivery dry but fact filled. His opponent mixes friendliness and fire, with an elfin bob framing her intense gaze. Mr. Scott has a power base in Milford, a city of 53,000 that runs the gamut from blue-collar to well-to-do. Ms. DeLauro retains a solid base in New Haven, the largest municipality in the district, where voters chose her at a rate of more than 5 to 1 in 1990.
Another major difference is their positions on taxes. Ms. DeLauro has proposed increasing taxes on the wealthiest tier of Americans, while Mr. Scott heads the Connecticut Taxpayers Committee, an anti-tax lobby formed shortly after his loss to Ms. DeLauro.
He is opposed to abortion, except in cases of rape or danger to the mother's health. Ms. DeLauro favors abortion rights.
Mr. Scott spent part of a day recently at a Waldbaum's supermarket in Milford, where his father was chairman of the Board of Aldermen and initiated his son into the ways of politics at the age of 8, when the boy began licking stamps and handing out fliers for one of his father's campaigns. His mother was a nurse in a New Haven hospital.
At the stop, a campaign worker trailed one woman across the parking lot and supervised the installation of a bumper sticker reading "Tom Scott/Change Congress" on the back of her aging maroon Thunderbird.
"You have my vote," chortled one elderly woman, as she shook Mr. Scott's outstretched hand. "And so does Perot." Mr. Scott's mother, Dorothy, greeted people and handed out campaign fliers, wearing a T-shirt that declared that she was "Tom Scott's Mom."
On Monday, Ms. DeLauro stood in the gray chill of a New Haven parking garage, greeting nurses, secretaries and interns in surgical scrubs as they raced through a pedestrian tunnel leading from Yale-New Haven Hospital. "Oh my goodness, this is a great honor," gasped one woman in a lab coat, as she was introduced. "No," said Ms. DeLauro. "My honor."
Ms. DeLauro is the daughter of a longtime New Haven Alderwoman, Luisa DeLauro, who represents Wooster Square, a historically Italian neighborhood. Representative DeLauro is married to Stanley Greenberg, a prominent Washington political consultant who heads the firm of Greenberg/Lake and who has been working on the campaign of Democratic Presidential candidate Gov. Bill Clinton.
Reflecting her family, the campaign mixes grass-roots approaches with sophisticated techniques, like television commercials aimed at showing her as an activist representative, in touch with the needs of her constituents. Mr. Scott said he considers that message fraudulent.
"She's just deceiving the voters, and her husband, the pollster, is manipulating her every move," Mr. Scott said. "She can't credibly say she is for change. She was a lobbyist, and she was a Congressional bureaucrat. That's all she's done."
But the DeLauro campaign is being run on her record, which includes pushing for tax cuts for the middle class as part of a bill that was vetoed by President Bush.
Ms. DeLauro, whose campaign handouts describe her as a "cancer survivor" -- she was diagnosed with ovarian cancer in 1986, and has since been given a clean bill of health -- also urged increased attention for women's health issues and joined a House task force to change health care. She caught the attention of the House majority leader, Richard Gephardt, and brought him late last year to Hamden, a town in her district, for a poignant discussion with middle-class residents who had lost their jobs in the recession and were faced with losing their houses, their cars and much of their way of life.
"We call her the ubiquitous Rosa," said Kathi McDonnell-Bissell, executive director of the Milford Senior Center, where Ms. DeLauro stopped recently for lunch and a country-and-western dance with elderly people. "She is a problem solver."
Both candidates have studiously avoided the kind of verbal warfare that dominated their last race. Mr. Scott seemed bemused as he described debating "an empty chair" on a radio station, because, he said, Ms. DeLauro refuses to face him. Her campaign staff said there were so many invitations to debate that they could not accept them all. But they have appeared together in two televised debates, both notable for the candidates' subdued demeanors.

She is facing Tom Scott, a Republican, in a rematch of their 1990 contest, which Ms. DeLauro narrowly won. (David LaBianca for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Dow Posts Drop of 19.99, But Has Gain for Week

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
Wall Street stocks, under pressure from unexpectedly soft economic data and a drop in bond prices, declined yesterday.
The Dow Jones industrial average gave up 19.99 points, closing at 3,226.28. For the week, however, the Dow gained 18.64 points. Yesterday, losers nudged out gainers on the New York Stock Exchange, where volume was 201.9 million shares. Traders said the sale of a large block of stock pressured the market at the close. 
 Two new opinion polls showing President Bush in a near dead heat with Gov. Bill Clinton served only to heighten pre-election anxiety.
The market had already virtually factored in a Clinton victory. While individual stocks showed reactions to specific news items, analysts noted few trends. "People don't want to make any major moves," said Steve Poling, an executive vice president at Fortis Advisers Inc.
"The market doesn't like uncertainty, and this is becoming more uncertain," Mr. Poling said.
David Bostian, chief economist at Herzog Heine Geduld, said: "Either way, I think the market will rally after the elections. No matter who wins, people will start making decisions that were put on hold."
Economic data continued to show weakness. The University of Michigan survey showed consumer confidence falling. The Chicago purchasing managers group's index fell to 49.7 in October from 59.6. The Commerce Department said sales of new homes in September dropped 1 percent.
Despite the signs of economic weakness, bond prices fell as investors bailed out of positions ahead of the election, pushing yields higher.
Shares of Curaflex Health slumped 3, to 5 1/2, in over-the-counter trading after it reported third-quarter profits below analysts' expectations. Smith Barney cut its rating, and Morgan Stanley lowered estimates.
Cooper Industries continued to fall on the New York Stock Exchange after it announced disappointing earnings on Thursday. Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette; Kidder, Peabody, and Lehman Brothers all cut their ratings. It lost 3/8, to 45 7/8.
The Standard & Poor's composite index of 500 stocks fell 2.18, to 418.68. The American Stock Exchange index rose 0.43, to 381.72.
The Big Board's composite index of all listed common stocks fell 0.85, to 230.57. The average share was down 12 cents.
The Nasdaq index fell 0.65,to 605.17.
The Wilshire Associates Equity Index -- the market value of Big Board, American and Nasdaq issues -- was 4,067.783, down 12.991, or 0.32 percent.
John Blair, head trader at County NatWest, said it was possible that the prospect of a Bush victory upset some investors by raising the expectation of "four more years of gridlock" with a Democratic Congress.
Mr. Bostian said that "even though Bush may have closed in, my impression is that Clinton still has a lead in the electoral college."

HEADLINE: New-Home Sales Drop 1%; Consumer Confidence Is Off

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Ending a pre-election week in which a stream of economic indicators continued to point to stunted recovery, the Commerce Department said today that new-home sales fell slightly in September. In addition, nongovernment bodies found consumer confidence and Chicago-area industrial activity eroding in October.
The findings, said Edward F. McKelvey, a senior economist at Goldman, Sachs, are "consistent with a very sluggish economy." 
 Today's data, like Tuesday's superficially optimistic report on national output, required close inspection.
Home sales edged down 1 percent, to an annual rate of 617,000, last month, the Commerce Department said. Increases in the Northeast and South were more than offset by declines in the Midwest and West.
 
August Drop Is Reversed
 This, however, was accompanied by a sharp upward revision for August, one that transformed what had been a 6.1 percent drop into a 1.6 percent advance. This change, based on new data, suggested to some that housing activity probably bottomed last spring, but analysts cautioned against expecting large gains.
"At best we're going to flounder at this level," said Samuel D. Kahan, economist for Fuji Securities in Chicago. Mr. McKelvey added that prospects did not seem bright if current low mortgage rates did not generate more than about 600,000 sales.
The sales figures suggest annual housing starts, which include the dormant apartment sector, of little more than one million. The National Association of Home Builders has been projecting a 10 percent rise in 1993, to 1.32 million.
As for consumer attitudes over all, the University of Michigan reported its Index of Consumer Sentiment slipped to 73.3 in October, virtually unchanged from its midmonth estimate; September's figure was 75.4. This was the fourth straight monthly decline, matching the drops in the Conference Board's consumer confidence survey published last Tuesday.
Consumer assessment of current conditions dropped to 82.5 from 83.7, reflecting downbeat views of jobs and incomes. But expectations edged up to 67.5 from 66.4, according to subscribers of the proprietary report. The expectations element is part of the Commerce Department's Index of Leading Economic Indicators, which is intended to forecast the economy.
In a third report, Chicago purchasing managers said their October index -- a composite of several manufacturing-related factors like new orders and inventory -- tumbled to 49.7 from 59.6, the biggest decline since May 1980. This followed seven months of broad growth.
Production slowed sharply as new orders slumped and backlogs shrank, the industrial managers found.
On the other hand, many analysts had considered the August index too high and at odds with the depressed index compiled by the National Association of Purchasing Management.
 
National Figures Due Monday
 Nationwide purchasing managers' figures for October are to be published on Monday.
Yet another survey, this one by Dun & Bradstreet, reported today that incorporations of new businesses climbed to 58,248 in June, up 12.8 percent from a year earlier. Incorporations were 6.9 percent higher for the first six months of the year.
Joseph W. Duncan, corporate economist and chief statistician for Dun & Bradstreet, said the rebound, after five annual declines, represented "a resurgence in entrepreneurial activity."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN;
In Their Own Words

 BODY:

PRESIDENT BUSH -- In a speech to Kentucky Fried Chicken owners yesterday in Nashville.
"Yes, AIDS is a national tragedy. But we don't need a bureaucratic czar in our nation's capital. We need more compassion in our hometowns. 
 More education. More caring.
And a President has to set priorities, because it is your money that we're talking about. And if you look at Governor Clinton closely, you see a philosophy where bureaucrats in Washington carve out the exact same programs to try and solve problems facing people in Nashville or Nashua or anywhere.
You might call this old-fashioned idea trample-down economics. Tramples down business with these deadly new mandates and regulations. Tramples down individual initiative with higher taxes. And tramples down the dreams of people with the power of that bureaucracy, the power of bureaucrats."
 
GOV. BILL CLINTON -- In a speech yesterday at a rally in Pittsburgh.
"Mr. Bush also said we were crazy yesterday. Now, let me tell you something. I tell you what. It takes one to know one.
I'll tell you what I think is crazy. I think crazy is unemployment going up and incomes going down. It's 100,000 Americans a month losing their health insurance. It's one in 10 Americans on food stamps. It's America going from first to 13th in the world in wages under trickle-down economics. Crazy is "read my lips" and then signing the second-biggest tax increase in history. Crazy is the candidate asking us to trust him with four more years when we now know he said to Mikhail Gorbachev, don't pay any attention to what I say trying to get re-elected.
When he's running a government where the F.B.I. is investigating the Justice Department and the Justice Department is investigating the F.B.I. They don't have any time for crooks any more. They're too busy looking into each other. They do have time to investigate my mother's passport file. Talk about crazy."
 
ROSS PEROT -- In an interview broadcast yesterday with David Frost, when asked about accusations that he is paranoid.
"No. 1, I'm not paranoid. No. 2, a great deal had been written and said about me before I started this process. Part of the political process in our country, particularly from one party, is when you have a candidate who is attractive to the people, and who can communicate with the people, is to try to redefine his character. You will never find the word "paranoid" used once in all of the thousands, hundreds of thousands of words written about me before this started. All of these new traits, I laugh, I say, I'll be, you know, I'll be glad to go back to being the person I really am, as opposed to the person they've redefined."

HEADLINE: Lufthansa's Arizona Flying School

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: GOODYEAR, Ariz.

 BODY:
Barring a cloudburst or a violent sandstorm known locally as a devil duster, skies over the Sonoran desert here are dotted with planes. This is where pilots train to fly jumbo jets -- using single- and twin-engine propeller planes for practice.
And while they talk with the control tower in English, they converse after-hours in German, Japanese, Spanish and Chinese. This is not some modern version of the Lafayette Escadrille, those volunteer pilots from many nations who battled the Germans in the skies over Europe in World War I. These are budding pilots and co-pilots with Lufthansa German Airlines, All Nippon Airways of Japan, Iberia Airlines of Spain and EVA Airways of Taiwan, a year-old carrier scheduled to begin daily nonstop flights to Los Angeles and New York this year. 
 "We've been training our future pilots here almost 25 years," said Willy Kuhweide, a Lufthansa pilot and the president of Airline Training Center Arizona Inc. "The Arizona desert has some of the best weather in the world for year-round flying."
 
 
Germans Are Right at Home
 Lufthansa likes it so well in Goodyear, a city of 6,250 people 17 miles west of Phoenix, that in March it bought the 23-acre center for $12 million from the PS Group of San Diego, formerly Pacific Southwest Airlines.
For Lufthansa, the purchase is a badly needed opportunity to make money. Although it is well regarded for its technical and mechanical skills, the state-owned carrier lost $368.8 million in just the first six months of this year. To stem the red ink, the airline plans to eliminate 8,000 jobs and stopped hiring pilots.
In April, it will stop training its own pilots here and in Bremen, Germany, although Heiko Weber, executive vice president and treasurer of this center, said Lufthansa would resume its training here in 1994 to avert a shortage that is expected to result from the retirement of many pilots in 1996 and 1997.
In fact, 60 to 70 percent of airline captains worldwide are expected to retire over the next decade, according to the International Air Transport Association. Conceivably, that could mean a lot of business for Lufthansa.
But to cope with the shortages, some airlines are setting up their own training centers. All Nippon Airways, which has trained 115 pilots here and currently has 100 student-pilots enrolled, will open its own $16 million flight center near Bakersfield, Calif., in mid-November. It says it will send 130 students here in the next two years, but will do its own training after that.
"Like the other Japanese airlines, we face a critical pilot shortage, partly because the Japanese self-defense forces don't produce enough pilots," said Thomas Fredo, a spokesman for All Nippon Airways. "By the year 2,000, we estimate we will need about 2,500 pilots, up from 1,200 now."
To insure adequate flight crews for their expanding routes, Japan Air Lines and All Nippon recently hired some 300 experienced pilots and co-pilots, most of them from the United States and Europe, rather than train their own.
It is difficult to judge just how lucrative one new market will be for the center: it will begin taking United States enrollees, the first 10 of whom are expected in mid-November.
Rather than train students of their own, American carriers typically recruit experienced pilots from the military or graduates of one of the more than 100 colleges and private schools that offer flight training. While the decline in military training in this post-cold-war era would seem to give Lufthansa an opening, many carriers may hire from the vast pool of experienced pilots out of work because of the failures of Pan Am, Eastern, Braniff and Midway.
No one knows exactly how many pilots there are. But they probably do not come close to the 70,000 pilots that the Federal Aviation Agency recently estimated the large airlines and commuter lines will require over the next 12 years because of expansion, retirement and attrition.
 
From the Beginning
 Lufthansa pilot training, like that of many European carriers, is based on the method known as ab initio, Latin for "from the beginning," because none of the aspiring pilots has ever flown a plane. The trainees first spend six months in their homelands studying academic subjects and aviation theory, then eight months here, attending more classes and being trained by mostly American flight instructors, although each airline sends its own coach to help students acclimate to the United States.
Students here spend 30 hours in flight simulators and 190 hours flying. After an additional eight months' training after leaving Arizona, they are ready to begin flying as flight engineers (also known as second officer) on such smaller passenger jets as the Boeing 737 or Airbus A320.
By contrast, the average United States airline pilot hired since the late 1980's had approximately 3,000 hours of flying time, according to the Future Aviation Professionals of America, an Atlanta counseling firm.
The ab initio method may appear to produce pilots who are more rigid than those trained in this country. "But the accident rate doesn't support that assumption," said John Galipault, the president of the Aviation Safety Institute, a nonprofit research group. "And neither does the more important criterion of flight safety. The whole effort with the ab initio method is to train students to do the operational, safety and common sensical things the carriers want done."
Lufthansa says it costs about $200,000 to train a pilot, including room and board, but it will not say what it charges to train pilots of other carriers.
Lufthansa has required its own student pilots to pay $54,000 of the $200,000 and more than $100,000 if they join another airline at any time. Mr. Weber said he had no statistics on how many Lufthansa graduates had done so, but said the number "is minuscule."
Since the hiring freeze, student pilots have been covered by what Lufthansa calls training agreements, with no guarantee of a job.
Now, student pilots in Bremen want the carrier to waive even the $54,000 repayment. "But in such a difficult time for us economically, we can't and we won't do that," said Peter Hobel, a spokesman for Lufthansa in Frankfurt.
United States students will pay an estimated $70,000, but for 12 months of training rather than the almost two years that includes time in Bremen. This will qualify them as commercial pilots, but not to fly jets or turbo-props, skills that will have to be acquired elsewhere -- typically, by flying sightseeing trips, ferrying aircraft, crop dusting, hauling skydivers, flying charters or working as flight instructors.
The student body is 90 percent male, their ages ranging from 22 to 28. Iberia has sent a few women and Lufthansa began training women here four years ago.
One recent afternoon, Cedric Rossdeutscher, a 22-year-old aspiring Lufthansa pilot, talked with Mr. Weber before the student-pilot's solo round-trip flight to Tucson, about 100 miles away. Standing beside the single-engine plane and gazing at the cloudless Southwestern sky, for which high winds and storms had been forecast, Mr. Rossdeutscher said, "So far it looks like good flying weather."
After Mr. Rossdeutscher climbed into the aircraft, Mr. Weber turned and said, "My dream is within 10 years to see a captain of a U.S. carrier who has been trained at A.T.C.A."

HEADLINE: Dodd Strolls as Johnson Runs Underdog's Race

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ANSONIA, Conn., Oct. 30

 BODY:
Entering the final weekend of campaigning before the election, Senator Christopher J. Dodd offered handshakes and small talk through small towns today, while his opponent, Brook Johnson, met business leaders with an urgent message about turning the state's ailing economy around.
It was a day in which Mr. Dodd, a Democrat seeking a third term, chose to chat with bank tellers and local officials rather than stress his campaign theme that his experience in Washington would best protect the interests of the state.
 
Tickets to the Big Game
 His schedule included visits to Shelton, a rally at Wesleyan University with Senator Al Gore and an appearance at Willow Brook Park Stadium in New Britain for a football game between New Britain High School and Newington High School. 
 By contrast, Mr. Johnson started the day at a luncheon with business leaders in Stamford. Later, he met workers as they left the Pratt & Whitney plant in East Hartford, and then headed for the same football game as Mr. Dodd.
With a lead of almost 20 points in the most recent polls, Mr. Dodd said his strategy until Election Day was to meet as many voters as possible. "It's a small state," he said. "People want to see you, talk to you."
As he arrived in Ansonia, he said that he had traveled more than 12,000 miles in his Ford Explorer in the last three and a half weeks of campaigning. "I feel very good about the support we got," he said.
As he strolled up and down Main Street, Mr. Dodd, escorted by local officials, seemed to revel in the enthusiasm of those who walked up to greet him. Entering the Great Country Bank, he eagerly posed for a picture with tellers in Halloween outfits, among them a red devil, a striped jungle cat and a spotted ladybug.
"I love the outfits," he said, laughing. "Let's get a camera and show them how we do banking here in Connecticut."
For their part, people appeared to delight in his presence as he wandered up and down Main Street.
"The Senator has always been welcome here," said Tom Hallihan, the Mayor of Ansonia. "The Senator has always been very helpful to us. He's been very good to the area."
One bank worker, Shirley Ruzicka, said she hadn't given the election much thought. "But it's interesting to note that he came around here," she said. "He took time out from his busy schedule to meet with us."
Still, there were those who weren't won over.
 
Cleaning the Slate
 "It was fun," said Malena Eatom, a 55-year-old clerk with Great Country Bank. "But I have decided I am not going to vote for him. Senator Dodd has been in office for about 18 years. That's enough. It's time to get the slate cleaned."
That was one message that Mr. Johnson delivered to the business leaders at the Marriott Hotel in Stamford during a luncheon sponsored by the Southwestern Area Commerce and Industry Association.
More than 100 people attended the luncheon, which featured Carla A. Hills, the United States trade representative, as a speaker. Mr. Johnson, a businessman who has never held public office, talked about the need to pass a balanced-budget amendment, reduce the Federal debt and set term limits for Congress.
He also stressed his business background and the need to have the state expand its industry base through foreign trade.
"We know what's happening in our country right now," he said. "Children are doing what we have told them to do. They went to school. They worked hard. They went to good colleges. Then they got out, and they had to go back and live with their mothers and fathers because there aren't jobs out there for them."

HEADLINE: An Embattled Downey Defends His Record

BYLINE: By JOSH BARBANEL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: AMITYVILLE, L.I., Oct. 30

 BODY:
After 18 years of steadily increasing seniority and influence in Washington, Representative Thomas J. Downey's quest for a 10th term in Congress came down to this: an open invitation to voters to drop by his two-family house here, simply to prove he actually lives a few hundred miles north of the Beltway.
"I live on Long Island, I have lived here all my life," Mr. Downey said in a televised debate the other night, as his Republican opponent denounced him as a corrupt Washington big shot. "If you have any doubt, come to 155 Cedar Street, where I have lived for 13 years." 
 Once one of the youngest Congressmen ever to take office, a grass-roots outsider who routed an entrenched Republican with the help of the Watergate scandal, Mr. Downey, at the age of 43, finds himself transformed by circumstance and his opponent into a quintessential incumbent facing his toughest re-election fight in at least a decade.
 
Straws in the Wind
 At a time the public is yearning for change, when for some the word Congress is synonymous with scandal, Mr. Downey's campaign as a Democrat in an overwhelmingly Republican district is being watched by both parties as a sign of the times and the lingering effects of the House bank scandal.
"This race clearly shows the dichotomy between 'Re-elect our Congressman who is a powerful insider who can get things done,' and 'Get a newcomer who can shake things up,' " said Steven Sutton, a field representative for the National Republican Congressional Committee, who has been working feverishly in the district to defeat Mr. Downey, spending $50,000 on the effort.
Mr. Downey's Republican opponent, Rick Lazio, a 34-year-old two-term Suffolk County legislator and a member of a prominent Republican family, says his supporters see him as the same kind of fresh face with the same message of reform that Mr. Downey took to Congress years ago. They see Mr. Downey as someone "who has been corrupted."
When Mr. Downey talks about his seniority on the House Ways and Means Committee, Mr. Lazio prefers to discuss Mr. Downey's Congressional junket to Barbados a few years ago, including a jet-ski excursion captured by hidden cameras on ABC's "Prime Time Live." Mr. Downey says simply that the trip was a mistake.
If Mr. Downey brings up his central role in securing extensions in unemployment insurance benefits, his challenger talks about overdrafts on 151 checks totaling $83,000 at the House bank at a time when Mr. Downey's wife held a patronage job as an auditor in the House office that ran the House bank.
"While Tom Downey was writing bad checks, his wife was serving as the only auditor of the House bank," one ad reads. Mr. Downey said that the auditor title was only a job title, that his wife's work with the Sergeant-at-Arms office had nothing to do with the bank.
If Mr. Downey talks about his efforts to find money to convert Long Island's military industry to peaceful purposes, Mr. Lazio contends that Mr. Downey has all but abandoned Long Island in favor of Washington, where his wife works and his children go to school.
Asked about the considerable political baggage he brought to the campaign this year, from the House bank to his trip to Barbados, Mr. Downey smiled wanly and said, "Well, I have been there a long time."
For Mr. Downey, who was once so young and green in Washington that he was mistaken by colleagues for a Congressional page and had to show proof to order a drink at a restaurant near the Capitol, this year's election has been a watershed.
For the first time, he said, he has gone on the offensive against an opponent with a negative campaign on cable television and he has been working the district harder than he has in many years, holding 300 meetings in the district during the last term, and, spending every other day in the district this year.
Mr. Downey expects to spend more than $1 million on his re-election, about four times what his opponent says he will be able to muster by next Tuesday.
In his television campaign, he criticizes Mr. Lazio for providing a crucial Republican vote for a fiscal bailout plan for Suffolk County at a time it was running out of cash last year. The plan called for a $35.5 million tax increase.
"One day alone Rick Lazio voted to raise Long Island property taxes by $35 million," Mr. Downey's ads say. "Just imagine what he could do if we gave him two years as a Congressman."
But the plan, which included selling notes backed by a $35.5 million county property-tax increase, was drafted by Patrick G. Halpin, a Democrat who was then County Executive and who is one of Mr. Downey's close friends. Mr. Lazio said that while he voted for the plan, he eventually voted against the tax increase when it came before the Legislature.
Mr. Lazio, considered a moderate Republican, grew up in the same town, West Islip, graduated from the same high school, West Islip High School, and the same law school, at American University, as did Mr. Downey.
A former press spokesman and assistant district attorney in Suffolk County, Mr. Lazio sees himself as more low key than Mr. Downey, despite a campaign in which he has been relentlessly attacking him.
"I don't want to be a big-time Washington big shot," he said in the televised debate. "I just want to be a local guy who is going to fight for you and fight for local concerns and bring jobs back to the district."
After misgivings earlier this year, Mr. Downey said he is now confident that his message -- that he can make a difference in the lives of the people in his struggling middle-class district -- is getting across to voters, and that his past constituent services have been remembered.
"We've done our homework," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Representative Thomas J. Downey speaking yesterday at a high school in Oakdale, L.I. (Michael Shavel for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Bonds Off After Home-Sales Report

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Treasury bond prices fell yesterday after the Government revised its figures for new-home sales in August to show a gain and reported a drop in September home sales that was smaller than expected.
The price of the Treasury's main 30-year bond fell 17/32 , or $5.31 for $1,000 in face amount. Its yield, which rises when prices fall, was 7.62 percent, up from 7.57 percent late Thursday. 
 Many traders were concerned by the revised report on August new-home sales, which showed this area of the economy was not as weak as previously thought. The Government, which originally said August home sales fell 6.1 percent, now says they advanced 1.6 percent, to 623,000. September home sales dropped 1 percent, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 617,000.
 
Better Than Expected
 Still, the September figures were better than economists had expected, said Ray Stone, managing director of Stone & McCarthy Research Associates Inc. in Princeton, N.J.
In addition, many investors are reluctant to buy bonds now in advance of the Presidential election on Tuesday. The markets sold off earlier this month amid speculation that Gov. Bill Clinton might defeat President Bush and impose spending programs that could revive inflation.
Finally, investors are concerned about next week's announcement of how much in new bonds the Treasury will sell at its quarterly auction, said Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at the Norwest Corporation in Minneapolis.
Trading was light before the weekend. In the secondary market for Treasury securities, short-term issues fell by 3/32 to 15/32 point, and intermediate maturities fell by 18/32 to 25/32 , Telerate Inc. reported.
The movement of a point is equivalent to a change of $10 in the price of a bond with a $1,000 face value.
The Lehman Brothers Daily Treasury Bond Index, which measures price movements on all outstanding Treasury issues with maturities of a year or longer, fell 5.41 points, to 1,252.24.
 
Bill Yields Rise
 Yields on three-month Treasury bills rose to 3.01 percent, as the discount rose 2 basis points, to 2.96 percent. Yields on six-month bills rose to 3.28 percent, as the discount rose 2 basis points, to 3.19 percent. Yields on one-year bills rose to 3.52 percent, as the discount rose 6 basis points, to 3.39 percent. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
The Federal funds rate, the interest on overnight loans between banks, was quoted at 31/16 percent, unchanged from late Thursday.
In the tax-exempt market, the Bond Buyer index of 40 actively traded municipal bonds closed at 93 3/4, up 6/32 . The average yield to maturity fell to 6.68 percent, from 6.70 percent late Thursday.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Backer Fills Top Position At U.S. Unit

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Carl Spielvogel, chairman and chief executive of Backer Spielvogel Bates Worldwide, announced yesterday that Kenneth R. Rogers had been named chairman of the Americas region and chairman and chief executive of Backer Spielvogel Bates Inc., the advertising agency's American subsidiary.
Mr. Rogers, who is 46 years old, has been president and chief operating officer of Backer Spielvogel Bates since May 1991. No successor to Mr. Rogers was named at these positions. 
 By assuming the chairmanship of the Americas, which includes Canada and Latin America as well as the United States, Mr. Rogers joins the ranks of Michael Bungey, 55, chairman of the European region, and Alex Hammill, 53, chairman of Backer Spielvogel's Asia/Pacific region.
 
Not a Horse Race
 Mr. Spielvogel, who is 62, insisted that Mr. Rogers's appointment did not set up what could be a three-way struggle for his job.
"There is no horse race," he said. "Ken is not a candidate for my succession. He first has to stabilize the U.S. company. As of today, there are three regional executives, and he is the most recent."
Mr. Bungey has been in charge of European operations for the last five years. Mr. Hammill has been in his post since September 1991.
Backer Spielvogel has 167 offices in 50 countries and is based in New York. Like the rest of Madison Avenue, it has been hit hard by the recession.
"In the last three years, media budgets have leveled off, after rising by 12 percent a year" during much of the 1980's, Mr. Spielvogel said. "It is only now that I can tell clients that they can't save their way to prosperity, that they have to put some money into investment. That message is starting to get across."
 
$1.5 Billion in Billings
 The agency that Mr. Rogers now heads generates about $1.25 billion of the $5 billion in annual worldwide billings at Backer Spielvogel.
Mr. Spielvogel said that to assist Mr. Rogers in some of his day-to-day responsibilities, Frank Assumma and Jerrold H. Gottlieb had been named managing directors.
In his new post, Mr. Assumma, 40, will be primarily responsible for the firm's Miller Brewing Company business. Mr. Gottlieb, 46, will oversee multinational accounts, with responsibility for Mars Inc., the candy company, and the Philips Consumer Electronics Company.
Mr. Rogers is a graduate of Hofstra University and Columbia University, where he received an M.B.A. He joined Backer Spielvogel in 1987 after a stint at the J. Walter Thompson Company, where he was an executive vice president.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Closes Mostly Higher Despite Weak U.S. Data

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar settled mostly higher in quiet trading on world currency markets yesterday, advancing first in European trading and continuing its rise when activity shifted to domestic markets.
Analysts said the dollar showed surprising resilience in the face of two negative economic reports. The Government said sales of new homes fell 1.0 percent in September, the first drop in five months, despite the lowest mortgage rates in nearly two decades. 
 Later in the day, the Chicago Purchasing Management Association reported its October index slid sharply.
"As the election draws closer, market uncertainty increases, leaving the dollar in a tight trading range," said Marc Chandler, an analyst with the advisory firm IDEA.
After the election, however, "weak economic fundamentals should reassert themselves," increasing speculation that the Federal Reserve might have to lower interest rates again to stimulate growth, Mr. Chandler said.
Meanwhile, the prospect of lower interest rates in Europe prevents the dollar from falling, analysts said.
In Tokyo, the dollar closed at 123.35 yen, up 0.33 yen from Thursday's close. Later in London, the dollar was quoted at 123.25 yen. In New York, the dollar settled at 123.40 yen, up from 123.28 on Thursday.
In London, the British pound fell to $1.5625 from $1.5735 late Thursday. In New York, a pound cost $1.5565, down from $1.5730 on Thursday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Thursday's rates included: 1.5420 German marks, up from 1.5375; 1.3735 Swiss francs, unchanged; 5.2345 French francs, up from 5.2140; 1,322.00 Italian lire, up from 1,313.75, and 1.2385 Canadian dollars, down from 1.2387.
Other late dollar rates in Europe, compared with late Thursday's rates included: 1.5435 German marks, up from 1.5390; 1.3770 Swiss francs, up from 1.3735; 5.2340 French francs, up from 5.2210; 1.7364 Dutch guilders, up from 1.7325; 1,318.25 Italian lire, up from 1,316.00, and 1.2397 Canadian dollars, down from 1.2413.

HEADLINE: It Pays to Stick to Basics in Credit Cards

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT

 BODY:
With competition rising in the credit card business and more enticements being offered almost every week, consumers should weigh carefully whether the cards they now have are serving their needs at the lowest cost. A switch might save them money.
But it is no easy task tracking all the wrinkles of some cards. The GE Capital Corporation's Mastercard, introduced in September, offers discount coupons and rebates from 24 companies, including Kmart, Macy's and Northwest Airlines. To profit from these deals, consumers must use them to offset the higher costs of the card itself -- a $25 annual fee and an interest rate set at 12.4 percentage points above the prime rate, for a current total of 18.4 percent. 
 For some, the card may offer rewards much like a discount shoppers club. But for consumers who lack the time to organize sheaves of coupons, experts suggest sticking with the basics. Choose a card, they say, for the cost of its core services, not for fringe benefits.
"Pay yourself by saving money right away rather than later," said Robert B. McKinley, president of RAM Research in Frederick, Md., which publishes Cardtrak, a monthly listing of well priced credit cards.
 
Benefits, With Restrictions
 Some consumers may consider fringe benefits a good value, despite the extra cost. With the American Express card and many others, holders earn a free travel mile for every dollar they charge. Typically, these cards carry high annual fees and interest rates, but some consumers fly so often that they more than recoup the upfront costs. Many issuers of these cards, however, have capped the number of miles they will redeem to 10,000 a month or 50,000 a year.
The cost of providing fringe benefits has prompted some card issuers to add many restrictions. "When you get down to the fine print, they tend to lose their glitter," Mr. McKinley said.
The fledgling General Motors Mastercard, introduced last month, has relatively little fine print. It offers what many consider to be an attractive package, including no annual fee, a relatively low interest rate of prime plus 10.4 percent, and 5 percent rebates on spending that can be used to buy a new G. M. car. With additional rebates available from companies like the Marriott hotel chain, a cardholder could save more than $3,500 on a new car after seven years.
But even G. M.'s package bears a closer look. A $3,500 rebate will be worth much less in seven years than it is today. And the card uses a potentially expensive billing system known as the two-cycle method, also used by the Sears Discover card. It works this way. A $500 purchase made Jan. 1 does not have to be paid off until late February under typical grace periods. If only a minimum payment is made, most cards calculate interest for the March billing period on the unpaid charges from the February bill. Under the two-cycle method, a card issuer will go back two cycles and calculate the interest from the time the purchase was made.
This extra charge will be levied only once for people who carry balances from month to month. But for those who constantly switch from paying off to carrying a balance, this two-cycle method can be costly.
 
Hurting Credit Rating
 The G. M. card, like many others, requires no annual fee, giving consumers little reason not to add it to their collections of plastic. That feature drew many subscribers when the AT&T Universal card was introduced. But there is an indirect cost. The number of cards owned by consumers is listed on their credit reports, and those who have too many are perceived to be greater risks.
To help narrow the choices, consumers should first take stock of their spending habits. In broadest terms, there are two types of credit card users. More than two-thirds are "revolvers" who carry balances from month to month. Those who pay off their balances each month are known as convenience users. And many people move between these two camps over the course of a year.
For those who faithfully pay off their balances every month, interest rates are irrelevant. They should use a card that does not charge an annual fee and that offers a 25-day grace period before payment is required, as most cards do. With carefully timed purchases, these consumers can get about 50 days of free credit.
For revolvers, the interest rate is most important. And while research has shown that consumers are not likely to switch to save a percentage point or two, they will switch for bigger breaks. Since many people are paying close to 20 percent interest, a little effort could effectively cut interest payments by about a third.
 
Ask for a Better Deal
 The simplest move for consumers is to call the bank that issues their cards. Banks are often willing to dole out breaks on rates and fees rather than lose better customers.
Be wary of cards that offer interest rates near or below 10 percent. The approval rates for such cards are low, applicants must fill out long forms and, as a rule, they carry low credit limits and high fees.
Mr. McKinley recommends searching for cards that charge interest of 12.5 to 15.5 percent and offer an "800" number for services. Consumers with good credit can be approved for a realistic credit line and a low or nonexistent annual fee with such cards.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "Finding the Best Deals" shows cards available nationally that offer reasonable credit lines and approval rates as well as bargain pricing. (Source: RAM Research's CardTrak)
