HEADLINE: TAXES;
Do Some Planning Now To Avoid 1993 Surprises

BYLINE: By Jan M. Rosen

 BODY:
MORE taxpayers than ever before -- middle-class salaried people, as well as those who live on investment income -- need to do year-end tax planning in the next few weeks to avoid any rude surprises in April. Some will even find a few well-spent hours can pay dividends in lower '92 tax bills.
One reason for a new urgency this year is the reduction in withholding for income taxes that President Bush ordered last winter. It meant fatter paychecks, up to $345 for the year, but it did not reduce the taxes owed. As a result, many taxpayers may discover the refunds they have come to expect are disappointingly small, or, worse, that they owe money. 
 Conversely, with the plunge in interest rates over the year, many incomes have been cut deeply. Those taxpayers may want to reduce estimated tax payments or withholding on other income. The rate drop has also created a wave of mortgage refinancings, which can have important tax consequences.
David A. Berenson, national director of tax policy for Ernst & Young in Washington, said, "On April 15, a lot of people are going to find themselves caught short."
New statistics from the Tax Foundation show why they'll be caught short. Income for the median two-earner family this year is projected to be $53,984, but Federal income taxes, Social Security, indirect taxes and state and local taxes will take $21,445 for an effective tax rate of 39.7 percent, leaving the family with $32,539. On a constant 1992 dollar basis, the independent research organization says, that is $214 less than a year ago.
The people in the toughest situation, Mr. Berenson said, will be those who have unwittingly walked into the alternative minimum tax, which increasingly is snaring middle-class people in high-tax states like New York and California as well as upper-income people whose sheltered income it is meant to tax.
The goal of the alternative minimum tax is to snare people of means who have whittled away their tax liabilities under the regular tax rules. Many standard deductions for state and local taxes and accelerated depreciation are disallowed, and the income base is broadened. Taxpayers with complex finances or many deductions are supposed to calculate their taxes under both sets of rules and pay whichever tax is higher.
With a top Federal rate of 31 percent under regular rules and 24 percent under A.M.T. rules, any sizable deductions that would not be allowed for A.M.T. purposes can spring the trap. So can interest on a mortgage refinancing, unless the loan is used for home improvement.
That is why it is particularly important for people who refinanced to do calculations before the year ends, Mr. Berenson said. Depending on the results, some will want to pay the balance of any state and local taxes owed this year so they can take the deduction; others will want to postpone the payments until January, if they would lose the deduction in 1992.
Julian Block, a Larchmont, N.Y., tax lawyer and author of "Julian Block's Year-Round Tax Strategies for the $40,000-Plus Household," said many people in this economic downturn had income on which no money or too little had been withheld, like unemployment compensation, severance pay and money withdrawn prematurely from I.R.A.'s.
"They should think about this before the end of the year and pay estimated taxes on it," Mr. Block said, lest they subject themselves to penalties and interest, as well as taxes.

HEADLINE: Q & A

BYLINE: By Leonard Sloane

 BODY:

Taxing the Pensions Of Former Residents
Q I have heard that New York State is assessing tax on withdrawals from appreciated 401(k) funds even when a resident moves to another state. Is this true, and if it is, how is the tax computed?
 
 RICHARD R. STEBBINS JR.
 Manhattan
 
A New York has long had a law authorizing taxes on retirement benefits, including 401(k) plans, of those who worked in the state and later moved. Periodic payments or funds rolled over into individual retirement accounts are not taxed, but tax must be paid on money received in a lump sum. 
 "If we didn't tax lump-sum distributions, it would be very easy to design compensation arrangements so that what would be salary is paid out after retirement," said James W. Wetzler, Commissioner of the New York Department of Taxation and Finance."
Nonresidents must first compute their tax as if they were residents, Commissioner Wetzler said. Then they use a formula to determine the amount due by multiplying the resident tax by a fraction in which the numerator is income from New York (including the retirement benefits) and the denominator is total income.
 
Appraising Property For Insurance Coverage
Q We had the contents of our apartment appraised two years ago to make sure our insurance covered current values. Is it time for another appraisal or can we wait now that inflation has eased?
 
 MARGOT SPELMAN
 Manhattan
 
A With today's low inflation rate, it does not make sense to get a more recent appraisal, said Sean F. Mooney, senior vice president and economist of the Insurance Information Institute. The value of personal property has probably not changed very much in the last two years.
"But if you have insured a painting or other valuable art object on a value basis," he added, "then you may be paying too much because many values have gone down. So you could save money by getting a new appraisal."
 
When Gifts to Charities Lead to Alternate Tax
When people give appreciated stock as a charitable contribution, at what level of income do they become liable for the alternative minimum tax?
 
 WILLIAM V. LEWIT
 Scardsdale, N.Y.
 
A The threshold for the alternate minimum tax is not the level of a taxpayer's income but the amount of the untaxed gain on appreciated stock and other so-called preference items. If the total exceeds $40,000, the taxpayer may be subject to the alternative minimum tax, said Edward Mendlowitz, a partner of Mendlowitz Weitsen, an accounting firm. The tax is calculated on Form 6251 and applies only if it is greater than the regular tax.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Jays' Cone and Carter Declare Their Freedom

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS

 BODY:
David Cone and Joe Carter, two members of the World Series champion Toronto Blue Jays, put themselves into position yesterday to become ultra-wealthy. Dion James just made sure he had a job.
Cone and Carter were among 27 players who declared themselves free agents. Beginning Nov. 9, they will be free to sign with anyone for whatever anyone wants to pay them. In the cases of Cone and Carter, that will be a lot of money because Cone is one of the top three starting pitchers available and Carter is one of the top half-dozen hitters. 
 James, on the other hand, is a reserve outfielder who first reached the majors in 1983, but did not distinguish himself in 67 games for the Yankees this year. He batted .262, hit 3 home runs and drove in 17 runs. He turns 30 years old in 10 days, but the Yankees thought enough of his value to keep him from joining the 109 free agents.
 
Bonuses Abound
 They signed him to a one-year contract, giving him a $25,000 signing bonus and a $325,000 salary plus a possible $150,000 in bonuses. James can earn $35,000 if he is on the Yankees' roster for 30 days and $25,000 each if he is on 60 days and 90 days. He also has five levels of bonuses based on games played or plate appearances. He can earn a maximum of $65,000 if he plays 90 games or has 275 plate appearances.
James, who won a job with the Yankees last spring as a nonroster player, earned $387,500 this year, including bonuses.
The Yankees have indicated a willingness to spend big for Cone. They have coveted the former Mets right-hander so publicly that they have verged on tampering with him. Cone has said he would seriously consider returning to New York, but hasn't said that's his No. 1 preference.
The Blue Jays, who achieved their objective of winning the pennant and World Series after obtaining Cone in a trade with the Mets on Aug. 27, have told Cone they would like to have him back, though they have not made him an offer. Until Nov. 9, they are the only team that can sign him. The 27 other teams can only express their interest in him and elicit his interest in them; they cannot negotiate with him or even mention money.
Candy Maldonado joined Cone and Carter in filing yesterday, raising to 10 the number of Blue Jays who have declared themselves free. Dave Winfield and Manny Lee are eligible to declare free agency, so the Blue Jays could have an even dozen free agents.
The Oakland Athletics have a baker's dozen. Mike Moore, who was not offered arbitration, and Rick Honeycutt, who didn't have to be, raised the Athletics' roll call of free agents to 13. Rich (Goose) Goosage and Carney Lansford, who has said he is retiring, remain eligible.
The National League champion Atlanta Braves had two players file, Jeff Reardon and Mike Bielecki, both pitchers. The Mets had four players -- Willie Randolph, Daryl Boston, Barry Jones and Lee Guetterman. Paul Molitor of Milwaukee also added his name to the list.
The Cleveland Indians signed relief pitcher Ted Power to a one-year contract, giving him a $25,000 signing bonus and a $425,000 salary, one-quarter of it guaranteed.
Indians reliever Steve Olin learned yesterday that he can expect a higher salary for 1993 than he might otherwise have had. Olin is one of 15 players who will be eligible for salary arbitration as the result of the 1990 collective bargaining agreement.
Players generally need three years of major league service to be eligible, but the players and owners settled their lockout dispute by agreeing to make eligible 17 percent of the players who have between two and three years of major league service and who spent at least half of this past season in the majors.
Olin, only two days short of three years, heads the group of 15 taken from the 89 qualifying players. He had 29 saves this year.
Another player who will benefit economically is Philadelphia pitcher Curt Schilling who had a 14-11 record and a 2.35 earned run average.
Just missing this group was Albert Belle, who hit 34 home runs and drove in 112 runs for the Indians.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Political Pulse -- Pennsylvania;
Pennsylvania Poses the Big One: Can Bush Do It?

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PITTSBURGH, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Is President Bush really catching up? On the final weekend of the 1992 Presidential campaign, that is the question of the hour in Pennsylvania, as it is everywhere. But here at least, there are no definitive answers yet to be discerned.
Ask some experts, and they say Mr. Bush has a real shot at Pennsylvania's 23 electoral votes, a valuable pile that has made the Keystone State one of the great dueling grounds of American politics for generations. Ask others, equally well plugged-in, and they say Bill Clinton locked things up here weeks ago. 
 "I don't think Bush can possibly make it," said Rick Robb, a law partner of Drew Lewis, the former Transportation Secretary, and like Mr. Lewis, a leading Republican politician in the state.
"It comes down to two questions: Do you think George Bush is as strong a candidate in Pennsylvania as he was four years ago? Of course not. Do you think Bill Clinton is a better Democratic candidate than Michael Dukakis was? Obviously, and George Bush carried this state four years ago by only about 100,000 votes."
 
Different Views of Perot Factor
 Prof. G. Terry Madonna of Millersville State University, a political scientist who specializes in Pennsylvania elections, said that it was "hard to imagine any scenario, however outlandish, that would enable the President to win here." He added: "There's nothing very profound about this election. There's a massive pro-change vote in Pennsylvania, waiting to be cast on Tuesday, and everything indicates Clinton, not Ross Perot, will get most of it."
Yet Elsie Hillman of Pittsburgh, a longtime Republican National Committeewoman from Pennsylvania, pooh-poohed the pessimists in her party with the comment, "I think we're going to surprise everyone again and carry this state." She sees Mr. Perot, who made one of his few campaign speeches in Pittsburgh last weekend, splitting the anti-Bush vote and pulling as much as 20 percent of the total.
"In 1988, they wrote us off," she said. "President Reagan never came into the western part of the state to campaign for Bush, and we had no advertising to speak of. It was dead again this year until the last debate. Until then, people didn't think the President had any fire in his tummy, but now they sense he's caught fire."
Gov. Robert P. Casey, a Democrat, said that Mr. Clinton still "has to be favored in Pennsylvania, overwhelmingly." But he added that "something is definitely happening out there, and he's losing ground, because Bush has succeded in framing the end-game issue, not as jobs but as trust and taxes."
A major problem for Mr. Clinton, the Governor said, was the widespread suggestion earlier this month that a Clinton landslide was imminent. Talk like that, he said, "makes it hard to get your traction --
 "Talking about landslides and mandates is the political equivalent of talking to the pitcher about his no-hitter in the bottom of the ninth," Mr. Casey said. "You have to run hard until the last possible minute."
 
Poll Shows Clinton Advantage
 The most recent state poll, completed last Sunday, just before some national polls began recording a surge by President Bush, showed Mr. Clinton with a 10-point lead. The survey, taken for the Pittburgh Post-Gazette and television station WTAE, gave the Arkansas Governor 41 percent, Mr. Bush 31 percent and Mr. Perot 21 percent, with 7 percent undecided, based on interviews with 775 registered voters. The margin of sampling error was listed as plus or minus 4 percentage points.
But a smaller sample of 578 "most likely voters"showed Mr. Clinton with only a 7 point lead, 42 per cent to 35. In that case, the margin of sampling error was a bit bigger, 4.2 percent. In both polls, the Democrat's lead had been cut in half since mid-October.
Senator Arlen Specter, a Republican, appears to be winning his battle for re-election against Lynn T. Yeakel, a Democrat who has denounced him for the prosecutorial tone he adopted while cross-examining Prof. Anita F. Hill during the Senate hearings on Judge Clarence Thomas's nomination to the Supreme Court.
 
Senator's Advice for Bush
 But Mr. Specter said he doubted the accuracy of the polls in the Presidential race, because it had become "politically incorrect" to tell poll takers that one was supporting Mr. Bush for re-election. He criticized the President for what he termed a perilously late-starting campaign.
"A lot of us have been trying our very best for a long time," he said, "to get the President to do something about economic recovery and health care, the two biggest issues. But he had his own ideas and his own timetable."
The Senator said Mr. Bush would hugely benefit in Pennsylvania if he signed the economic recovery bill now on his desk, which includes provisions for enterprise zones and other elements favored by the White House. It also includes tax increases and tax cuts, supposedly equal in size, that may well attract a Presidential veto.
In a sense, the Bush-Clinton race here is a rerun. In 1991, Harris Wofford, a Democrat with a long record of government service but little name recognition, defeated former Gov. Dick Thornburgh, a heavily favored Republican. Mr. Thornburgh had served Mr. Bush as Attorney General and was closely identified with the Administration.
Then as now, health care and jobs were the big issues. Then as now, the chief Democratic strategist was James Carville, a wisecracking Louisiana populist.
Senator Wofford, who carried the state by 55 percent to 45, said: "I see a victory for Clinton of about the same magnitude as mine," said Senator Wofford, who carried the state by 55 percent to 45. "Perot has deepened the indictment of Bush, labor is solid, and Clinton is doing a good job in pulling back the Catholic Democrats in the Philadelphia suburbs and in other parts of the state."
Neil Oxman, a Democratic campaign consultant in Philadelphis, said, "There's nothing happened to the people of Pennsylvania in the last year or so "to make anyone who voted for Wofford vote for Bush this time."
 
Economy Dominates Concerns
 Although a few local politicians suggested that the economy has lost its acuity as an issue in this state, which has suffered less than some in the current recession, most said it continued to dominate voters' concerns. In western Pennsylvania particularly, where industrial decline has given counties like Fayette and Westmoreland unemployment rates in double digits, the President is blamed.
"No doubt character questions are starting to hurt Clinton," Professor Madonna said, "but a lot of the votes leaving him are going to Perot, because the people in the depressed areas are unwilling to risk four more years like the last four by voting for Mr. Bush."
Still, there are complicating factors for Mr. Clinton, some of them of his own making.
On Wednesday night, for example, the Governor said on the CNN program "Larry King Live" that he had "real problems" with the proposed acquisition by British Airways of a major interest in USAir. He commented: "We get no access to the British market if we do it. It's just an admission that we've allowed American companies to get in terrible financial trouble."
 
Abortion Issue a Factor
 USAir has its main hub in Pittsburgh, with more than 10,000 employees in the area, and the company has enlisted many of them in its campaign to convince Washington that it needs the deal to survive.
Antagonism over the abortion issue between Mr. Casey and his party's national ticket has also raised fears among some Democrats.
Governor Casey takes a middle position on abortion, and he signed into law a measure, upheld this year by the Supreme Court, authorizing limited restrictions. He hoped to state his case at the Democratic National Convention in New York but was refused permission to do so. Ever since, Mr. Wofford said, "he's had his Irish up, and he has done nothing to help Clinton and Gore."
Last Tuesday, Mr. Casey skipped a dinner in Scranton, his home town, at which Hillary Clinton spoke. In a statement issued at the time, he said he was staying away as a matter of principle. "The national party's embrace of abortion on demand demands too much," he said, echoing John F. Kennedy's dictum that "Sometimes, party loyalty demands too much."
Mr. Wofford and Professor Madonna said the abortion issue was only a minor factor in the Presidential contest. But Mr. Casey insisted that "on balance, it helps Bush in this state, because it's one of a bundle of issues and values that turn attention away from hard times and onto character, forcing people to look again at Bill."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 7; Column 4; National Desk

LENGTH: 1171 words

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Media;
What Is Scarier Than Halloween? Tune In to Candidates' Ads and See

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
The Presidential candidates have embarked on the electronic-age version of a final whistlestop tour. But the view from America's living rooms is not a pretty one.
As they bombard the airwaves in the closing 100 hours with 30-second, 60-second, two-minute, half-hour and hourlong commercials that are running practically back to back, the campaigns are leaving unmistakably depressing images they hope the voters will carry to the polls. 
 The overall tone is bleak, particularly in the Republican advertisements. President Bush is treating viewers to a new spot that warns, through creepy music and dreary scenes of buzzards and barren deserts, that the country under Gov. Bill Clinton would be transformed into a virtual stage set for horror films.
Tune in to the latest Clinton advertisement and there are blurry images of Mr. Bush looking weirdly irrational four years ago as he made optimistic pronouncements about the economy.
 
Perot Joins In
 Even Ross Perot broke from his practice of not using advertisements to directly bash his opponents. He bought a half-hour on the networks Sunday night to broadcast a new commercial titled "Chicken feathers, Voo-doo Economics and the American Dream." The message here is not hard to decipher: Mr. Perot has demeaned Mr. Clinton by saying the principle industry in Arkansas is chicken plucking, and the Texas billionaire has regularly ridiculed the President for espousing "voodoo economics."
"What we're seeing is a frantic campaign by candidates to hold onto the people they've got and to make slight inroads," said Timothy E. Cook, chairman of the political science department at Williams College in Williamstown, Mass. "People are going to get into the polling booths and hold their noses, and under those circumstances you can't afford to let up for a minute."
In using these final days to tear into Mr. Clinton's record as Governor of Arkansas, the Bush campaign seems to have settled on one of its most effective attacks after earlier, less successful efforts.
If the polls are any gauge, voters were not moved by the Bush campaign's attempt to taint Mr. Clinton with the time-worn "tax and spend" attacks. And the advertisements that hit him on the "trust" issue seemed to be neutralized by Mr. Clinton's commercials that used old film clips of Mr. Bush to remind voters of his President's "read my lips" pledge.
 
Effectiveness of Ridicule
 Clinton aides said an early Bush commercial that tried humor and hillbilly music to ridicule Mr. Clinton's record in Arkansas was one of the Republicans' more effective advertisements, and they expressed surprise that the campaign did not keep it on the air longer.
Dean Alger, a political science professor at Moorhead State University in Minnesota, who assembled undecided voters to view several advertisements, said, "Our focus group had a pretty striking reaction to the hillbilly ad in terms of memorability."
Although she derided the Bush campaign for what she called a relentlessly negative advertising campaign, Mandy Grunwald, a Clinton media adviser, conceded that while they were hardly devastating, the attacks seemed to be hurting the Democrats in the closing days. "The combination of advertising and free media has been more sharpened," she said, adding that it has "raised our negatives."
Sig Rogich, who supervises advertising for the Bush campaign, said the Republicans would press on with their stinging denunciations of Mr. Clinton. "We're not finished," he said. "We know it's a horse race and we're not about to let up."
In seeking to combat the negative impressions left about them by their opponents, and to say some nice things about their candidates, the campaigns are each spending well over a million dollars on network advertising this weekend. In the last three days alone, the Clinton campaign has bought $2 million more in broadcast time on the networks alone; the Perot campaign, $4.2 million, and the Bush campaign, $1 million.
The campaigns have also increased their attacks even more in battleground states, where they have bought huge amounts of television time, called "saturation buys." The Bush campaign has made these purchases in 30 markets, and the Clinton campaign has done so in an estimated 17 markets.
Mindful that there is more to winning votes than frightening the electorate, the campaigns have leavened their televised images of buzzards and storm clouds with some more uplifting messages.
The Bush campaign is running two-minute commercials that show the President talking to a rapt audience about the importance of a steady commander in chief at times of crisis. It only indirectly takes a swipe at Mr. Clinton by raising the issue of trust.
In response, the Clinton side offers a 30-second advertisement it calls a "validation spot," seeking to reassure voters that Mr. Clinton has a steady hand. It features shot of a sober looking Governor sitting at his desk as names roll on the screen of experts who support his economic prescriptions.
Clinton aides said the half-hour spot they have bought to be broadcast on the networks Monday night will have an upbeat message and will include footage from the biographic film shown at the Democratic National Convention that depicted Mr. Clinton as a man of unstinting honor.
And a series of Perot advertisements seek to build the businessman's stature. In one commercial, the Texan looks into the camera and earnestly says, "We can be a country with its spirit restored."
As if these advertisement blitzes were not enough, the candidates are accepting practically every offer to appear on television programs. After months of carefully calculating what programs they would appear on, both Mr. Bush and Mr. Clinton are agreeing to interviews with journalists their advisers had once viewed as hostile, like Sam Donaldson of ABC News.
 
About-Face by Bush
 Mr. Bush, who only weeks ago defended his refusal to appear on MTV, the music television network, saying "I'm not much of a mod MTV man," went so far as to agree to be interviewed Saturday on the network.
"If free air time is going to be given to your opposition," said Mr. Rogich of the Bush campaign, "you certainly are entitled to match it. We want people to come away with the clear difference beween Bill Clinton and George Bush; people have to realize that there are real problems in Arkansas."
Frank Greer, Mr. Rogich's counterpart on the Clinton campaign, also speaks of crystallizing the choices. "We want the final days of this election to focus on the real choices," he said. "We want people to walk into that polling place saying we can't afford four more years."
Despite all the slashing attacks and scare tactics, some voters seemed to be swayed by the old-fashioned feel-good spots. After viewing a gushy advertisement that depicted Mr. Bush as a fighter, an undecided voter in Minnesota said: "I keep hearing the same things from George. However, the advertisement nearly had me standing with my hand over my heart."

HEADLINE: Here's to Kale, Sweetest of Leafy Greens

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Milking Our Memories" (editorial, Oct. 1) discusses the possibilities raised by a group of doctors that milk might not be the childhood elixir for growing up strong and healthy, and ends by proposing other sources for calcium, such as broccoli and kale, "a green known more for hardiness than taste." You apparently have a serious kale deficiency and tastebuds dulled by a thick coating of milk. You also need cooking lessons. 
 Kale, when young and fresh, is delicious and tender, with just a few minutes of steaming. It is perhaps the sweetest of the "healthy dark leafy greens," and can be relished in a myriad of seasoning profiles: Italian-style with garlic and olive oil, Oriental stir-fried, simmered in a zesty Indian lentil soup and as a classic accompaniment to Brazilian fejoida.
Let's hope that kale will not fall to a Presidential veto, as poor broccoli did, and that our new leader will choose a diet as healthy as his promised economy.
 
SUSAN ASANOVIC
Wilton, Conn., Oct. 2, 1992
The writer is a dietitian.

HEADLINE: House Aged Prisoners In Converted Hospitals

 BODY:
To the Editor:
You report Oct. 4 on the blocked efforts in the New York State Legislature to close the almost disused Gowanda Psychiatric Center. With staff retraining and relatively minor renovations, any one of such facilities could meet the needs of a rapidly aging inmate population at less cost than a correctional institution. 
 Prisoners who have spent a lifetime in jail often see prison as home and fear freedom more than continued imprisonment. Maximum security, therefore, is not generally important. Far more critical are medical care, geriatric dormitory services, such as special diets and wheelchair access, and protection from young and violent inmates. These added services frequently triple prison costs of caring for older prisoners.
Using empty psychiatric centers as geriatric prisons would increase employment in economically dependent communities, be more cost-effective than correctional facilities and provide a more human environment for jailed octogenarians no longer a threat to society.
 
JACOB REINGOLD
Executive V.P., Hebrew Home for the Aged at Riverdale
Bronx, Oct. 6, 1992
